G. G. McC.
A Big Horse to Ride —E. B. Dewing (Mac-
millan). Rose Carson, to whom danc-
ing had been a passion as well as a pro-
fession, tells the story of her life —the
Big Horse she had to ride. The early
part is slow, but the reader is led on in
expectation of events. After Rose is
married she falls in love, and gets into
a matrimonial tangle, which is at any
rate curious. Dewing brings out char-
acter very well, but the yarn is too big
tor a Horse which is not extraordinary.
The Bolted Door—George Gibbs (George
Robertson and Co.). The uncle’s will
said that if his niece and his wife’s
nephew shall marry, they can have his
millions; if not, the said millions go to
the Home for Lost Dogs. Now, She
was a very elegant and fashionable
young lady, whereas He was no class,
and almost unknown to Her. Neverthe-
less, the millions suggest a marriage in
name, wherefore the bolted door between
the sleeping apartments. There are
some nice people in the yarn. Of the
Yi, e . i lt is s , aid •' ‘.‘Her set was rotten.
All its men had histories, all its women
mysteries—a set, in short, in which it
twk unusual cleverness or unusual
virtue t° be virtuous.” The style is good
slick American.
Irish Plays
The Irish literary revival has proved to
be no temporary outburst. When the maga-
zines were busy about the Celtic renais-
sance 12 or 15 years ago, it was feared that
the whole Irish movement depended upon W.
Yeats. Then came the establishment of a
national theatre, the appearance of Lady
Gregory as a comic dramatist, and the dis-
covery of a first-rater in J. M. Synge, whose
early death was a big loss to literature at
large. New writers, however, sprang from
the ould sod, who have carried on the work;
and, as a result of the heroic efforts of the
pioneers, they found ready for them a
theatre and an audience—also a publisher.
There has been nothing finer in recent liter-
ary history than this late re-flowering of the
national spirit of Ireland. The poems of
leats had been appreciated by the elect
wherever English is read; other Irish writers
of less degree had made their mark; but the
clear-sighted patriots amongst them knew
that nothing in verse would appeal to the
mass of their countrymen unless it dwelt on
their ancient wrongs, or stirred their hatred
of the Sassenach. A literature of revolt or
protest could not be enduring, and its occa-
sion had departed. To get to the people,
and to represent the true character and sen-
timent of the nation, drama, the democrat
of literature, must be employed. So, at it
they went. Yeats led the way; wrote plays
himself, encouraged others to write, started
a magazine as organ of the movement, se-
cured actors and a. theatre, and a beginning
was made with his Countess Kathleen in
1899. They struggled through heaps of
difficulty until 1904, when a generous ad-
mirer gave the use of a theatre free of
charge. Since then the way has been easy
and great progress has been made, not only
in the quality and number of plays, but
m the art of stage production and acting.
Yeats, however, devoted so much to art
and his country, that he had to accept, the
other day, a pension of £l5O a year from the
British Government.
Not the least of his services was that of
stirring John Millington Synge into literary
activity. Synge’s work is of greater moment
than anything done in English drama for a
hundred years. He evolved a dramatic
style which built up a mbving tragedy or a
comedy of mischievous humor out of the
living speech of the people. He broke away
from dramatic conventions; and put a new
kind of life on to the stage; the contrarieties
of Irish character, the wilful exaggeration
and the essential poetry of the peasant’s
speech.
Maunsel and Co., of Dublin, who are pub-
lishing. nearly all the plays and poems of
the Irish group, have issued the complete
works of Synge, in four volumes, at 245. net.
Most of the plays have been printed separ-
ately _ (2s. 6d. each), and were mentioned
on this page some time ago. Deirdre of the
Sorrows has now been added to the edition of
single plays, and, though it has not the ad-
vantage of the author’s revision, it is inter-
esting as an attempt to tell an old story in
modern language.
The distressful Helen of old Ireland had
been the ward of Conchubor, King of Ulster,
who intended to marry her. She, however,
married Naisi, and, some years after, Con-
chubor sent Fergus to bring them both to
him. Lavarcham, Deirdre’s nurse, begs her
not to go; prophesying woe:—
Deirdre : “I’ve dread going or staying
Lavarcham. It’s lonesome this place,
having happiness like ours, till I’m ask-
ing each day will this day match yes-
terday, and will to-morrow take a good
place beside the same day in the year
that’s gone; and wondering all times is
it a game worth playing, living on till
you’re dead and old, and our joy is gone
for ever.”
Lavabcham : “If it’s that ails you, I tel
you there’s little hurt getting old
though young girls and poets do be
storming at the shapes of age. (Pas-
sionately.). There's little hurt getting
old, saving when you’re looking back
the way I’m looking this day, and see-
ing the young you’ve a love for breaking
up their hearts with folly.
I’m late so with my warnings, for Fer-
gus’d talk the moon over to take a new
path in the sky. You’ll not stop him
this day, and isn’t it a strange story
you were a plague and torment, since
you were that height, to those who
hang their lifetimes on your voice.
(Overcome with trouble, gathering her
cloalc about her.) Don’t think bad of
my crying. I’m not the like of many,
and I’d see a score of naked corpses
and not heed them at all, but I’m des-
troyed seeing yourself in your hour of
joy when the end is coming surely.
The student of drama would find it useful
to compare this version of Deirdre’s story
with that in conventional style by “A. E.”
(George Russell), also published by Maun-
sel and Co., at Is.
The younger playwrights have followed
Synge in the path of realism, and one of
them, Padraic Colum, is also a poet of
some accomplishment. The two latest arri-
vals are T. C. Murray, author of Birthright,
and St. John Ervine, of Mixed Marriage.
Both these folk-dramas grip the reader as
true and vivid representations of life; and
in the case of Birthright the story is one
that should move any audience.
Lady Gregory has turned three plays of
MoliSre into English with a touch of the
brogue for the Abbey Theatre; for they mix
a little imported drama of intellectual in-
terest with the native products. If you read
The Kiltartan Molidre (3s 6d.), you may be
surprised to find how modern the French-
man is, and how faithful to the spirit of the
original is this queer-looking version.
Price Warung
The late William Astley, who wrote as
“Price Waning,” had a more intimate know-
ledge of “the System” than any man of his
time. For nearly 20 years before he began
to write stories he had been reading and
absorbing all the available material relating
to the prison history of Australia. He had
a flair for the dramatic interest which some-
times lies hidden in bald records, and he had
a habit of order. Actual episodes from the
blood-stained history of lagdpm were
trimmed artistically, and set forth in a
clear, nervous style for The Bulletin, as
Tales of the Convict System. Published in
book form in 1892, they at once took a place
next to the -magnum opus of Marcus Clarke,
and still remain the best of Astley’s work.
The following passage from one of the
stories _ shows Astley striking the pathetic
note with a sure touch before introducing a
tragedy:—
The organ pealed out its opening volun-
tary. How the prisoners loved that organ!
There was no reason why they should not,
for, being the Itev. Mr. Taylor’s private
property, the System had nothing to do
with it. Of small volume, and a not very
copious variety of sound, the instrument
had a rare, sweetness of tone that awoke in
its convict- auditors an infinity of soul which
their gaol surroundings could not altogether
cramp. Touched by Mrs. Taylor’s sympa-
thetic fingers, its notes spoke to them of
another world than the one to which they
were moored, like Lucifer in Hell. It gave
bodily shape and substance to the color and
beauty which Nature spread over their prison
isle, and which, so debasing was the influ-
ence of their prison associations, they could
hardly believe was true—r-could scarcely re-
gard as aught but an entrancing mirage.
Smiled on daily by the softest and bluest of
skies, its witchery never stole into their
hearts till the organ proclaimed their kin-
ship with the eternal world, that other
sphere where spirits out of prison lived,
and moved, and had their being. When the
instrument sang, it filled sin-stained breasts
with unutterable aspiration. When it
sobbed, subtle ideas, embassies of “words
not quickened by the sun,” stirred brains
dulled by routine. When it sighed,
The silence that is in the starry sky,
The sleep that is among the lonely hills,
became audible to embruted ears. Its mas-
ter-chord called up to some men visions of
dear home faces and cherished English
scenes; and to others, as the melody faded
into the key of the sub-dominant.
The silent spectre—the gleam —
The shadow —and the peace supreme
of some great cathedral minster where they
hau worshipped— once! Once, but ah! so
long ago, so long ago!
Mrs. Taylor swept the keys on that Com-
mission Sunday with more than her usual
depth of passion. Perhaps some fore-reach-
ing filament of the impending horror touched
her, for she played as she had never played
before, and, as she ended, a peculiar sound
that was neither a sob nor a sigh, but like
unto both, arose from the grey and yellow
ranks, as an echo to the dying chord.
“It seemed to me,” she said simply to her
husband that night, “like an angel’s heart-
break.”
Three further collections of stories —Tales
of the Early Days (1894), Tales of the
Old Regime (1895) and Tales of the Isle
of Death (1898) did not exhaust his
gleanings from Norfolk Island and Port
Arthur records; but the public had had
enough. In Half-crown Bob and Tales
of the Riverine (1898) he drew upon
an acquaintance > with the Riverine
district made in early journalistic
days. He was anxious to write a
novel; but it seemed to be beyond him. A
long story was commenced in The Aus-
tralian Workman (which Astley edited for
a time), but never completed, and the pro-
mising beginning of a history of “Bush-
Outlawry in Australasia”
( Bulletin , August, ’92) was not continued.
As a journalist, Astley did a large amount
of valuable work for Federation, of which
he was an enthusiastic advocate. One of his
special articles' is remembered, an esti-
mate of Henry Parkes, printed in this paper
just after the old man’s death, and called
“Within an Ace of Greatness.”
New French Books
The Arabian Nights, <i in the form that we
know them, d|te from the Fifteenth Cen-
tury, but there were previous collections,
with a similar frame, as early as the tenth
century. One of these Prof. Gaudefroy-
Demombynes thinks he has discovered in the
work he translates as Les cent et une nuit
(E. Gmlmoto, Sfr.). It includes nineteen
tales, with others inset, notably a score tha
begin, “Doubtless you have heard of- the cun
ning ot womankind; here is another instanc
of it. A few tales, like the Ebony Horse
are common to both collections, and in sue
cases the editor gives the variants of th
later. The earlier series is noticeably mor
primitive, it is “Arabic, with very little ac
mixture of Persian influence”; the storie
are not yet literature like the Nights, bu
tales that are told. There are crude smoke
room stories of the period, awaiting thei
Armand Silvestre, folk tales, and tales o
marvellous adventures; but in each case th
telling is more direct ; the hero does not cal
a halt while he recites certain verses, bu
attacks the “Cleaver of Skulls,” or embrace
the “Miracle of Beauty” without many words
Equally with the Arabian Nights, there
an absence of morals, at least of the morals
that are ours.
Plenley preferred to read the Nights in
the elegant Eighteenth Century translation
of their first translator, Galland, who gave
the. stories without the pornographic diva-
gations of the 'original; the English transla
tion known as Forster’s is a rendering of
his version. A reprint of Galland’s first edi-
tion has just been published by Flammarion
Mille et une nuits, trad., par A. Galland,
4v015., Sfr. per vol. Those who want the
whole may have the French translation of
Dr. Mardrus, at a much lower price than
Burton (Sr-^Payne— Mille et une nuits, trad,
du Dr. Mardrus (Fasquelle), 16vols. at 7fr.
per vol. There is a dispute about the texts
he used, but his style is highly praised by
Andre Gide.
* * * *
Fraugois Villon, Oeuvres, ed. par Un
ancien archiviste (11. Champion, 2fr.), partly
replaces the excellent edition of Longnon,
now out of print. The editorial work in-
cludes the facts of the poet’s life, with
authorities and a bibliography; the only
objection is the glossary, which is too scanty
for the wants of “ung pauvre escollier,” and
needless to a great clerk. The few notes are
chiefly textual; but a helpful feature is a
cqmplete list of proper names. There Archi-
mades ( Dames* du judis) is identified
with Alcibiades, whom Boetius had men-
tioned as* a paragon of beauty, and the
Middle Ages toox for a woman, and “La
Grosse Margot” drops personality and be-
comes “enseigne de plusieurs mauvais lieux.”
The text is Longnon’s, with some corrections
of Gaston Paris. The publisher announces
that the notes and documents, collected by
Marcel Scliwob, on Villon and his com-
panions, will shortly be issued in his Biblio-
theque .du XVe. sidcle.
• * * *
UAmour masque (J. Gillequin, 45c.), the
unpublished story, which Balzac presented
to the' Duchess de Dino, is a mediocre
nouvelle, with an ingenious plot, that should
have yielded better results in his hands. A
passage from lleny Duvenois’ preface is
worth citing, as a half-truth: “One may say
of Balzac, that he understood men, but
revered the mystery of woman. I-Ie did not
vfenture to attack her. till he had given her
chin a beard and had left her toothless ; he
even desired the salvation of that delightful
monster, Madame Marneffe. Ever he as-
crioed the wickedness of the wife to the vil-
lainy of the husband, as the Creator made
Eve from Adam’s rib. He wished to behold
his_ heroines, by the taper’s milky light,
which gave youthful beauty to the woman of
30, and cast a glamor of simplicity over the
gossip of a drawing-room.”

A Collector of Moons
Dear “Red Page”— /
I have the collecting mania. As a boy, I
tried tops of matchboxes, postage stamps,
birds’ eggs, and perhaps other things for-
gotten now. I remember those three be-
cause the first lot earned me a good hiding at
school—the story is too long to tell; my
beautiful eggs were smashed on the furni-
ture van when the family was a-moving—a
habit it had, and my heart was broken for
nearly a week. I swapped the stamps for a
pin-fire revolver, which, I was assured, had
seen service in the Franco-Prussian war. I
was proud df that weapon, and with it
played “Buffalo Bill” out Cook’s River way,
I was doing finely in the name part when a
“copper” gave chase, and, in my fright, I
threw the shooting-iron into a gutter.
Books accumulated in my room from the
beginning of memory, but I did not regard
them as objects for collection until I picked
up at Skinner’s the first editions of Enoch
Arden, In Memoriam, and a few others, at
3d. and 6d. each. For many months I
haunted second-hand bookshops, until my
luck went dead out, and then I gave up all
thought of .competing with Pierpont Morgan.
Still, the hankering for some definite aim,
some tangible hobby-horse to tide, has re-
curred frequently. Pictures, china, pewter,
Chippendale furniture, and other obj&tg-
d’art were beyond me, and of late years all
I could collect were prescriptions and re-
ceipted bills, until I dropped upon a virgin
field. I discovered some objects of in-
terest which I believe I am the first
to collect. I cannot lose or brea c
them; no one could buy them from me
they cost next to nothing to collect, am
they occupy little space 1 am collectin
Moons —I mean, of course, the moons of th
poets.
My first moon was Rossetti’s, in “Th
Blessed Damozel”:—
. . . the curled moon
Was like a little feather
Fluttering far down the gulf.
And immediately afterwards I came acros
his sonnet —“A Match with the Moon”
Weary already, weary miles to-night
I walked for bed: and- so, to get some ease
I dogged the flying moon with similes.
And like a wisp she doubled on my sight
In ponds; and caught In tree-tops like
kite;
And in a globe of film all liquorish
Swam full-faced like a silly silver fish;—
Last like a bubble shot the welkin’s height
Where the road turned, and got behind me
and sent
My wizened shadow craning round at me,
And jeered, ‘'So stop the measure —one,
two, three.” —
And I faced on her, looked innocent.
But just at parting, half-way down a dell,
She kissed me for good-night. So you’ll not
tell.
That started me on the Quest in earnest.
Soon I found the new moon in the ballad of
“Sir Patrick Speus” :
I saw the new moon late yestreen
Wi’ the auld moon in her arm
which inspired Coleridge’s “Dejection, an
ode”:—
For, lo! the new moon winter bright
And overspread with phantom light
(With swimming phantom light o’erspread,
But rimmed and circled by a silver thread)
I see the old moon in her lap, foretelling
lhe coming on of rain and squally blast.
The last two are bad moons for a collection,
not being definite images. There are lots
of similar moons, such as Ben Jonson’s
“Goddess excellently bright,” and Philip Sid-
ney’s—she of the “long-with-love-acquainted
eyes”—very beautiful, but not pictoriaL
Coleridge’s “Rime of the Ancient Mariner”
yielded nothing more concrete than: — *
The horned- moon with one bright star
Within the nether tip.
and "the star-dogged Moon,” Shelley has
some mad moons, one in a fragment pre-
served by Mrs. Shelley :
And like a dying lady lean and pale
Who totters forth, wrapped in a gauzy veil
Out of her chamber, led by the insane
And feeble wanderings of her fading brain
lhe moon arose up in the murky Bast
A white and shapeless mass.
I don t care for that one much; the Moon’s
Song to the Earth in Prometheus Unbound
is better:
L a most enamoured maiden.
Whose weak brain is overladen
With the pleasure of her love,
Maniac-like around thee move
Gazing, an insatiate bride,
On thy form from every side,
Like a Maenad round the cup
Which Ag;ave lifted up
In the weird Gadmean forest.
I have not gone far into American verse for
moons. I have that well-known one of
Longfellow’s in “The Bridge,” and this one
of Frank Dempster Sherman:—
Within this silent palace of the Night
See how the moon, like some huge phantom
moth, 1
Creeps slowly up across the azure cloth
That hangs between the darkness and the
light.
There are some Australian moons in my
little lot —E. J. Brady’s “When the moon a
silver bugle,” in The Earthen Flow,” ’Rena
\\ allace s “When the moon a silver sickle,”
in A Bush Girl's Songs,” and the best yet,
Sidney Partrige’s “slim silver boomerang” in
Life's Wallaby (which is a prose book, with
passages close to poetry).
From your Page I have appropriated
Frank Henty’s:—
The first lone thruppence in the plate of
night—
A far faint moon lies on the evening blue.
There are others, but perhaps you’ve had
enough.
N.S.W.

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Early Spring.
I love the days this time of year,
When Spring, half frightened, shows her head,
And willow trees that long seemed dead,
Their hair with tender green have dressed.
When in the golden-crested morn,
The wonder-things of earth are born.
The river faintly hung with mist,
Is tranquil, save a bird whose wing
Dips down and stirs it ring on ring,
Then shaken are the purple flowers
That crowd the bank, and startled rise
Tremulous gold-winged butterflies.
VICTORIA.
G.J.
A Painful Interview About Nothing.
I sang my hymn to the morning star, and I swore
the forthright vow.
(I’m not quite clear as to what that means, but it
sounds well, you’ll allow.)
I lifted songs to the Brave and True, and supplied my
own applause;
When, out of the fresh-turned, frosted mould came
impudent, loud hee-haws.
I stopped and looked at the blushing rose; then I
said: “Come out of that!’’
The frightened voice of the cringing worm said:
“Wait till I get me hat!”
The earthworm scuttled out, hat in hand, and
trembled in front of me;
I looked him straight in the eye, and said: “Come,
answer me truthfully!
Who mocks my hymn to the morning star and snig-
gers behind the rose,
When out of my spangled, pulsing heart, the echoing
anthem flows?”
The earthworm fumbled his grimy hat, and fidgeted
in his seat;
Then he dropped his eyes, and loudly sniffed, and
shuffled his guilty feet.
“Vile thing of the earth!” (A deep red blush went
billowing down his spine.)
“Ho! What do you know of ‘lifted’ songs and
planets that shove and shine?
I lift my songs to the Brave and True, and I swear
the forthright vow,
But what has it got to do with youf” Then the
earthworm mopped his brow.
“I didn’t mean to offend,” he sighed. (A smirk on
his features played.)
“I thought that I’d have a joke, that’s all, and hide
by the garden spade.”
He blew his nose in a mournful way, and looked at
his blucher boots.
“You lifted that anthem well, and 100k —I’m sorry
for those few hoots !
You know how it is—worms will be worms! I was
born, of course, to crawl,
And here I’m walking about and mocking your forth-
right vow and all!
I don’t know what has com e over me!’’ (A silence
and then a sniff.)
I turned, and gave him a chance to use his sixpenny
handkerchief.
He cleared his throat in a little while, when he felt
himself again,
And said that the garden did me proud, and he rec-
koned we’d soon have rain.
His corns, he told me, were troubling him, and his
jaws were one big ache.
“I think I’ll toddle, if you don’t mind, in case the
weather should break.”
“Oh, no,” I said. So he seized his hat, and rushed
like a thankful soul
Out of my presence and house august, to his mean
suburban hole.
N.S.W.
THE EXTRAORDINARY GARDENER.
Bedtime.
The trees are sleeping, and the sky
Is one soft vague that holds the moon ;
The elves of night fluff whispering by,
The fays have donned their silvern shoon,
The wrinkled gnomes from Nowhere creep
To bid good people go to sleep,
And soon
The fairy folk will come and peep,
And pipe and croon
To keep all naughty dreams at bay
And guard us sweetly till the day
Let in the light,
And Mother Night
Goes gravely o’er the hills away.
So close your eyes, so deep, so blue,
And let the world slip out of sight.
The fairies, sure, are calling you,
Out there beyond the candle-light.
They’ll lead you gently by the hand,
Up and away to Slumberland,
And so
Sweet secrets you shall understand,
And you shall know
Such things that joy in glad surprise
Shall blossom, dearest, in your eyes,
And I, who ’m here
To hold you, dear,
Shall have my share of Paradise.
i. aoRIIiAND.
FRANK MORTON.
A Cycle of Wet Roses.
Blush roses of the time of dawn and dew,
Clear airs of Spring that sprinkle all the blue
With cloudlets gay;
In life’s large udder all the milk is new —
Drink while you may!
Lush yellow roses, of the glowing noon,
Wakeful, while languid lilies lie a-swoon
In the hot sun;
The world’s large cask gurgles a carel-ss tune —
Let the beer run!
Red roses of the sunset. Day is o’er,
The brown dusk draws her chariot to the door,
The first stars shine;
Let glasses clink; life has not spent her store
Of heartsome wine.
White roses, shining in the quiet night,
Breathing all sweetness of the old delight
And joys that fled;
Sweetheart —the words we speak are somewhat
trite —
Toddy, and bed!
N.s.w.
MARY McCOMMONWEALTH.

How to Address Letters.
Correspondents to The Bulletin would oblige the
paper {and. to an extent, themselves ) by taking
some care as to methods of address.
Business letters {with regard to subscriptions 3 ad-
vertisements, etc.) should in no case be addressed to
the Editor, but to the Manager. To send them to
the Editor is to invite inattention.
It is not a good thing to address editorial letters
to anyone by name. "The Editor, The Bulletin,” is
the proper superscription for “copy.” Delay, and
even loss, is sometimes caused by addressing to per-
sons instead of officials. The person may not be
there; the official aluays is.

THURSDAY, OCTOBER 19, 1911.
FREETRADE AND THE MINIMUM
WAGE.
The shortest and simplest explanation of
the recent annoyances in the British Isles is
the discrepancy between food and wages.
That explains most annoyances. Chiozza
Monev, a notable Liberal, recently published
a series of calculations on this subject,
though he apparently missed the natural
and obvious inference to be drawn from his
own figures. He estimated that between
1895 and 1910 wages in Britain rose, on the
average, 12 per cent., while the price of food
went up to 18 per cent. Therefore, the toil-
ing J. Bull —on the average, the worst-paid
worker in Western Europe—found him-
self (5 per cent, hungrier than before, and
in a perfectly natural way, he grew angry.
There were many fluctuations in the 15
years, though the final result was as shown
above. Wages rose from 1895 to 1900;
dropped from 1900 to 1904; rose again in
1905-6-7; and fell after that. On the other
hand, in the last years during which wages
were dropping, food prices were rising faster
than usual; between 1907 and 1910 the
workman’s pay fell per cent., and the cost
of his victuals went up 3 per cent. The
wonderful Tom Mann, well-known in Aus-
tralia, and one time keeper of the Beer and
Water Hotel somewhere in England, pro-
poses, as a remedy for all this misery, a legal
minimum wage of £2 per week. But that
remedy hardly applies in Freetrade England.
There wages reached the summit —the very
humble summit —in 1907, and straightway a
slump set in, because the manufacturer, with
enormous taxes to pay, and the competition
of all the cheap races of the world to face,
couldn’t stand the racket. The non-produc-
tive landlord —especially the city one—may
be on a brilliant wicket, but the man who
actually makes things has rather chequered
luck in many instances. Coal-mining reached
the top and started downhill in the latter
part of 1907; ditto the export of coal and
coke; ditto the production of iron, woollens,
cottons and linens; and out of 309 blast fur-
naces 51 closed up. The fact that the Ar-
gentine person in South America charged J.
Bull, the toiling iron-worker, more for his
imported loaf didn’t endble J. Bull, Esq.,
the owner of the iron works, to get any more
for the pig-iron he sold (say) to the Dago
in Italy. Rather he got less for the pig-iron,
because the Russian, who grows his own loaf
and thus saves the freight which J. Bull
has to pay on his loaf, is starting to make
iron and compete in the Italian market.
So food prices and wages bear no fixed ratio
to each other in England. Tom Mann, of
course, saw tfie minimum wage idea at work
in Australia, and he annexed it; but, with the
natural dulness of a man who used to run
the Beer and Water Hotel, he annexed it in
a badly mixed condition. Victoria, for in-
stance, which devised the minimum wage
idea, did not enact that hoots should he made
at Melbourne with white labor exclusively,
and that the white labor should be paid a
minimum of 50s. a week for 48 hours, while
providing that similar boots might be made
at Ballarat by imported Hindus working 72
hours for Bs. a week. It was not so mad as
to pass a law which, under pretence of giving
the Melbourne white bootmaker a steady
wage, would leave him no wage at all. Yet,
that is exactly what the British Freetrade
advocate of the minimum wage proposes to
do. Under the British system the cheap
colored worker at Bombay' or Canton has the
same access to the London market that the
Ballarat worker has to the Melbourne mar-
ket, and while that state of things endures
the highest wage the British worker can pos-
sibly get is simply the highest figure at which
it is possible for him to compete with the
foreigner—the cheapest foreigner. The fixing
of any legal minimum wage which is above
that figure, whether that figure is 12s. or
lGs. or 30s. or 7s. 6d. a week, must simply
deprive the British worker of his job and
leave him to starve, as he began to do when
the slump happened after 1907.
Thus it comes to pass that if the price of food
goes up so high that the British workman
can’t live on his wages, he may simply have to
die on his wages. The price of his food is the
biggest amount that some wholly unsympa-
thetic Argentine or Russian or Canadian can
charge for wheat and beef in a world where
the demand for wheat and beef is growing-
faster than the supply. (As a side issue, the
world’s wheat crop for this year is estimated
as being over a million tons less than in 1910
and less than in 1909, so, with a large increase
of population and consequently of de-
mand, the foreign supplier should be
able to shove the price of the loaf
up a bit higher still). On the other
hand, the wages with which the British
toiler and moiler buys this variable loaf arc;
ruled by the selling value of his goods, and
these are fixed in open competition with many
countries which grow their own victuals and
are not at the mercy of foreign suppliers—
some of them countries where rents are low,
and clothes and fuel almost superfluous.
Supposing the wage figures and the food
figures happen to disagree so widely
that the British worker is unable to
exist, no mere minimum wage law
can help him. That device is good
enough in a country which raises its own
provender in its own paddock, and shuts out
foreign goods made by foreign cheap labor ;
but it is of no earthly value to one that has
destroyed its local agriculture and opened its
doors to all the cheap labor manufactures of
the globe. All Bull, the toiler, can hope is
that one foreigner, perhaps a rice-fed
foreigner in India, won’t be making cloth
very cheaply just at the time when another
foreigner, perhaps a foreigner with a huge
Panama hat in Argentina, sees a chance to
sell wheat and ox-meat very dear. A great
deal may be done to ease the strain by im-
proving social conditions and wiping out the
rich and unproductive class of parasites, but
no social reform is likely to affect the basic
problem. It represents a trouble that will
recur while Freetrade lasts.
THE NECESSITY FOR KICKING JOHN
WEARY.
When John Mukbay, State Premier of
Victoria, slouched home from England the
other day, long after his contemporaries had
arrived, he found an item of news awaiting
him. The compilation of the census figures
has advanced far enough to show that one
progressive State is entitled to gain a mem-
ber in the House of Representatives, and one
unprogressive State has lagged sufficiently
to be qualified to lose a member. Queens-
land makes the gain, and Victoria stands the
loss. A similar event happened at last re-
adjustment ; then, it was N.S.W. that
gained, and then again it was Victoria that
lost. Tasmania, which is the worst case of
all, is prevented from losing members by
that proviso in the Constitution which gives
every State a minimum of five Representa-
tives. It doesn’t fall, because it is down on
the cellar floor already, and it can’t get
down further unless it digs a pit. Victoria
not being protected in the same fashion, is a
chronic loser. It sinks lower and lower in
the scale of political importance, and be-
comes, by comparison, of less and less ac-
count. It is hampered, of course, by the
fact that it is far the smallest of the main-
land States, being only a quarter of the
area of the least among its neighbors It is
further hampered by the fact that it doesn’t
get anything like the trade that naturally
belongs to it. It is the outlet devised by
Pbovidence for a large section of Riverina,
but N.S.W. wont allow railway communica-
tion except at one spot, and would rather
keep a most valuable part of its own territory
a howling desert than allow it to prosper ■
and ship its goods via Melbourne. Victoria
is retarded some more by the circumstance
that it includes a larger proportion of pic-
turesque, but barren and almost inaccessible.
mountain country than any other mainland
State. Also, it is already by far the most
thickly peopled of all the States, so it has
less room for expansion than its neighbors,
let, with all these drawbacks, it need not
have commenced, for many years, to
drift downward from its proud position as
the second member of the Commonwealth,
but for the insane policy of its rulers, as
typified by Bent and Weary, and but for
the crazy jealousy with which Melbourne
tries to grab everything, and the vinamous
hrck of patriotism which makes that big-
city prefer to see half Victoria a wilderness
man see it prosper by trading through any
Victorian port that isn’t Melbourne.
The eastern part of Victoria is very
empty, because it consists largely of beauti-
ml and picturesque rocks, extending up to
(1500 ft. or more above sea level. The rich
and magnificent western section is very
empty, partly because of the State’s de-
ranged anxiety not to disturb the huge land
monopolist, and partly because a great part
of the district is too far away from Mel-
bourne to do business profitably with that
bloated metropolis. The west has, at Port-
land, a better harbor than Melbourne, but it
isn’t allowed to do its commerce'there be-
cause the place isn’t Melbourne. JMelbourne
arrived first, for the reason that, in the
days of the State’s beginnings, the few feet
of water in the Yarra were sufficient for
all the shipping that wandered round the
Australian coast, and, having arrived, it
would rather kill Victoria quite dead than
give any other port a chance. So there are
no direct railways to Portland, to bring
thither the trade of the north-west. The
lines which reach that fine harbor are wildly
circuitous, and devised to turn 100 miles
into 200, and the charges are specially high,
in order to turn the 200 miles into 400.
There is no big city at Portland, by reason
of these drawbacks, and no great array of
deep-water wharves. Melbourne makes very
little out of the north-west, because that
region is almost empty, while Portland
might make millions, because, with direct
communication to a handy, deep-water port,
the north-west would cease to be empty.
The north-western lines drag a fragment of
goods to Melbourne, at a loss, while they
might carry immense quantities to Portland
at a profit. The rise of Portland wouldn’t
happen at Melbourne’s expense, because the
old city would still serve the same district
as at present. By opening up the west, and
giving Portland every possible chance to be
another Melbourne, a vast amount of immi-
gration might be diverted to a region which
now gets no immigrants. Then Victoria
would have two ports of call for big ocean
steamers, in place of one, but, apparently,
it doesn’t want two. Its banks and insur-
ance offices and mercantile establishments
might double their business, but it would
seem that they hate the idea of doubling
their business. And Victoria wouldn’t be
steadily losing its membership in the House
of Representatives. But then, perhaps, it
prefers to lose its membership, and is rather
pleased than otherwise to find itself dropping
from its old pomp and importance.
* * * *
There are large experiences ahead —and
not very far ahead—for Australia, the coun-
try which keeps its first-class ports mostly
closed, and its very inferior ports mostly
open. The day of the Big Ships is coming.
The Big Ships are wanted, partly because
of the growing taste for luxury and partly
as a matter of economy, for one 40,000-ton
vessel can be worked much more cheaply
than four 10,000-ton vessels, and very much
more cheaply than eight 5000-tonners. In
the Atlantic, the passenger ship that is
45,000 tons register and 850 ft. long, ar-
rived a while ago, and the 50,000-ton
vessel is coming, and the 60,000-ton affair
is threatening to come. These monsters can
get into very few places, and the places they
can get into have a notable start over all
rivals. Australia has , already the 7000-ton
coaster, which can onlysget into a few of the
ports in actual use, and the 12,000- or
13,000-ton oversea vessel, which can get in
at still fewer. The original curse of Aus-
tralian coastal cities was that the site for
a new settlement was generally selected be-
cause it was far enough up a river to make
fresh water obtainable, and because, at the
place where the settlement was pegged out.
the river was so shallow that it could be
forded with safety. The secondary curse
was that the cities which were put in these
ill-selected places, used, and still use, all their
influence to prevent new cities being founded
in better-selected places. Thus, Townsville
dreams alongside the shallow Ross Creek,
while a much better harbor lies neglected a
few miles awa.v. Rockhampton is located
far up a hot ditch, which even the small
Queensland coastal vessels have mostly out-
grown, while another good harbor lies almost
unknown at its back door. Maryborough is
an unsuccessful sort of place, a long way up
a still worse ditch than Rockhampton’s
ditch. Brisbane is Rockhampton over again,
located on a narrow section of an ill-con-
ditioned stream that all the cash in Queens-
land couldn’t qualify to receive a first-class
modern vessel. Newcastle is on a lament-
ably difficult river mouth. Sydney will only
rank as a second-rate port when really big
vessels come here. The channel is tortuous
and much blocked by islands. Most of the
shores are precipitous, and quite unfitted for
wharves, and the most useless and precipi-
tous parts are those where the water is
deepest. The comparatively small stretch
of low-lying shore, where there is fairly
deep water, is already congested. Mel-
bourne, with its miles and miles of possible
wharfage, would in the end beat Sydney out
of sight but for the lamentable fact’ that
there isn’t enough money in Victoria to
make a channel across the wastes of Port
Phillip deep enough for the biggest type of
steamer. Adelaide isn’t a patch on Port
Lincoln, just as Melbourne isn’t a patch on
Portland. As for Perth, it has given up
professing to be a port, and depends on Fre-
mantle. Yet even Fremantle doesn’t com-
pare with Albany for possibilities.
If Australia does not realise the fact that
it ought to make use of the easilv-accessible
deep-water ports, which have been so care-
fully kept closed through the jealousy of
badly-placed cities, it must alwavs be a
back number in shipping affairs. Thanks to
the lamentable shortsightedness of the Oldest
Inhabitant —a person who now dodders
around in spectacles, and tells strange tales
of the amazing ouantity of wood he used to
chon in his bovhood —almost every first-class
Australian harbor has no city upon it, and
hardly any .business facilities beside it.
There is a great prospect ahead of the State
which is the first to realise that it will pay
to put its best harbor foremost, quite irres-
pective of the frenzied jealousy of some big
town, the site of which was selected
in days when a 1000-ton ship was
a rarity, and a 1500-ton vessel a
leviathan. John Weaby, of Victoria,
should think especially hard over this
matter, for his State is the smallest on the
continent, and the one that can least afford
to throw away chances. It is the one State
which is dropping steadily from its high
position. It entered the Commonwealth with
a rank and dignity that far exceeded its visi-
ble resources, and, as a matter of pride,, even
the tiredest Premier might do something to
preserve that rank and dignity. It is up to
some State to set an example by abandoning
the ruinous policy of congestion, and Premier
Weaby has an inducement to lead the way,
inasmuch as his State is the first to fall into
contempt and disrepute by reason of the con-
gestion policy. Whether the contempt and
disrepute will move Weaby to action is a
question which no one can decide as yet.
People who know Weaby are of opinion that
nothing would move him to anything. Man
is of three kinds —the kind that moves with-
out being kicked ; the kind that moves when
kicked; and the kind that is kicked, and
doesn t move. There is a suspicion abroad
that the present Victorian Premier belongs
to the third variety.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Child of the Bulldog Breed.
Ihe A.M.P. Society has been digging into
its mortality experience for the third time
since it was born, in 1849, and the results
hit two or three people in the eye. They
hit the society itself, to begin with. Two or
three years ago, it decided to lose its purely
Australian character by hunting tor busi-
ness in Britain and elsewhere; and now it
lias to admit that its own Australian mor-
tality experience has been far more satis-
factory than that of the British offices. On
son.> ex P enence of the latter, from 1803 to
18J3, it would have had 10,713 deaths to
pay tor, whereas, as a matter of fact, it
only had to pay out on 8259. That applies
only to insurances of “healthy males’’; but
in the other classes the result is the same,
and explains why the Australian office can
pay bigger bonuses than the one that in-
sures the Britisher. But an even more vio-
lent knock is given to the person who tells
the Australian that he is a decadent, and
not half the man his Dad of the Bulldog

How an “Unbearable Position” Suddenly
Became Bearable; and tlie Moral Thereof.
Foe over a fortnight, now, the N. S. Wales
Assembly lias been a decently behaved bodv.
the D ?/ sslles hay e bee n thrown at anyone by
the flower of the Liberal” party; there
has been no blasphemy or obscene language
or mule-headed holding-up of the country's
business by the crowd that used to claim
that it represented law and order; and as-.i
result, the Speaker has not been forced ,o
from fi be ii GXpUl i- on of fading “Liberals"
tiom the House for quite a while. Yet the
conditions are identically the same as tliev
were throughout the discreditable period
when Wood, Onslow, Fitzpatbick and the
rest daily and nightly trailed the good name
of the N.S.W. Legislative Ass. in the mud.
McGowen is still in office; Willis is still
Speaker; Wood is still firmly squelched so
often as he attempts to obstruct business or
insult the Onalr under cover of LarlicLiuent-
ary privilege. What is the answer to it?
YVhy does Liberalism,” which pleaded, only
the other day, in extenuation of its disgrace-
ful conduct, that “human nature could not
i k* n dure the situation created
by the Will ±a- Holman compact/’ now en-
dure that same situation without a curse?
The simple explanation is that the Speaker-
baiters have been “called off.” Sydney Tele-
bapii fomented the campaign of insult, slan-
der, assaiut, etc., the object whereof was the
ejection of Labor from office. The thing was
a failure, as abject as the Dreadnought sub-
scription, or Anti-Sosh, or any other of the
two or three dozen reactionary efforts that
sheet has made of late years. Realising that
fact, the Telegkaph, on October 4, delivered
the following ukase :
The members who persist in moving these motions
of dissent x:an do no good to their own party and less
than none to the Parliamentary rights that they are
trying to vindicate There have been no less than
five abortive votes of censure moved within the few
months of this Parliament’s existence, and most of
them, by their want of tact, proved distinctly bene-
ficial to the Government. A prolongation of this
futile conflict with the Speaker can only serve the
same purpose, and as a matter of common-sense, it
is time that it stopped. No principle is vindicated,
for in these cases protest stands for nothing. The
Opposition must see that it is now only aggravating
the evil it is trying to cure.
It acted like magic. Next morning, the
paper was able to say :
There were no questions for the Speaker when the
Legislative Assembly met yesterday, neither was any
ruling of his subjected to formal scrutiny by way of
motion of dissent. The House took qaite an extra-
ordinary turn, in fact. Questions without notice,
which have always consumed from half an hour to an
hour each afternoon this session, were disposed of in
about 10 minutes, and by a quarter to five members
were engaged in the consideration of the first motion
on the business paper. But what the House gained
in the way of orderliness and respect it lost in in-
terest for the public.
And a week later: —
Yesterday, as last week, the Assembly was a most
orderly place.
There are several useful lessons to be learned
by the N. S. Wales electors from the above.

It will contain the best Stories, Verse and

tralasian writers.
The illustrations, by “Hop,” Alf Vincent, Nor-
man Lindsay and other leading representative
artists, will be the finest Black and White work
obtainable in Australia, and will reflect the
natiunal humor.

reflection of the life and thoughts of our country,
and therefore useful as well as interesting.
Agents will be supplied at the usual rates, but,

One is that the policy of “Liberalism” in
Parliament is evidently directed from some
back office or other in King-street, Sydney;
and another is that, however illegal and dis-
graceful and imbecile the said policy may be,
“Liberalism” may be relied on to cheerfully
adopt it. The astounding abruptness with
which the Wade party stopped persecuting
Willis, upon the order to “cease fire” issu-
ing from the demesne of “Whiskers” Ward,
argues, either that the “Liberals” were liars
when they said that they could not help riot-
ing “because they were only human,” or else
that they have now ceased to be human. The
general impression will be that the truth
was not in them when they denied that the
whole uproar whereby N. S. Wales has been
rendered a jest and a byword, of late, was
part of a carefully worked-out plot. The
coming by-elections will give at least two
constituencies the opportunity of saying that
they don’t want Parliament deliberately de-
graded for party purposes; and that, in par-
ticular, they don’t want it degraded on the
say-so of an irresponsible anti-Labor editor.
The elector who omits to lodge this badly-
needed protest deserves all that is coming to
him in the way of misgovernment.
F.A.Q. Wheat and the Men Who Grow It.
Of late, certain wheat farmers of Australia,
especially in Victoria, have been exercised
over what they call the F.A.Q. standard and
other matters pertaining to the grain that is
turned into the staff of life, and the Vic.
Assembly has appointed a committee to inves-
tigate the matter and recommend improve-
ments, if it can. l’robably that committee
will discover some surprising facts, but the
circumstances surrounding the wheat-growing
industry are so well known, and the remedies
so obvious that no inquiry is really needed.
The F.A.Q. (fair average quality) standard
of wheat, which purports to represent the
bulk of the merchantable wheat crop—a
mixture of all the grain grown in the State
that is fit to be turned into flour —is fixed
by the corn trade section of the Chamber of
Commerce through a sub-committee consist-
ing of two millers, two shippers, and one
grower! This standard is such a fool of a
thing that the wonder is the farmer hasn’t
chased it into oblivion long ago. Neces-
sarily, it penalises the progressive agricul-
turist, who tries to raise the quality of his
wheat at the expense of the quantity, and
advantages the man who studies quantity
only, even though he lowers the standard by
doing so. As for the method by which it is
fixed, only a mild lunatic would have en-
dured it for more than a season. It is as
though a fruit-grower took a case of plums
to the city, and there met two fruiterers
and two jam-makers, and the five put a
value on the plums. The grower is in a
minority of one to four every time. An even
worse folly is the system of “free storage,”
under which a farmer who stores with a
miller or shipper gets gratis board and lodg-
ing for his wheat during a period of six
months, provided he agrees to sell to the
storer. This latter person agiees to buy the
goods at current rates on a basis of equal
merit and weight to that declared at the
time of delivery. If the farmer declines to
accept the then price, and sells elsewhere,
the storer may charge for storing, insurance,
etc., any old price he likes. The pffint of this
piece of humor —or rascality—lies in the
fact that the wheat is milled, just as butter is
milled ; its identity is destroyed, and, as no
detailed certificates are issued setting forth
the number, brands and weights of bags, if
the farmer takes “his” wheat back and sells
it elsewhere he doesn’t get his own wheat,
but merely so many bushels out of the bulk
stock. This means that the storer —whether
miller or shipper—can sell, mortgage, manu-
facture or otherwise manipulate the farmer’s
wheat and use the proceeds for further
deals. He doesn’t store the wheat free for
six months; he borrows it free of interest
for six months. Also, as wheat notoriously
gains in weight at certaimtimes of the year,
the farmer makes the storer a present of this
additional weight. Another trouble, which
is no fault of the farmer’s, is that the cabled
London quotations for wheat, as published
in the Australian press, don’t correspond
with those of even dates, given in the Mark
Lane Express, the standard trade journal,
and in most cases the cabled prices are lower
than the journal’s figures. This latter ir-
regularity could easily be corrected by the
Agent-General; but the other troubles call
for energetic action by the Government. In-
stead of the Chambers of Commerce —of all
bodies —fixing their absurd F.A.Q. standard,
the State agricultural authorities should fix
standards for first, second and third grade
wheat; the State should erect storing sheds
at railway stations, and certificates should
be issued showing the «nftmber, brands and
weights of bags stored and the grade of the
wheat, the farmer being charged the trifling
sum necessary to cover cost of storage,
etc. That individual would lose his precious
“free storage,” and the system wouldn’t suit
the careless, dirty grower who puts quantity
before everything; but the painstaking, pro-
gressive man who exercised skill and energy
in selecting and improving his grain would
get a good deal more for his wheat than at
present; he would get the value of the full
weight of it, and he would get the cash for
it as soon as the miller bought it and took it
away—not six months or more afterwards.
The wheat-buyers would probably disapprove
of the new system, but then Australia has no
urgent need to study wheat-buyers. It could
do without them, but it would have some
difficulty in getting along without the
growers.
The Australian Pressman Gets a Move On.
Probably the most rapid and complete or-
ganisation of any body of workers in the
Commonwealth, since Federation became an
accomplished fact, was that which resulted
in the recent formation of the Australian
Journalists’ Association, and its registration
under the Federal Arbitration Act. Hur-
riedly formed in Melbourne, in the first
quarter of 1910, by the working pressmen
on the city papers there, the enthusiasm then
engendered has swept round and over the
continent, until, to-day, branches have been
formed in Brisbane, Sydney, Melbourne, Bal-
larat, Bendigo, Adelaide, Broken Hill, Perth,
and Hobart. And, in each of those places,
practically every working pressman has
joined his branch. Since the registration of
the A.J.A. (May, 1910), in the Federal Ar-
bitration Court, as an industrial body, an
enormous amount of work has been done, all
of it voluntarily and in the spare time—
what there is of it —of the officers who guide
the movement. The most significant sign
of the strength of the new body is to be
found in the last issue of the Australasian
Typographical Journal. In its leading
article that paper says:—
The moving spirits of the Australian Journalists’
Association were not long in coming to the conclu-
sion that their organisation would gain greatly added
power if they could form an alliance with the organ-
ised typographical sections of the Commonwealth,
which are so well welded together in the Austra-
lasian Typographical Union, and the A.J.A. execu-
tive officers have . been in consultation with the
typographical officials in an endeavor to consummate
an entente cordiale. It seems to be the desire of
the journalists to bring about the establishment of
a uniform set of conditions that will be consonant
with the high-class duties which devolve upon them
generally, and in their endeavor it is not likely they
will look in vain for support from the typographical
section. If the spirit of unionism which at present
appears to pervade the journalists continues to be
exercised in the future, there is no reason why a
close affiliation of the two sections may not eventuate.
In the formation of an alliance between the two
bodies great mutual benefits would undoubtedly re-
sult.
It is an extraordinary transformation. Up
till yesterday, or the day before, the average
Australian pressman excused his acquies-
cence to hours of duty that would have made
a blackfellow fight, and wages that were a
miserable recompense, by the frequent and
free use of the gag “loyalty to the paper” ;
and if the manager was a person, with the
hide of a sawmill boiler, and the soul of a
pawnbroker when the question of rises in
salaries was mooted, the pressman merely
cursed under his breath and slogged on with
(1) energy that was expected to have steam
turned full-on from sun-up to sun-up. if
necessary; (2) ability to meet and handle
any and every emergency from the explana-
tion of a local or political tangle to a
reasonably intelligent article on the right
of the ancient Jews to occupy the land of
Canaan; (3) ox-like patience that made him
appear content to see his family only when
they were asleep; (4) resource, tact, per-
sonal fearlessness and any other article of
equipment that the occasion called for; (5)
a sufficiently asinine temperament to stand it
all. But in a puff of sudden intelligence, the
Australian pressman has decided that the
old game is not good enough, and that it is
urgently desirable that new rules should be
provided forthwith, or as near to forthwith
as possible. Twenty-four hours a day won’t
do any longer as the time within which a
reporter shall be liable and ready for duty,
the holidays in a year will have to look less
like the plums in a workhouse duff, and the
pavment for services rendered will be re-
quired to put on a bit of weight in order to
make a fairer fight with the grasping rent-
lord and the fat food rings. And if the
wealthy papers of Australia say they cannot
afford to make the required improvements,
there doesn’t seem to be much doubt that
they will be invited to prove it. Which
will be a job of some magnitude.
How Good Men Are Made Criminals.
As a general thing, the legal principle that
an embezzler deserves gaol is sound enough.
But there are exceptions to the rule: in fact,
cases crop up from time to time, where the
man who is technically a criminal has a
claim on the sympathy of the community,
while his “victimised” employer merits
naught but the brick of scorn. Such a case
was advertised at the Adelaide Criminal
Sittings, the other day. According to the
Register (4/10/T1), William Gods-
maark pleaded guilty to having embezzled a
considerable sum. In the words of the prose-
cuting firm’s own counsel: —
Accused had been in South Australia for 30 years,
and until recentlv had enjoyed a first-class character,
and the confidence of his employers. He had ex-
cellent testimonials to hear that out. . . .He had an
invalid wife, whom he had been regularly support-
ing, and a comparatively young family. Accused’s
wife was 63 years of age, and suffering from heart
disease, which might prove fatal at any moment....
Godsmaark was paid £1 a week, and allowed 15
per cent, commission on his sales. The wages had
averaged £3 16s. 9d., and accused stated that the
maintenance of two horses used in the van for hawk-
ing the goods, and his own travelling expenses
totalled from £2 to £2 10s. per week.
Here was a middle-aged man, in one of the
most prosperous States of the most prosper-
ous country on earth, who was asked, in a
manner of speaking, to keep himself, an in-
valid wife and four children, on a wage
which works out —after deducting the cost of
horse-feed and travelling expenses—at fl
11s! 9d. per week. It was tantamount to
expecting a trainer to get a Cup favorite into
hard condition on a quart of water and two-
penn’orth of chaff per diem. The most as-
tute of financiers, the most resolute of econo-
mists, would be baffled by such a problem.
When it is understood that the half-starved
Godsmaark had over £lOOO worth of goods
to handle at a time, the question arises
whether some law ought not to be framed
making it a criminal offence for big business
firms to deliberately manufacture criminals,
as in this case, by rendering men desperate
and then putting them in the way of temp-
tation. Europe elevated its hands in pious
horror the other day. at the following de-
scription, by a German authority, of the
wav the Brazilian rubber-collectors live
The seringueiros (rubber collectors) of the Amazon
district, Brazil, are mostly recruited from the States
of Ceara and Maranhao. The wastage from death,
sickness and desertion is very high. The serin-
gueiro’s profit never amounts to more than £5O or
£6O, which, as a rule, is barely sufficient to cover
the living expenses of his family during his absence
at the seat of operations, and very often he remains
in debt to the owner or sub-owner of the seringal.
Thanks to the soulless methods of Private
Enterprise, in those commercial byways
where our industrial laws have not yet
penetrated, there are Australians to-day
whose lot is no better than that of the
rubber-collectors of South America. The
Brazilian’s family is liable to starve while
he is away working. So is the family of
the South Australian of 30 years’ standing,
with “a first-class character and the confi-
dence of his employers.” It is the per-
sistent recurrence of cases like Gods-
maark’s which causes “Anti-Socialism” to
look not merely futile, but vile and immoral
and unclean.

The Yell for Artisans.
The N.S.W. Commission, appointed to in-
quire into the alleged shortage of workmen,
reported, the other day,'that there was room
for 3000 more artisans; and, almost simul-
taneously, the president of the Master
Builders’ Union implored young men to
come along and learn to be masons and car-
penters and bricklayers. There probably is
a considerable shortage of labor in the build-
ing trade at the moment —to this extent,
that various people want to rush things, and
there isn’t the labor available to meet the rush.
The State Government alone is raising the
wind at the rate of about £5,000,000 a
year, and a great part of that money is going
in wages. Nobody supposes that that wild
loan boom can last; to continue to borrow
£5,000,000 a year is unthinkable. And
when it stops, and the artisans and others
who depended on it are sacked, there won’t
be any scarcity in the building trade —there
probably will be a superfluity. A country
that deliberately encourages booms, by bor-
rowing madly, and thus creating an arti-
ficial but quite temporary demand for labor,
must expect to suffer from shortage of sup-
plies ; and if it does any equally wild thing
in the way of importing men to meet the
boom demand, it will inevitably have an un-
employed surplus when it wakes up with a bad
head in the morning, and finds that it can’t
put its lOU on the market any more, and
that the bottom has dropped out of the
wonderful prosperity overnight. Australia
wants population, and wants it worse, per-
haps, than any other country in the world,
but it doesn’t want its lads turned on to the
building trades, for instance, in quantities
sufficient to meet the demands of master
builders who are working under inflated
conditions. There was the same disastrous
inflation in the late ’Bo’s, and the Sydney
brickworks had an annual output of over
225,000,000 bricks. The master builders
yelled for apprentices to the building trades.
But the Great Drunk ended ; the State’s ac-
commodation from Uncle, which had reached
£4,750,167 in 1891, shrivelled to about a
million, and there was such a slump in the
building trades that, whereas 225,000,000
bricks didn’t meet the supply while the
bubble was growing, the total output was
only 92,113,000 in 1894. The fact is, this
country hasn’t yet learned how to grow
steadily and rationally. It depends so much
on “the great primary industries,” and “the
great primary industries” are so erratic,
that it is either a feast or a famine. When
it is a feast, and money is plentiful, there
is a boom in buildings and other construction
works, and the Government mostly makes
matters worse by fleeing headlong to the
pawnbrokery, and spending wildly. When
it is a famine, the construction boom stops,
and a big part of the population finds that
it has been seduced by big wages into trades
and other callings which, to a great extent,
only exist during booms. Under the circum-
stances, the best thing to do is to disregard
the howl for more artisans in the building
and allied trades. There may be buildings
hung up for want of labor; but, even with
the present supply, it is just as certain as
anything can be that presently there will be
a whole lot of labor hung up for want of
buildings. There is certainly an urgent and
permanent demand for very many thousands
of skilled workers—to make here, under de-
cent Australian conditions, the immense mass
of goods that this country now imports : but
until there is a very large repairing of the
tariff, the importation of unlimited artisans
would only mean wholesale reduction of
wages, or the manufacture on wholesale
principles of an unemployed class. And it
is a long and weary job waiting for that re-
pair of the tariff.
The 4ft. B&in. Railway.
A few lines from “Henky Horsecollar” :
If the last week’s Parliamentary debates count for
anything, the 4ft. 84in. gauge is going to be ac-
cepted for the transcontinental railway to the West.
Apparently, it will be accepted without investiga-
tion, simply because it is the British and United
States gauge, and because the infallible Ma State
followed their example, and adopted it. The broad
gauge isn’t likely to be even inquired into, and, as
for the Brennan mono-rail, which may yet relegate
all the gauges to the scrap-heap, it doesn’t get so
much as a mention, because it is new, and all new
things are accursed. The proposed arrangement is
rather rough on some of the States, when the history
of Australian railway-building is considered. First,
the 4ft. 84in. gauge was agreed upon by N.S.W.,
Victoria and S.A. Then N.S.W. ' lunged its mind,
and clamored for the Irish (sft. 3iu. 1 gauge—clam-
ored so bitterly that the other Stale! 1 assented. And
when Victoria* and S.A. were hard at work building
according to the 6ft. 3in. gauge, which N.S.W. had
insisted upon, and Victoria had got one line com-
pleted and working, N.S.W. changed its mind again,
went back to the 4ft. 84in. idea, and, without even
the empty politeness of an apology, started the dis-
crepancy which has cursed Australia ever since. It
was the same old story as in the matter of the Aus-
tralian capital and other instances—the story of
N.S.W.’s utter inability to keep faith. Meanwhile, it
is a notable circumstance that the 4ft. 84in. gauge
isn’t gaining ground throughout the world to any
great extent, though it is still far ahead of its rivals.
In 1885 it represented 74 per cent, of the world's
lines, while narrower gauges stood for 14 per cent.,
and broad ones for 12 per cent. In 1910 the 4ft.
84in. gauge stood for only 71 per cent, of the world’s
lines, and broad gauge railways had increased till
they represented 14-J per cent. The 4ft. 81in. gauge
is really the standard in only two countries of great
importance. These countries were the first railway
builders, so they had no experience to guide them,
and were thus extra-liable to make blunders. And
they are countries which stick solidly to private
railway ownership, so unless Prime Minister Fishf.r
believes in private ownership, he can hardly profess
to believe that Britain and the U.S. are infallible.
He may be right, but it is a pity that he doesn’t
take a little more trouble to make sure that he is
right.
The Old Men and the New.
“G. 11.” offers a few observations: —
The Sydney Worker lately rose to the occasion
with a series of anti-immigration article by one
Newton Wanliss, and gave a considerable amount
of space thereto. Newton Wanliss was solid all
through on the necessity for keeping Australia empty
as long as possible. This particular item struck me
as the keynote of his policy:—
“To admit indiscriminately to the full rights of
Australian citizens, hordes of assisted immigrants
who have done nothing towards building up the Com-
monwealth, is about as equitable as to ask a man
who has spent a quarter of a century in arduously
building up a lucrative business to receive into that
business on equal terms a partner who is not paying
anything for the privilege.”
Now, I rather admire that attitude ; still it is best
to leave off generalities and come down to details.
The early Australian settlers used up most of the
gold—especially the easily-won gold—and had a
fairly good time on it while it lasted. Nowadays,
the gold output is less than it was in 1856, and it is
no longer mostly alluvial metal that the casual
person can dig out single-handed with a pick and
shovel. Then, the best of the land is sold. The days
of free grants are over. The new arrival no longer
gets a few acres just outside Sydney or Melbourne
casually thrown at him. And all the millions that
were obtained by land sales have been used as rev-
enue, so that there is little or nothing to show
for them. The debt is enormous, and while the
remunerative public works are mortgaged up to the
eyes, there remains a very considerable amount of
debt which is represented bv no remunerative public
works whatsoever. Taxation is higher than it ever
was before, because the interest bill must be paid,
and the simple, lazy, easy process of living on the
proceeds of land sales is nearly at an end. The new
arrival doesn’t come here merely to collect a divi-
dend, like the man who is “received into a lucrative
business on easy terms.” He comes to pay heavier
taxes than ’did *the early settlers ; to carry the rifle
which they didn’t carry, because Australia is now up
against a danger that the early arrivals never had to
reckon with ; to help pay off the loan money which
they used for revenue purposes ; and to find that they
have consumed the best of the sudden-wealth chances
in the shape of free land and easily-accessible gold.
So, though this is a much better country than the
one he left, there is something to be said on the side
of the desirable immigrant. The old hands shouldn’t
put on too many airs about what they have done in
building up the Commonwealth. Digging up surface
gold was easy. Selline- ownerless land was easier.
Borrowing money was easiest of all. The old hands
were on the easiest wicket in all history. Anyhow,
unless many more white men come here to defend
Australia, or are willing to defend Australia without
coming here, there is a poor prospect ahead. Let
the world be informed that this “lucrative business”
has no defenders, and will have no defenders, save
its present owners, and the “man who spent a quar-
ter of a century in arduously building it up” is
likely to find the whole thing taken from him very
shortly. The congestion policy has planted so much
of the business in two or three handy places beside
the sea-shore that it is altogether too accessible.
Lastly, I would like to know where the line is to be
drawn between the people who are rightly here and
the people who wrongly want to be here. A consider-
able proportion of the people arrived here rather re-
cently, and were taken into the “lucrative business,”
and some of these now object to anyone else coming
in. Attorney-General Hughes, for instance, arrived
in 1884, when other people had been toiling about a
century in building up the “lucrative business.” I
arrived earlier than that. Premier McGowen
(N.S.W.) got here in 1855. When did Mr. Newton
Wanliss happen along?

Song of the Cow Slave.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Past four o’clock, the stars is up, an’ like-
wise Dave an’ Joey.
Oh! Holy Smoke, there’s little Sue ! —she
might ’ave slept a w’ile !
Pore kid« ! They earns their bit o’ grub!
(Whoa back! Bail up there, Snowy!)
An’ all to ’elp their mean ole Dad ’eap up ’is
blasted pile !
I often thinks it’s flamin’ ’ard th’ way them
kids is treated —
(Stand over there, yeh mongrels! Where's
tli’ legrope got ter now?)
Never gets a bit ter eat till milkin’ is com-
pleted—
Never sees a school or church, but Cow, Cow,
Cow !!
Vows, cows, cows! —cast yer optic on ther
sows !
Black an’ w’ite an’ brindle cows,, an’ cows
both fat an’ lean ;
Spotted cows an’ speckled cows,
fyoan an’ red, an’ freckled cows;
Short’orns, Jerseys, Ayrsliires, " Alongs” an’
crosses in between!
Quite often in th’ camp at night me mind's
made up ter chuck it;
But some’ow, in th’ mornin', I’m toilin' in
th’ dust;
I never get a chance ter leave! {Lift up!
Don’t kick ther bucket!)
An’ all me resolutions, made ther night be-
fore, are bust!
Yet ev’ry night, in that ole bunk, I chew th’
dashed thing over;
An’ ev’ry night, it seems ter me, I make th’
same old vows ;
When I sleep, I dream th’ cows are stuffin’
in th’ clover!
Strike me pink, it’s nothin’ else but cows,
cows, cows !
Cows, cows, cows! — ev’ry sort th’ law
allows !
Cows with sores an’ lumpy jaw, an’ cows
with crumpled horns!
Stumpy cows, an’ lanky cows;
Stupid cows, an’ cranky cows;
Cross-eyed cows, an’ crippled cows, an’ cows
with warts an’ corns.
I wish them blokes wot gas erbout “Th’
Dand,” an’ “Milk an’ Honey”—
An’ likewise them as makes up rhymes
erbout th’ “Milky Way,”
Was graftin’ ’ere where fifteen bob is thought
er lot of money—
Yes, fifteen bob er week —fer coves that’s
toilin’ night an’ day !
Lor’ bless me, but th’ magpie wouldn’t wake
them with ’is warnin’!
Th’ cow boss beats th’ magpie, an’ ’e makes
much louder rows!
An’ them winds they call th’ “balmy breezes
of th’ mornin’ ”
Mixes pigsty hoo-kay with ther cows, cows,
cows !
Cows, cows, cows —how I ’ate tli’ flamin’
sows !
I ’ate the black an’ brindle cows, an’ cows
that’s w’ite an’ broion!
Coves wot milks ’is cranky cows!
f will chuck th’ blanky cows!
I will roll Matilda up an’ do er bunk ter
to wn ! !

that they don’t fear it. The bleating on the
one side, and the silent indifference, if not
active approval, on the other, are very signi-
ficant.
—
Joseph Ward, of Maoriland, is always
struggling to find new ways of dissipating
money. His latest proposition is to reduce
the pension age under certain circumstances,
to t>o years for men and 55 for women, and
to pension every impecunious widow with
children ac the rate of £l2 for one child, £lB
for two, £24 for three, and a maximum of
£3O for four or more. It is a noble idea, for
this world is a cold place at the best for a
widow with a quiverful of young “encum-
brances.” Still, it is a pity that Joseph
Should run the risk, by building on a founda-
tion of sand, that some harassed successor
will have to abolish his good deeds through
sheer lack of money. Year by year Joseph
shoves his big crazy loan on the English
market, or the Australian market, or any
.other old market that will lend the money.
The loan keeps up the boom, and the boom
keeps up the revenue, for a million of bor-
rowed money may change hands 20 times
during a year till it looks like £20,000,000,
and this borrowed million that looks like
£20,000,000 may easily add a lot more than
a million to the revenue, because nearly
every sovereign in the country passes through
the Treasury once, if not twice, in the course
of 12 months. That is why the failure of a
£2,000,000 loan may make the revenue slump
a lot more than £2,000,000. And then the
gaudy folk who have been buying popularity
by paying lavish pensions out of loans gener-
ally feel themselves due to retire, and some
one else has to take up the sordid struggle to
make ends meet. Always the lavish, whirl-
ing person—the Thomas Mcllwraith, or
DTjncan Gillies, or Julius Vogel, or
Joseph Ward—is called away by business
or increasing venerableness when the loan
boom breaks, and some plain, brown hod-
carrier inherits the job and the ruins. And
blessed is the land which can find the plain,
brown hod-carrier to take charge of the
ruins.
That ill-judged person, N.S.W. Attorney-
General Holman, does himself and his party
no good by refusing to reveal the reasons why
John Dixon, who lately raised some kind of
a dust at Lithgow, was released from prison.
The manner in which Holman dodges the
question is more agile than convincing. Still
less convincing, and not half so agile, is the
bogus wrath of the Opposition over the mat-
ter. One of the wonderful doings of the
Wade Government was the premature release
from gaol of a very unsatisfactory alien. It
didn’t give any good reason for its proceed-
ings ; it didn’t produce the papers; and, ap-
parently, it, or someone on its behalf, went
so far as to burn or swallow the documents
—anyhow, when the new Government went
to look for them, they weren’t there, and
they haven’t been there since. Holman is
foolish to refuse an explanation, seeing how
he and his party blamed Wade and co. on
a somewhat similar occasion, but, on the
other hand, Wade and co. are gifted with
a mountainous impudence to put on airs of
lofty indignation over Holman’s doings. If,
perhaps, 20 years hence, the Wade push
gets back to office, and breaks its aged neck,
and falls over its long Dan O’Connor
beard, in a sprint for the official archives, and
finds on arrival that the papers in Dixon’s
matter have disappeared, then it will have
a right to get moderately angry, on the
ground that its opponents are almost as bad
as itself; and it was so darned bad that for
another person to be nearly as bad is hor-
rible. But its present wrath is premature.
The country has a right to be angry if it
wants to. The Opposition has only a right
to take a back seat.
One Ewing, an earnest but flabbergasted
member of the Tasmanian Legislature, is
cruising around with a Bill to make prefer-
ence to unionists punishable by fine or im-
prisonment. Lie also proposes to abolish the
minimum wage, and establish instead a stan-
dard wage—no one to receive either less or
more without a special permit. A most in-
teresting man is Ewing, though born 300
years too late. Possibly he is a re-incarna-
tion.

Fathers of handsome, well-set-up young
daughters should go to Her Majesty’s (Syd-
ney), and there see “Rigoletto,” and mark
the moral, which is, “Keep a bulldog.” Even
a fox-terrier pup—the species that mistakes
the moon for a stray sky-cat, and barks
furiously at its* midnight saunter across the
heavens —would have kept the wicked Duke
out of Iligoletto’s garden. Here, as in other
scenes, Verdi served up all the old traditions
that go to make “Rigoletto” far more effect-
ive as an opera than the modern style of
Puccini’s “Tosca.” The latter lacks the
wealth of chorus singing, which the old style
opera always provided. In “Rigoletto,” the
chorus people, heavily disguised in small
masks, hats, cioaks, satin slippers, etc., hide
themselves outside the walls of Rigoletto’s
house, and do the deed in a fine burst of con-
certed melody. Rigoletto, very slow in the
up-take, has previously opened the garden
gate, and, having no dog, allows the Duke
to slip in, get behind a small bush, and sing-
enchanting ditties to his daughter. Rossi,
as Rigoletto—and the opera is mostly Rigo-
letto —gave a performance of quite unusual
merit from a histrionic point of view on
Monday night, and his make up was a genu-
ine work of art. Vocally, Rossi is an artist
of considerable tonnage, with an occasional
drop below his own best efforts. Melba, as
Hilda, showed no trace of her recent illness
on Monday, but there isn’t enough of Melba-
Gilda to go round, for Verdi keeps the star
soprano out of the first short act of Rigo-
letto, and divides the fat between the Duke
and his jester. The famous “Caro Nome,”
by which Hilda lights up the stairs, as she
goes aloft, in trills, cadenzas and exquisite
flute-like warblings, with a soft shake like
an echo at the end, gives Melba one of her
golden moments. McCormack, as the girl-
hunting Duke, is rather disappointing, and
for the first time this singing season the
tenor fell off his perch as second to, or neck-
and-neck with, Caruso. John is an actor
sometimes, but that time doesn’t happen in
“Rigoletto.” Even the ear-haunting “La
donne e mobile” misses fire, because it needs
temperament as well as voice to give this
passionate lilt 20s. value in the pound. Miss
Voluntas-Ranzenburg, as Maddalena, scat-
ters her beautiful voice over too small a
patch in the fine production of “Rigoletto.”

SYDNEY.
Scarpia, the Grand
Opera villain
(Scandiani) and

“La Tosca,” with Scandiani as Scarpia,
and Wayda as Tosca, is magnificently staged,
and McCormack-Cavaradossi, on the first
night, was in silvery voice. Scandiani is
(after Melba) the most tiresome person in
the present opera co.’s aviary. He never
makes mistakes, knows his music, never
hangs round the prompter for his lines,
never sings to his friends in the boxes or
stalls (these weaknesses have been observed
in other members of the company, Melba
and Wayda always excepted), and gives a
histrionic and vocal representation of the
villain Scarpia which follows the usual
Scandiani precedent of perfection. Soprano
Jeanne* Wayda, who made a hit or bang in
“Madame Butterfly,” repeated her assaults
in “Tosca,” and caused such a tumult, up-
roar and frenzy amongst the populace, at the
first performance of “Tosca,” that once more
The Bulletin brings her under the eye of
the law for inciting a riot. On the evidence
before the Court, there is a great and won-
derful career before Jeanne Wayda.
Miss Ethel Irving vacates the Criterion
on Friday night, and on Saturday Maggie
Moore is to appear as Mrs. M‘Sweeney,
The lady with the palpably Italian name
was invented by the late T. E. Spencer, who
dropped her into his humorous verse and
sketches. Just before he died, he collabora-
ted with another writer to drop her into a
farcical comedy. The season is limited to a
fortnight.

As a preliminary to the Dago-Turkish
war pictures, that will roll in upon Sydney
presently, “Scenes around Tripoli” were
shown during the week at the Lyric. The
film, a very good one, leaves the geperal im-
pression that a good shelling and a re-build-
ing would be the best thing that could hap-
pen to the town. Pictures of Cavalry man-
oeuvres were also shown, and Puccini’s "La
Bohbme” was the subject of another excel-
lent outburst. “Gaumont Graphic,” at Col-
onial No. 1, showed the 'world’s latest news
in a nutshell, with some North Wales
scenes, to give a touch of the picturesque.
Tue Colonial No. z list included the “Bathe’s
Gazette,” showing current Australian news
in pictures, and the Fogwell-Barry race in
England.

Brun, the Potter-Llartwell Trio, Will Sulli-
van, Smite and Smote —these appear to have

A “Boy Comedian” and a “Society Reciter”
