The “Beanstalk” pantomime is a travel-
worn veteran of nearly one year, but it goes
fresh and sprightly at Adelaide Royal in the
hands of Mabelle Morgan and Dorothy
Firmin, with Marie Eaton, an Adelaide
girl, as Fairy Queen. The comedians. Jack
Cannot- —who has an unusually fine ’voice for
such parts—Darrell and Loydall, are learn-
ing that the mere mention of suburban Bow-
den is, for some unknown reason, a “sure
laugh,” William Hassan is a fair Cow when
he isn’t a dark horse, and the extra turns by
the Pantzer troupe and the Doherty sisters
nut ginger into the show. At the Tivoli. McLal-
len has been proving that there is still some-
thing new left to show in roller-skating;
Ossipoff, of the wicked eye and the grand
voice, has been singing; and the Reeces’
musical turn has been mixed with the infant
Reece’s contortionist business. At the King’s,
Beatty and Miss Grimston are now giving
the powerful little “Bishop’s Candlesticks,”
Hassan is conjuring, and Jules Garrison has
come in with his Roman Maids. And at the
Oval, the commercial travellers have friv-
olled wildly, occasionally cleverly and al-
ways successfully, in the cause of Charity.
This annual show had to go to the Oval. It
had grown so much, from a modest begin-
ning, that no other ground was big enough
for it.
Two more nights of “Louis XI.” will dis-
pose of that cruelly dramatised monarch at
Her Majesty’s (Melb.). The play that
Boucicault adapted from the French pre-
sents Louis, in four scenes, as a mere ghastly,
ghostly remnant of his former self, doddering
about in fear of death, and making himself
horribly unpleasant to everybody save two
women characters who have nothing to do
with the case. A previous scene, in which
Louis does not appear, serves to the
audience of the forthcoming apparition, and
to explain that a young man in tin armor,
who brings an official snort from the Duke
of Burgundy, bears also an old family
grudge against his Majesty the Spook. This
grudge he duly pays by seizing the old bundle
of hatred and malice, in an unguarded mo-
ment, and threatening to' kill him. This,
apparently, causes the Dauphin to succeed
to the crown a day or two earlier than had
been expected. The young man from Bur-
gundy is very noisy, as well as violent, and
by the time he has finished sparing i,ouis's
life, there is little life left to speak of. An
air of reality in the Court surroundings, and
of pomp and circumstance in the dialogue
gives a sombre shade of interest to “Louis
X 1.,” especially in the first three acts, or
scenes. Seriously considered, it is a purely
theatrical drama all the same. The dodder-
ing monarch is left alone and unprotected
on his ridiculous last legs whenever he wants
the stage to himself. On the particular
occasion when his assailant is roaring him
into a delirium of funk, the old gentleman
is supposed to be safeguarded at close quar-
ters. Yet the roarer can walk in at one door
and out at another without interruption, nor
does anybody hear a noise until the drama
calls for assistance.
This libellous sketch of a famous French
king has been cleverly drawn in the past, of
course, otherwise the drama would not have
survived. Charles Kean is said to have been
an appallingly crafty, treacherous, humor-
ous, superstitious old Louis, and the late
Henry Irving was admittedly at his best in
Kean’s best parts. lie was reasonable in his
mane up, and subtle in his suggestions of
decrepid fiendishness. LI. B. Irving com-
mands attention only by the frank falsity of
his performance. It is all on the surface,
like the chalk on his face. An ordinary
circus clown is not more pale than was Irv-
ing last Saturday night. Lie provoked smiles
Francis B. Stubbs (Temple Harrison), the
journalist, interviews Satan.
where he was striving after shudders. His
appearance was funny, and at the back of
his feigned venomousness one could almost
detect the amiable mummer enjoying his own
joke. Two or three showy character parts
are noisily played, one of the best-fitted mem-
bers of the co. being Frank Tyars, as the
grim old provost, whilst Tom Reynolds and
Miss Brown have the distinction of introduc-
ing a whiff of comic relief into the play’s one
rural scene. The last week of the Irving
season at Her Majesty’s starts on Saturday,
with “Dr. Jekyll and Mr. Hyde.” And then
the Opera, with a large O.
“A Girl’s Temptation,” at the Princess's,
continues to rejoice in the fact that it was
written by a woman for women. Anyone
could guess as much without reference to the
advt. There are moments when tue words
■‘Ladies Only” seem to be painted on the
drama’s brow. As, for instance, when the
gentle heroine informs the haughty father of
her partner in impropriety that she hopes
to make him a grandpa, but has not yet men-
tioned the matter to the author of her bud-
ding happiness. Her intention to get
married in the meantime is thwarted by the
scandalous behavior of the villain in the
plum-colored frock coat, and the triumph of
Virtue leaves Mrs. Grundy still wondering
what the world is coming to. Mrs. G., by
the way, is the one member of the sex for
whom women don’t write nowadays.

Melbourne knows it as the annual occa-
sion for a revival of Wirfchs’ circus. This
vear, the circus makes its first appearance in
a new Hippodrome building of Palatial
Dimensions, and 12 performing Polar bears
will nod their heads at the amazing scene.
Also, 40 humans, including the Pick of the
Earth’s Marvels, will be supported by our
old friend the blood-sweating hippopotamus,
and the rest of the menagerie.
Roy Redgrave is still playing the Devil
at the King’s—a nice, gentlemanly
Devil, who encases his hooves in pa tent-
leathers, _ and wags his tail underneath his
dress suit. Satan is placed in a most em-
barrassing position when the wedded Lady
Sybil comes out of her bedroom in an un-
fastened state, and makes such suggestions
to him as the late Mrs. Potiphar is alleged
to have made to .Joseph, the Canaanite Wow-
ser. Redgrave, happily, proves equal to the
occasion. He pours a cold stream of moral
verbosity upon the lady’s hot, unruly pas-
sion, damping her so much that she commits
suicide in front of a mirror. Then a female
journalist, who is alleged to be “pewer as
the driven snow,” makes love to Mr. Poti-
phar over the corpse of his late missus, and
the Devil, after bestowing a paternal bless-
ing. removes his fur cap, and looking up at
the flies, announces that he is “a step nearer
home.” On Saturday, the bill will be changed
to “The Midnight Wedding,” with Miss
Duggan as the midnight bride.

Ere the next Bulletin has delivered its
message to a listening world, “The Choco-
late Soldiei;” will be no more at Melbourne
Royal. These are the last nights of that sub-
stantially melodious opera and thoroughly
well-built entertainment. Some people have
heard it only once, which is, perhaps, a
worse sin of omission than not having heard
it at all, and to these misguided members of
the Miss Gibbs persuasion The Bulletin
would say: “Have another”; “Repeat the
dose”; “Wet the other ear.” On Saturday
(28th), the Irving co. will shift to the Royal
from Her Majesty’s, for the purpose of rob-
bing “The Lyons Mail” five times before pro-
ceeding to Adelaide. But, in the meantime,
an amateur co. of great social weight and
proud distinction will play “The Importance
of Bfeing Earnest” for one consecutive night.
This event will transpire (n.v.) on Friday,
the 27th.
At Spencer’s, Melbourne, last week, was
the yarn of Henry VIII. and Catherine
Howard, which was very pleasing on its fem-
inine side. The Miss Howard of cinema-
tograph fiction is a lady with fetching con-
tours and a smile of many meanings. Her
very best smile is probably intended to sug-
gest infidelity. “The New Faith,” Spencer’s
other star drama, belonged to the Mercia and
Marcus Outrageous school of melodrama. It
told the old, old story of the noble Dago
who dropped his sins and good feeding for
prayer and snuffle among a herd of old Ro-
man Wesleyans.
“Lex” writes; —
As the N.S.W. legal profession looks at it, At-
torney-General Holman’s only offence in ordering
the release of the Lithgow striker, Dixon, lay in the
fact that he disclosed that Judge Hey don had re-
commended the step. Nothing is more necessary, if
justice is to be done in such cases, than that the
Judge should be protected by having his name kept
out of them. So strongly did the late Chief Justice
Darley feel on the subject, that, on the
Reid Government taking steps to make public a
report of his on a capital case, he threatened that,
it it did, he would never make another report so
long as any member of the Ministry held office. G.
B. Simpson adopted a similar attitude on another
occasion. The reason is obvious. These reports
are necessarily of the most confidential character.
They may contain reflections on the conduct
of the case by the Crown; they may point
to incompetence on the part of the defending
counsel or the attorney instructing him, or both;
they -may impute corruption, ignorance or bias to
the jury or to the witnesses. Obviously, if the
prisoner is to get a fair run for his freedom, the
Judge'; in his comments to the Minister for Justice,
must have the same feeling of certainty as to his
remarks not being disclosed that the penitent in the
Confessional has.
Holman, it would seem, hastened to “give away”
the fact that Heydon had recommended the exercise
of the prerogative in regard to Dixon,, by way of
scoring off the Wade party, which freed the Syrian
abortionist, Ben Habdait, on the eve of the last
elections, without consulting any Judge in regard
to the merits of the case. It may have been good
political tactics, but it was not playing the game, so
far as Heydon was concerned. A stronger man
than Holman would have followed the Judge’s re-
commendations and, strong in the consciousness of
duty done, carried the whole burden of blame—
assuming that any materialised—on his own shoul-
ders.
Speaker Willis, in the N.S.W. Assembly,
after a wearying course of vulgar insolence
from Opposition members :
The House will see how difficult my position is.
owing to the unruliness of the hon. member (Wood),
the veiled insults to the Chair, and the disregard of
the dignity of the House, It is a most difficult posi-
tion in which I am placed, and I must ask hon.
members to protect me against personal insult.
Subsequently, the Bega branch of the Liberal
Ass. carried a motion “expressing approval
of Mr. W. H. Wood’s conduct in Parlia-
ment recently, and their unbounded confi-
dence in him.” Which, coming from the
people who elected Wood, shows the sort of
people that Wood represents.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Stargazer”: Stirling Whyte, the veteran act-or
who died at the Barrier the other day, was a mild
man of regular and methodical habits, and even
those who thought they knew him best believed he
was perfectly, content to go on playing Methuselah
till the end of the scene. But about four years ago,
when he was 70, I heard him confess: “I want to
play Romeo. I have always wanted to play Romeo.
For 40 years I was told 1 was too young to play it.
If I don’t get my chance soon, these d —d managers
will be telling me I am too old.”
“0.K.”: Harry Rickards is said to have come to
Australia for the first time in 1870, when he
warbled ditties of the “Captain Jinks” order to an
admiring world. But it was not until the end of
1885 that he carried out his design of running his
own show in these latitudes. ’Arry arrived with a
Rickards-Leete combination (Mrs. Rickards being
the Kate Leete thereof), and they nlayed a two-act
farce called “Bric-a-Brac,” in which every member
of the small co. appeared in various parts, and sang
or danced frequently, the dominant Harry being
rarely off the stage. A while later he was associ-
ated with the Cogill Brothers in a mixed show, the
Cogills figuring as managers. Then, after one or
two trips back to Britain during the time he was
building up a bank account, Rickards made a start
as a permanent manager in Sydney—converting the
old unpromising Garrick into the Tivoli. In 1895 he
was strong enough to extend his operations to Mel-
bourne, and he took the old Opera House for just as
long as the Board of Health would allow it to be
inhabited. In those days, Geo. H. Wood and Ada
Delroy were Harry’s principal rivals in greatness
on the bill'l of fare. Winton, the ventriloquist,
and Johnny' Coleman came soon afterwards. The
Rickards wicket had been good from the start, and
it improved rapidly. In ’96 or ’97 the energetic
old “lion comique” imported a high-class vaudeville
co., with the new biograph as a chief attraction,
this spec, being quite apart from his ordinary Syd-
ney and Melbourne entertainment. Whilst he was
renting the Bijou, as well as the Opera House, in
Melbourne, he imported Charles Cartwright and
Co. to play “Moths,” and a couple of brilliant come-
dies by R. C. Carton. With this co., by the way,
Arthur Styan came to Australia. The engagement
of Pattie Browne, who returned to Australia as
the heroine of “The Little Minister,” was another
of Harry’s unprofitable endeavors. Outside of the
never-failing music-hall business, Rickards had no
luck in theatrical speculation. He staged two very
good pantomimes, and probably lost by them. The
old, reliable variety show was his money-getter all
the time. Once he was threatened with powerful
opposition by an American co. engineered by Henry
Lee, but Harry rushed off to Europe, engaged a
new “host of talent,” regardless of expense, and
presently knocked the rival concern into oblivion.
Dog-fanciers in Melbourne are grieved to hear that
the noblest tyke of their acquaintance—the champion
St. Bernard —has been guilty of an unprovoked
assault on its owner. Jules Grant, business manager
for Billodrama Anderson, is the owner. Until a few
days ago the relations between the dawg and its master
were amicable, and then, for some reason or other —•
indigestion or “nerves,” or temporary insanity, per-
haps—the mighty brute went for Grant with all its
mouth and main. His brother came to his rescue
before any portions of his face were actually re-
moved, but, in the meantime, the hundredweight of
dog-meat had been playing havoc with his amiable
expression. Just now he shows a number of bite-
marks, in addition to some which are covered with
sticking-plaster. than that, Jules suffers from
a conviction that his prize-winner and he will have
to live apart for the future. The animal having
turned dog on him, he could never more look at his
teeth without thinking of the awful moment when his
head seemed to be coming off.
“0.K.”: Compared with “Curley” Bellew and many
others, H. B. Irving is but a tame reproducer of his
father’s mannerisms. Thirty years ago and there-
abouts the profesh, for a time, was obsessed with
a weird spirit of mimicry. Young men-members of
the Bateman Co., what time Henry Irving was its
high-salaried star, and the same young men and
others who supported him after he became his own
manager, were slaves to the habit of talking, walk-
ing and posing like the “chief.” More or less like
him. One of his closest mimics was A. W. Pinero,
the dramatist- —tall, thin and angular. If Pinero,
who played minor parts, had to come along previous
to the star’s advent on the scene, his general re-
semblance to Henry Irving was always liable to
bring him a burst of misguided applause. More
than one of Pinero’s young contemporaries in the
co. had to leave it on account of their half-uncon-
scious imitations of the leading man; they detracted
from the oddity of Irving’s original personality.
Older men in other companies became imitators, if
they had a natural bent that way, and'a very strik-
ing actor, named Floekton, practically ruined his
chance of getting good London engagements by re-
moulding his mannerisms on Irvingesque lines.
Floekton went to America, and died there. Kyrle
Bellew, who may be said to have been a graceful
dandy mimic of his old boss at the Lyceum, gave
Australia some very forceful fugitive suggestions of
the real Irving at his best. But Bellew was com-
paratively small and nretty, whereas H. B. Irving
is rather tall, and not at all pretty. In fact, he is
not nearly so picturesque in facial expression and
figure, or in his posing, as the father whom he re-
sembles.
From London: The Earl of Yarmouth, who will
be remembered in Australia as an unspeakable skirt-
dancer, has been undergoing his public examination
in the London Bankruptcy Court—the second time
he has gone up the spout. He has been trying
poultry-farming lately, and one of his few available
assets reads this way: "‘Stock of fowls, £7.” The
poultry lured him on to write a farmyard play—-
“The Pigeon House” —and he lost £l5OO by pro-
ducing it at the Court Theatre. He owes seven
Jewish money-lenders £17,000, but swears he only
got £ 4000-odd. The rest of the debt represents in-
terest, premiums, commissions, etc. His father, the
Marquis of Hertford, has been allowing him £3OO
a year, but that wasn’t enough to keep him and the
other poultry. Still, he hoped to pay off his liabili-
ties when he came into the estate —he was “antici-
pating the early death of his father.” Pleasant
reading for the old man!
“Adelaide”: “Marguerite Ismay” and her girls are
at the King’s, Adelaide. She is advertised as having
created a sensation in England. The latest London
papers describe “Margaret Ismay” as playing lead
in “The Chocolate Soldier” at the Lyric there. Two
of them, perhaps.
“S.T.S.”: Can’t understand why The Bulletin
(28/9/’ll) calls the late Miss Eastlake “angular.”
I saw her in the original “Pink Dominoes,” and
through all Wilson Barrett’s earlier successes—
“ Lights of London,” “Claudian,” “Silver King,” etc.
As an actress she was charming. And physically—
well, W.B. himself told me much later, in Australia,
that extreme stoutness had caused her retirement
(he said nothing about insanity) at the age of 35.
William Arrowsmith Ryan, who died in Melb.
Hospital, last week, aged 84, first appeared in Aus-
tralia as an actor of heavy villains in the early 'so’s,
lie played with Brooke, in Geelong, on G.V.B.’s
first visit there, and was Brooke’s understudy at the
old Royal, in Bourke-street. G.Y.B. was often in-
disposed, and Ryan took many of his “turns.” In
the first ’6o’s he went with Clarence Holt to Maori-
land, and open <1 the theatre at Dunedin. Just pre-
viously, he had been part of the backbone of George
Fawcett’s management at the old Princess’s; the
company included Clarence Holt, Hoskins, Fawcett
(3), Milne, Rosa Dunn, Miss Montrose (Mrs. Ryan,
who is still living), R'ay and wife, Miss Bush (who
married George Collier)’, and many other mummers of
note.
“Mime”—his tribute': Harry Rickards was one of
the few managers that I ever met, saw or heard of —
in fact, I may sav he was one of the few of his class
in the world —who never made the beginning of a
pretence at a flirtation with any of his female em-
ployees. The reputation of the average entrepreneur,
in this regard, is rotten. In the big European con-
tinental cities it is practically impossible for an
operatic, vaudeville or other artist to bourgeon into
a “star” unless she first goes through the formula of
“being friendly with the manager” ; and she has
little hope, in most cases, of keeping her place in
the firmament unless she is satisfied to go on doing
so with him, or with someone else in authority. It
is much the same in America and the United King-
dom. A frequent remark of one of London’s leading
actor-managers is: “I divide my lady artists into
two groups; those I have kissed, and those I propose
to kiss when I get time.” And no one ever hoots
the sentiment; for the obvious is a thing that people
do not trouble to hoot. Harry Rickards had heaps of
little weaknesses. He was absurdly, vain; he was
ostentatious; his dress effects #ere calculated to
make the plain man wince ; and so on ; but, even in
those censorious haunts where spurned pros, gather
for the purpose of absorbing beervand denouncing the
entire managerial tribe, it has never, to my know-
ledge, been alleged that he so much as looked side-
ways, in a dishonorable manner df speaking, at any
of the host of fair damsels he employed in his time.
Jules Grant, manager for Anderson at the King’s
Theatre (Melbourne), who came off worst in a dis-
agreement with a pet tyke the other day, and had
his face woefully gnawed, has a grievance against
Redgrave. As the gentle hero, in his own
version of “The Sorrows of Satan,” Redgrave is
reminded nightly of how, on one occasion, a dog had
flown at his throat. “Yes,” he replies, “dogs are
honest. They are good judges of character.” This
leaves poor Grant, with his face in a sling, no option
but to reward the dog and kick himself.
The deceased Harry Rickards made two or three
trips to Australia before finally settling down in
Sydney, at the end of the ’Bo’s. Had it not been
for a disagreement with the lessee of the old Opera
House, in King-street, with whom he was working on
a percentage basis, he would probably not have settled
down at all, at this end anyhow. The result of the
wrangle was that Rickards, against a lot of well-
intentioned advice, leased the Garrick, in Castle-
reagh-street, in 1893, and has been on the s'te ever
since, reaping a fortune. When the old Garrick was
burned down, the present Tivoli was erected, and,
in turn, the “king of vaudeville” became proprietor
of the Melbourne Opera House, Adelaide Tivoli and
the Perth Palace. As' a performer in the English
halls, Rickards made hits with a number of comic
songs, notably “His Lordship Winked at the
Counsel” and “Hi ti tiddly hi.” After that came
Australia. Rickards was the embodiment of a
square deal, a good sport, and, perhaps best of all,
a man whose coin did not clink aloud when he
passed it on to the downhearted and the needy. The
actual amount he gave away in that direction will
never be known. Probably the happiest day in his
year was Christmas Day, when he gave a dinner to
the poor of Sydney, in the Town Hall. There, in
between spells of parsing plates and loading young-
sters with indigestibles, he listened to hard-luck
stories, and did what he - thought the fair thing.
And generally it was a very fair thing.
“Kobbi”: Australian parents spend a big lump
on the musical training of their children ; yet when
they desire to get a certificate of proficiency, the
onlv colleges which conduct examinations are those
that hang out their shingles in Fogland. Moreover,
in spite of our Melbas and Graingers, we can’t even
examine the few youngsters in the elementary sec-
tions, but must import Smith-hyphen-Jones with a
haw in his voice and a monocle in his eye to do the
job. It’s pretty rotten, isn’t it?
The Vic. Artists’ Society’s Annual Displa y
In its exhibition of some 200 pictures—ex-
clusive of miniatures—the Victorian Artists’
Society, this year, offers few attractions to a
public that takes no personal interest in
painters, and looks only for results. At the
far end of the Athenaeum Hall (upstairs),
which serves for the V.A.S. gallery, nowa-
days, a vast, broad-framed picture, by J.
Ford Paterson, is notable mainly for its
size. The subject is Catani Lake, Buffalo
Mountains, treated in the artist’s typical,
low-toned manner—a painting with no force
in it. Flat, stale and unprofitable, from a
commercial point of view, but soothing to
any eye that lias previously been staggered
by the amazing color freaks of, say, Tom
Carter’s "Reflections,” hanging on the same
wall. The lady in this and in another pic-
ture from the same hand, might be pleasing
but for her gaudy environment, ilis worn
suggests a sane, capable draughtsman who
has lost his color senses and gone on a “jag”
of contrasts. One of the few redeeming fea-
tures _of the show is Max Meldrum’s
“Jennie” (No. 32), the head of an ugly
woman, painted on old-masterly lines and
well worth considering for its Rembrandtian
appeal to human interest, Realism is lack-
ing in the face of Bernard Hall's portrait of
a lady, titled "The Pheasant Hat.” The
hat is all right; the painting of the gloved
hand shows the craftsman’s talent for tak-
ing pains, but the face has no truth in its
tint. A nude, reclining figure by the same
Bernard Hall, at the opposite end of the
room, is unworthy of him. The figure seems
even faulty in drawing, the pose is ultra
buttocky, the flesh painting unfinished, and
the blue-greeny reflection on the model’s
stomach is obviously overdone. His head
and bust of an Anglo-Jap girl shows Ber-
nard Hall in his most trying mood. The
face is convincing enough, but a browny-red
ribbon in the girl’s hair, and a dead blue
kimono, bound with a purple violet sash,
contrast horribly with a smudgy mauve
background, whilst the waist is embedded in
a setting of pale green bile. Two of Rupert
Bunny’s portraits challenge criticism, be-
cause £5OO of the Felton Bequest money was
recently expended in the purchase of a pic-
ture by this well-befriended artist. One of
them (No. 113) is a full length portrait of
Mrs. Archibald Currie. The flimsy mauve
gown is well painted, the hat is bad, the
face beneath the hat has an air of strengtn
in the portraiture, and the picture, as a
whole, is ridiculous. There is not a reason-
able suggestion of a slim figure inside the
dress. Mrs. Currie’s full length portrait ter-
minates practically at the face. Fred McCub-
bin hangs his best effort, thus far, in clothed
figure painting. The full length portrait of
his wife (No. 10) may be a trifle out in the
lines of the lady’s admirable neck, but it is
generally satisfactory, and pleasantly strik-
ing. There are life in the black-robed figure,
charm in the face, and art without ugliness
in the making of the picture. “The Quarry”
(66), by McCubbin, less Turneresque than
his “Flood Waters” (11), is a very likeable
thing. A daring impression of Henley-on-the-
Yarra at night, with fireworks “going off” in
the darkness, hangs to the credit of Ambrose
Patterson, whose “Henley in the Afternoon”
is an ambitious failure. A portrait by the
same artist of his brother is a successful
attempt to give serious picturesque effect to
a soldier in scarlet tunic and preposterous
busby. This is a difficult subject, cleverly
dealt with. Charles Wheeler has a pleasing
study of a wicked-looking lady in a long,
light cloak, marred by the feeble suggestion
of a gloved hand holding her hat; also, an
imposing full length portrait of Chief Jus-
tice Madden in fancy dress, which is satis-
factory as regards the headpiece only. One
of Alf Fisher’s, not being allegorical, is very
acceptable; a landscape, by Officer, escapes
the wide sweep of disapprobation ; and here
and there among the trivial things hung on
the screens there is something worth buying.
Withers is not well represented, but his prin-
cipal contribution is at least respectable. As
a rule, the more promising landscapes of im-
mature artists are rather too big for the
subject, whilst the exhibits of the less prom-
ising students’ deserve nothing but bad lan-
guage. Certainly the outlook for the V.A.S.,
is not hopeful.
From the Cold Tea point of view, Maine
has gone Bung.
The spot from which McDouall Stuart
started, just 50 years ago, on his successful
expedition across the continent from Adel-
aide to the Indian Ocean, is now owned by
Advektisek Bonython, who intends to erect
a commemorative tablet there.
While the Federal railway from Port
Augusta to Kalgoorlie was under discussion,
Bruce Smith and some others shouted to the
effect that the States through which the line
was built —S.A. and Westralia —ought to
hand over to the Commonwealth, free of
charge, a strip of land 25 miles wide, along
the route of the railway. It seems a pity
that Bruce Smith didn’t think of that idea a
little earlier, and propose that N.S.W. should
hand over to the Commonwealth a strip of
land 25 miles wide along the route of the
Federal railway from Yassberra to Jervis
Bay. It isn’t too late for Smith to make the
proposal now.
KINKS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The world doesn’t want the perfect actor ; it pre-
fers one with a few kinks that it can hang its after-
noon tea-talk on. — “0.K.” (B. 21/9/’ll).
A perfect mummer, nobly planned,
Is one we really cannot stand;
We very much prefer the brand
Of fair to middling pros. ;
At teas, described as “afternoon,”
Above the noise of cup and spoon,
You’ll hear our chat —Lord, what a boon !
They’ve kinks to diagnose!
Maereadv Jones, a fav’rite star—
“ His voice is most pec-ul-i-ar.”
“Well, shouting drinks in ev’ry bar
The best voice will undo!
His walk! Oh, isn’t it absurd?
The other night my brother heard
He had a wooden leg!” “My word!
I wonder if it’s true?”
Miss Bernard Smiff—“a dear, sweet pet!”
“But can she act?” “Well, yes.” “I’ve met
Her eldest daughter-—nice girl; yet
Her Ma looks half her age!”
“How is it done?” “Dear, don’t ask me.
For ‘make-up’ is, as you’ll agree
(The large size bottle, five and three),
A secret of the stage!”
“Like Romeo Brown? I’m fairly stuck!
Why, he’s a dear, a dream, a duck!. .. .
What’s that you say ? A tram-condue—
Tor once! My, what a shock!”
But isn’t it a treat to know,
That ev’ry actor in the show,
Has got some sort of kink! Let’s go
And view them on the Block!

FASHIONS CONSISTENT INCONSISTENCY.
THE MAID: “A penny for your thoughts, Adolphus .”
THE MAN : “I was pondering on the eternal fitness or unfitness of things. Why were the
women who accomplished the dainty minuet provided with ample skirts and crinoline,
whilst the women of to-day are fettered with yard-wide skirts, in which to dance the
extravagant Bon-ton?”
The time seems ripe to point out to D.
Jones that he is unduly cramming his
locker with the N.S.W. North Coast Co.’s
ships. Here is the recent manifest:
Steamer Oakland, foundered off Tore Ste-
phens, May, 1903; Wollumbin, wrecked
near the Bellinger River, November, 1905;
Tomki, lost near the Richmond River, Sep-
tember, 1907; Burrawong, wiped out at the
entrance to the Manning River, March,
1909; Minimbah, deceased, same place,
April, 1910. A few weeks back the Rose-
dale went missing with all hands, presum-
ably somewhere near Smoky Cape ; and now
the Macleay and all its people, bar two, is
added to the gloomy list. It looks like undue
preference, or spite, or something, on the
part of Jones.
“Handspike” sends a line from the sea: —
Nothing but gross carelessness sent the North
Coast (N.S.W.) steamer Macleay to Davy Jonesville
with 15 of her crew, last week. The vessel, although
within easy distance of a familiar lighthouse, and on
a sea-track as well known to coasting men as Pitt-
street is to bookmakers, was miles out of her course.
According to one of the two survivors, she was head-
ing landward when the rocks tore the lining out of
her so that, even if she had missed Big Cabbage Tree
Island, upon which it is supposed she struck, she
would have butted into something equally solid and
uncompromising. Her utter and complete disap-
pearance strengthens the theory that the lost Rose-
dale, another North Coast steamer, was wrecked
through Striking Fish Rock, a well-known danger
under Smoky Cape lighthouse. The idea is that
when the Rosedale ran into a heavy southerly the
skipper turned her round to head for shelter, and
found it —at the bottom of the ocean where the
stormy winds do not blow. The case of the Macleay
once more emphasises the danger of hugging the
coast, and skating round reefs and rocks to dodge a
current, and save half an hour or so. There is too
much of it on the coast, consequently there are too
many ships lost.’ Also, there are too many lives
lost, which is a matter liable to be overlooked.
Sydney lately saw some sizable transac-
tions in the real estate line. Thirteen city
properties, belonging to the Marks estate,
were submitted at auction ; seven were sold
on the spot, and the remaining six were ex-
pected to go privately; One block with a
30ft. frontage to King-street, by a depth of
only 44ft., and carrying two staka-de-oyst
shops, rented at £lOOO a year, brought
£21,000. A George-street (Ha.vmarket)
block, frontage 66ft., depth 134 ft., was
knocked down for £22,500. The Singer Co.’s
corner, which has a 30ft. frontage to Georae-
street, changed owners for £12,500. A Kent-
street block, frontage 64ft., hit up £12,000.
Altogether, £87,500 was bagged for the
seven sold properties. The daily papers
chronicled the deal with great jubilation,
and called it “evidence of Sydney’s great
growth and development.” Perhaps it was :
but f it was also evidence that the commun-
ity that allows the private landlord to
snavel the pick of its territory, and pocket
the increment in value that has been made
by the community itself, hasn’t developed its
intelligence along with its bulk.
The St. Kilda (Melb.) council is spend-
ing so much money in converting the suburb’s
marine frontage into a sixpenny show
ground that it has none to spare for
lighting the back streets. One of these days
the council will be morally responsible for a
murderous outrage in an engloomed thorough-
fare, whereupon the relatives of the victim,
if they are right-thinking people, will kill the
local mayor and bury the body under a
steam-roundabout or a switchback structure.
Or they will dress it as a pierrot and dump
it down on a garden chair in the Paradise
Pictures enclosure.
George V. has been good graciously pleased
to bestow the title “Royal” upon the Prince
Alfred Yacht Club, Sydney, which brings it
on an equal rating with the Royal Sydney
Yacht Squadron. Others to follow are the
Royal Moore Park Kick-the-Tin Club, the
Royal Surry Hills Ladies' Walking Soci-
ety, the. Royal Gregory Wade Life-Saving
Association, and the Royal Hotel Barmaids’
League.
HER POINT OF VIEW.
ELSIE : “Oh! mummy, I should so love to be a cherub, for then I could never, never be spanked."
The Sydney Master Builders had their
pleasant annual picnic at Cabarita, the other
day, and wept so much, as usual, that the
Parramatta rose 19ft. According to the S.
M. Herald :
The printed programmes prepared by the secretary,
Mr. Richards, were a masterpiece in themselves.
The frontispiece was a representation of a builder
standing in his office, apparently at his wits’ end.
Outside the office was a notice: “Wanted, masons,
bricklayers, carpenters and plasterers.” But there
was an absence of men. In the background was a
building in course of erection, but only one man at
work.
During the proceedings there was “a splen-
did repast,” of course, and, somebody having
proposed “The Day We Celebrate,” the
chairman responded:—
The builders had erected in Sydney during last
year, according to the Water Board report, 5712
buildings, at a cost of £3,286,525. But that did
not include all the other works, for the grand total of
the exoenditure in this direction amounted to over
£5,000,000 for the year, an increase of 28 per cent,
on the previous year.
And only one man on the job! There seems
to be either a very energetic workman or a
very energetic liar about somewhere.
“Brass Button”: The hoary-headed old
Orient liner, Ormuz, is on its last trip to
Australia —at any rate under the Orient
flag. The ancient ark was built in ’B6, and
has been plugging along the same old track
ever since. There are plenty of big, up-to-
date, sea sky-scrapers that will never be as
comfortable, but Speed is master nowadays,
and the liner that pants wearily into port
a few hours late has to pull out of the line.
And while Speed is the master, Size is the
mate; for about the first thing the average
man, and the first, last and middle thing
the average woman wants to know when
picking a boat for a passage, is how far up
in the thousands its tonnage runs. Most of
them have a vague notion of what is meant
by “tonnage,” but they all talk learnedly of
12,000-tonners, and sneer at anything less
than five figures. All of which is one of the
good reasons why the liner’s attics keep
climbing into the skies another storey, and
the cellar level gets further and further down
towards the place where the violent bump
is.

The Oldest Inhabitant strolls around :
An ancient building of frail material, in days
gone by the trading place and home of an old Syd-
ney identity, Richard Morton Lindsay, is to be passed
over the knackers. It is known, now, as Angus
and Robertson’s “Shilling Shop,” in Castlereagh-
street. Dick Lindsay was a colonist of the early
’3o’s. About 1840 he set up as a dealer, in Kent-
street; in the ’so’s he was in Josephson’s Terrace in
Elizabeth-street, where he was sandwiched between
the Misses Flower, who kept a ladies’ school, and
Dr. Foucart; in the early ’6o’s he moved to the shop
now about to be slain. In the mid ’6o’s he and R.
W. Skinner were the only second-hand booksellers in
Sydney, as H. T. Wright was the only one in Mel-
bourne. In the early ’so’s, Dick Lindsay went to
London, to buy a stock of moderateur lamps, a very
fashionable article then. They burned colza oil. But
there was a sudden slump in colza oil lamps, and
Dick lost a small fortune. His wife, if not a native
of Sydney, was close to it. When they were in
Oastlereagh-street, she used to point to the big school
building opposite, and tell how she had attended
Father Therry’s mass there. That was before Father
Therry built the temporary chapel—named St.
Gregory, if memory serves—on a part of the site
now devoted to St. Mary. After Lindsay’s death
the shop had many odds and ends of tenants, until,
10 years ago, when Mr. George Billing brought it
back to its old bookish occupation.
“Teetotal Maine,” as S. M. Herald
headed an account of that State’s recent
decision to try and clean up the mess that
has followed half a century of prohibition,
has been receiving some special attention
by a trio of investigating professors of the
M.L. University. A chip :
In 1891 over 40 per cent, of the committals to
country gaols in Maine were committals for drunken-
ness, and the proportion increased steadily, until in
1902 it had reached the amazing total of 60 per
cent, of the whole. In 1850, when prohibition first
came into force in Maine, the consumption of alco-
holic liquor per head of population was 4.08 gallons;
in 1910, after more than half a century of prohibi-
tion, it was over 23 gallons. In Maine we have
renewed proof that prohibition puts alcohol into the
houses of the people; so that drinking, from being
merely an occasional social indulgence, becomes a
dailv, inveterate habit.
Healing with the home life of the people
under prohibition, it is set out that: —
Maine stands absolutely and easily at the head of
the North Atlantic division of States in the matter
of divorce percentages—2B2 divorces per 100,000 of
married population, while “drunken” New York
only averages 60 divorces per 100,000 of married
population.
FROM THE PURELY SYDNEY VIEWPOINT.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
In accordance with the naval programme already
outlined, the flagship of the Australian squadron
(H.M.S. Powerful) and the rest of the warships left
Port Jackson, at the conclusion of the A.J.C. meet-
ing, for Melbourne, where the Cup festivities will
shortly begin. —News item.
The last of the parties is over,
The races and dances are done,
And so our defenders are leaving;
In fact, to be plain, they are gone:
The Flagship and fleet have departed ;
Unguarded we stand, and alone.
The pertinent question arises:
When war, the undodgable, comes,
And Sydney’s bombarded, and Horror
Through suburb and alleyway roams,
Will those whom we dread choose, for action,
A season of balls and “At Homes 7”
Assuming they don’t, our position
Appalling and hopeless must be
Who drowse, ’mid our women and children,
And savings, ’neath Pinchgut’s mild lea,
With naught between us and destruction
Save cruiserless, Dreadnoughtless sea.
Two methods of sidestepping ruin
Suggest themselves: This is the first :
Persuade our society leaders,
By way ot evading the worst,
To make social life, in this city,
One ceaseless and glorious “burst.”
With races each week-end at Randwick,
And harbor trips daily, and teas,
And motor-jaunts into the country,
And dejeuners under the trees,
And countless small dinners and dances,
(All things that the sailor-men please).
Our prospects of getting the squadron—
Grim emblem of Britain’s stark might—
To stand by our beautiful city.
And ftive the invader that fight
With which we ourselves can’t supply him,
Will look quite remarkably bright.
Another alternative offers :
To pay for a show of our own,
No tinpot affair, but a navy
Well fit for a nation full-grown—-
And man it with local-trained sailors.
Nor heed old Bruce Smith’s anguished moan.
One fact stands out clear as a lighthouse;
Whatever the ultimate cost,

“The Blotter” : In giving £15,000 for the
old X.S.W. training ship Sobraon, Australia
has paid top price and a bit over. The aged
ai-K was built at Aberdeen 45 years ago, and
wouldn't bring much more than a fifth of
the money in the open market—most likely
not more than a few hundreds for hulk pur-
poses. Where are its contemporaries, the
Tarramatta, Macquarie, Hawltesbury and
the others which flew Devitt and Moore’s
flag up at the beginning of the ’Bo’s. The
Macquarie, I think, was sold to Dagoes.
Anyhow, none of the fleet brought £15,000,
or anything near it, when D. and M. went
out of the wind-jammer business, and every-
one of the lot was over 20 years younger
than the Sobraon is now. On the British
register the Sobraon appears, or did until
two or three years ago, as owned by the
dead-and-gone Saul Samuel, for many years
N. S. Wales Agent-Beneral.
A WISE PRECAUTION.
FRIEND: “ Wha — what—what the deuce are you doing , Obese?"
OBESE: “I’ve (puff-puff) got the nomination for the State Parliament! ”
There is a clause in the Declaration of
London which will bring uproarious cneers
from fruitarians, vegetarians and other
erank-dietarians, also monkeys. The De-
claration gives, power to a warship in time of
strife to collar any vessel, neutral or other-
wise, if it is carrying foodstuffs of any sort
with the solitary exception of NUTS. Any
Bower which Britain is scrapping with will
be allowed under the new Declaration to
convert merchant ships into war vessels
without notification of any sort, and these
can immediately start out on the prowl for
every craft bound for any of Bull’s ports,
and run it ip as a prisoner if it carries any
tucker. Buf cargoes of nuts they dare not
(the writer nearly said dare nut) touch.
So we may pxpect to see all nations’ ships,
loaded down to the Plimsoll mark with what
the ape eats, serenely steering through the
enemies’ fleets and landing their cargoes, and
we can picture John Bull and Mrs. Bull, and
all the little Bulls waiting with famine-
stricken faces for the nuts to be unloaded.
When the other foodstuffs run out, as tuey
would do about 15 minutes after their im-
port was stopped, soldiers, sailors and civi-
lians will probably be served out with a
daily ration or a pound of peanuts and a
milky cocoaffut apiece, and the swaggerest
dish the Carlton and Savoy will be able to
mention on the menu will be Potage Peanut,
Brazil Nuts braised it la Charley's Aunt,
and Chestnuts (l la Punch. It’s going to be
somewhat rough on the lions and tigers in the
Zoo, though it won't be long Defore they
appear as prime cuts and joints on mil-
lionaires' tables. And the baboon with the
red nose and the blushing tail will be saved.
Some foodstuff's may be “absolute contra-
band,” and others “conditional contraband,”
but the fancy name they are captured under
won’t be much satisfaction to the poor for-
lorn beef-eater whose jaws are past service
as nutcrackers.
“J.R.”: That is a queer arrangement on
the part of the Parramatta King’s School,
whereby 14 members of the school cadet
corps are to be put up, during the Durbar
celebrations, at ua Martini6re College,
Lucknow. The Martini&re is a charity
school, run by the Catholic clergy. It was
founded by a Frenchman named Martin, who
made a great deal of money in the last half
of the 18th century, by raising and com-
manding European-disciplintd troops 'u the
service of native chiefs. A sturdy oiu anti-
Englishman was Martin—about the most
enemy of British rule in India
of his epoch. He endowed the college for
the education of children of what is known
as the “Domiciled Community,” otherwise
people of mixed blood. The White Austra-
lian parents of the King’s Scholars may
approve of their youngsters being herded
with a race which is sniffed at by both whites
and blacks in India; on the other hand, they
may not. When they appreciate the posi-
tion in its entirety, I am fain to think that
they will not.
The fate of the Lowe infant, which had its
arms cut off when crawling on the line at
Bentville, brings to light the quality of the
Victorian Railway Commissioners’ mercy.
The father of the child was a railway porter.
Avho became incapacitated by disease, and
finally L- ■ ridden, so the Department, in a
burst of benevolence, gave his wife, who has
a young family, a level crossing to look after.
Between the young family, the level crossing
and the bedridden husband, baby Lowe, as
might have been expected, escaped attention
long enough to crawl under a locomotive.
Thus, once again, the Department's efforts to
combine economy and lordly benevolence re-
sulted in disaster.

To the editor, “Sidney Bullettin.”
Sir, —In view of the facts that these antipodean
setlements styled australia is proposed to be ruled
by a gang of progenyed and branded and immortal-
ised bushrangers cuttroats whose laws are none less
that preference even in a hard day wore)! for a living
starvation and degradation ecc. ecc. to any one that
dare call himself a free worcher and refuse swallow
theirs criminal renowned slavery gospels self sty led
lawful laws ; and in view of facts that as an immi-
grant I have passed nearly a decade of experiences
out swan river way among blood suchin blood hounds
before the advent of so called unionism, 1 now hereby
in public declare: that as a free worcher I accuse
all concerned of the time vasted starvation suffered
and degradation experienced since unionism started
existence 11 years ago responsible of the above
products caused i»v theirs laws forbidding ways and
means to obtain employment and a living; that
as I want employment to earn enough to pay
a passagge 3rd class fare but from these ocean
archipelago called australia in public I accuse them
responsible of every day wasted and if I have a live
by methods others that lawfull ; that I refuse to join
these so called unionism by theirs methods for the
gift of a Continent; and that there is nothing in
Creation that shall forbid me the rights of dissemin-
ate my experiences anywhere I like and in languages
I please and hereby I disclaym all responsibilityes
from theirs effects.
Peter Rensany.
Sidney, October 11, 1911.

* * * *
Someone in Victoria's railway rolling-
stock department combines the pawkiriess of
the Scot with the thriftiness of the Dago.
Away back in Speight’s days, an old second-
class, ex-Hobson’s Bay four-wheeled dog-box,
numbered 2488, was condemned as worn-out,
and sent to the scrap heap. There, however,
a thrifty foreman noticed it, and fixed it up
for several mor-- years’ service. Its parti-
tions and seats were removed; bunks were
stuck in it, and it became a camping car-
riage for repairing gangs, till it became too
old and dry-rotted even for a service which
doesn’t involve any fast travelling. Then it.
stood for a while at Newport, till one day
another harassed foreman, looking around
for carriages to replace those that had been
smashed up, dropped his eye on it. The roof
was good and the draw-bars weren't quite
done, and with repairs to wheels, axles,
tyres, windows, sides and ends, it might
still be made to earn a bob or two. So it
was painted and furbished up afresh, the
bunks were taken down and three compart-
ments were built in, and with some of the
old doors nailed up and some of the windows
boarded over, it blossomed forth again—this
time as 57ST, which probably means
“Slung Together 57 times.’ Since then
it has carried some hundreds of week-
enders down to Mordialloc and back;
but the other half-year it became so
decrepit and so filled with senile decay that
it commenced losing its limbs on the road,
whereupon it was gradually and deliberately
brought back to Newport, empty. But there
is little hope of its resting in peace even now.
A few nails and another coat of paint, and it
may yet resurrect as 666 DF —the Dead
Finish and the number of the Beast.
* * * *
Dean Phelan (Vic.), talking of the mean-
ing of the word “flapper,” nas paved the.
way for a controversy:—
he presumed the term was taken from what hap-
pened in the case of birds. Young girls were brought:
up under the wing of a mother, and when big;
enough tried to flap their little wings. The word was.
intended, no doubt, for girls who felt they were able
to go from under mother’s wing and fly on their own.
The Dean’s information bn the subject would
sound conclusive if “flapper” were not an
old English term for a young fish of the
flounder type. The bird simile is poetic
enougfi, but the fish theory has more of
prosaic fact about it. Bred in a compara-
tively peaceful haven of refuge, the flapper
strikes out towards the troubled sea of
matrimony. In short, it goes forth to mash
a male fish and make sheep’s eyes at an-
other flounder.
LOVE AND L.S.D.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
MAT. —Gent., smart business tradesman, wishes
to meet refined, good AVoman, 35 to 40, with
means; love and a- s. d. —No. 422, Herald
Office. — Daily paper advt.
Cupid and cash go hand-in-hand ;
Gallants may scoff and girls grow haughty ;
AA T omen of sense all understand
“Business first,” when nearing forty ;
Love must wait till the hills are paid ;
Cupid flies from a dunning grocer ;
Ponder in time, O pretty maid !
Say to penniless suitors: “No, sir!”
Money is scorned at twenty-five—
Squandered gaily and labelled “dirty” ;
AVait a little till we arrive
On the shadowy slopes of thirty ;
When we’re travelling down the hill,
Then, my brothers, we’re thinking harder—
Saving up for each little bill—-
Peeping into the empty larder.
Love’s a joke without £ s. d.,
Penniless suitors cool and falter
AA’hen a damoset thoughtfully
Talks a lot of the bridal altar ;
“Mat” ’s a matter for sense and cash —
Love’s for penniless children sporty ;
Business tradesmen (if not rash)
Seek for bank accounts under forty.
X.S.W.

A RUSH ORDER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
More glorious weather could not have been de-
sired tor the garden party given yesterday afternoon,
at Federal Government House, by the Governor-
General and Lady Denman. A rather cold wind was
blowing during the early nart of the afternoon, but
fortunately this died down, and brilliant sunshine
flooded the gardens and lawns. —-Daily paper.
“Send down Thy Light! Turn off Thy Wind!”
The prayer of the Elect
AVentup. An angel, meek, resigned,
Of the celestial cable kind,
Received it with respect.
The wind was stilled ; the light came down
And lit the close-trimmed sward.
Next day the uninvited town
Read: “Catering by Bun and Brown ;
The weather by The Lord.”
N.F.W.

“Son of a Gun” : The strong suit of the
Outback Queensland Bung is his hide. Called on
one the other day, and was somewhat baffled to see
him immediately pour out and swamp a generous
potion, paying no more attention to me than if I had
been a spittoon. Then I put down sixpence. Blandly
tilling the coin, he inquired: “Ain’t you going to
’ave one yerself?”
“Dinkum”: Re “Vagabond” (B. 21/9/ 11)»
he is perfectly right in his assertions re the oil
tractor, and there are a whole lot more things that
the machine can do that he didn’t mention. lor in-
stance, it can chop up chaff and press it, pull down
trees, pull out stumps and saw them up, and kill
prickly-pear by rolling it down and then scraping it
clean, bore drains, and pull heavy loads over
soft sand or into heaps. It can drill deep
wells, and, best of all, swell the poor cocky's
bank account. “Vagabond” wants to know
why cock}' doesn’t take it on. This is what I
heard. in Charleville the other day: “I dessay them
tractors is all right, an’ can t'.o all them things, but
erbout the ’orse-breeder, ’ow will ’e git erlong? An
wot erbout ther bloke what breeds th’ worlcin’ bul-
licks? S’posin’ we takes them things on, wot’s ’e
goin’ ter do fer a outlet fer ’is biz? An’ if they
git them things up ’ere, there’ll on’y be ’arf ther
carriers on ther road, an’ then wot erbout ther pub,
an’ wot erbout the bloke wot makes ther ’arness, an
ther cocky growin’ feed fer ther ’orses an’ bullicks,
an ’er lot of other blokes wot gits ther livin’ w ere
’orses is erbout? Even s’posin’ ther oil pots* can go
50 miles in er day with a load,, an’ can.save money
orl roun’ th’ s’lection, th’ cocky’ll still stick to is
ole ’orse team, cause ’e reckons ’ p cun make er bob
er two buyin’ an’ sellin’ ’orses, which ’.e can’t if ’e’s
dealin’ in tractor coffee pots. An’ don’t you fergit
the Carriers’ Union, neither.’’ I didn’t wait to hear
any more.
“What”: There are several reasons why
the Australian farmer does not rush the motor-tractor
as “Vagabond” (B. 21/9/’ll) suggested he should.
One is the expert care and attention required. What
does the average farmer understand about sparking-
plugs, tappets, valves, coils, magnetos, caburetters or
vaporisers, not to mention compression, lubricating
systems, cycles of action, carbonisation, valve grind-
ing, etc. ? When motors have reached the almost
perfectly simple stage, so that their action can be
readily understood from drawings or booklets, and
when a person of average intelligence can peruse
such booklet and make himself familiar with all
the mechanical functions and possibible difficulties,
then, 'and not before, will the motor-tractor come
into general farm usage.
“Angora”: I agree with “Vagabond” (B.
21/9/’ll) that the motor will do most of the farm-
ing jobs with which he credits it; but not any two
of them at the same time. A horse team, on the
other hand, can be split up, one section ploughing
while another does harrowing. And I’d be charmed
to point this out. Admitting that an engine costs
only about the same as a horse team, the owner of
the team breeds it at a fraction of its market value,
gets three or four years’ work out of the animals,
and can then sell them for £4O, £5O or £6O a
head, Also, he gets his horse feed at first cost,
whereas benzine has to be purchased after various
Trusts and Combines have had their whack
out of it. We all know the amateur farmer who
crowds his place with alleged labor-saving appliances.
If I were a machinery agent I’d give three cheers
for him, but I’m not. I’m merely a plain bloke
who refuses to pour his good profits into bad patents,
or to spend his time telegraphing for experts when
the engines go on strike without notice or explana-
tion. “Vagabond” can have my share of a tractor
that I have long ago counted as a dead horse.
The live horse is good enough for me.

“Boronia” : I hereby apply for the imme-
diate extermination of “White Ants” (B. 31/8/’ll),
on the ground that he is a disseminator of grotesque
taradiddles. It is true that most of the Westralian
wheat country is experiencing a bit of a dry spell,
but to speak of- that dryness as a drought,-, in the
sense in which the eastern States use the word, is
absurd. He alleges that the Government pushes
settlers out over, the thirsty horizon, so as not to
tread on the corns of the coastal landlords. The
fact is quite the reverse. Mitchell, Wilson, Gregory
and Co. grow vociferous in urging intending settlers
to select in the south-west division, where there is an
abundance of excellent soil, possessing a certain rain-
fall of from 30 to 40 inches a year, and where even
the smallest creeks run until about Christmas time;
but the bogey is the heavy clearing, which costs from
£l5 to £4O per acre. Consequently , many are
scared off, only to be attracted by the light clearing
of the so-called wheat lands.

“Geofred”: “Warradulla” (B. 21/9/’ll)
enters the wild, unboiled cat for the; “hardest-beast-
to-kill” stakes. Now, although he is on the right
track, his pussy only runs second to the gentleman
pig and his pigess. I had such an experience as
that mentioned by “Dulla” near Goulburn (N.S.W.),
a few years ago, except that my cat did eventually
lose its life, by a rifle bullet. We wanted our trap,
and, as the inmate disputed possession, we had to
shoot first, and then amputate the cat to re-
gain our trap. But, re the pig, his pigess,
and the family. Biking between Adavale
and Listowel, the other summer, I went to
sleep one night (to
quote “James Panni-
kin”) “under an open
expanse of glorious
Blue sky.” About 4
in the morning I
heard a spacious grunt
at my left ear, and
quickly sat up. There,
trying to introduce his
missus and kids (about
six) to me, Was the
great male pig. I
snatched my rifle and
fired blank in the ani-
mal’s . frank, open
countenahce. The un-
earthly grunt and
screech that followed
were' worse than Wood
being suspended by
Speaker Willis, but the
swine didn’t seem
much hurt. I fired
again as he galloped
off, and he made the
same remark as be-
fore. That day, after
riding about three
miles, I came across
pig again. He was
lying in a clump of bushes off the
road, stone dead, and ma pig and the
kids were holding an inquest. I dis-
mounted, and, after a fierce round or
two with ma, I frightened the mourn-
ers off and had a look at father’s
body. There were two bullet holes in
him, ne fair between his eyes (from
the front). The othe 1 bullet had entered his back,
furrowed down towards the heal, and then suddenly
slanted in, straight to tin heart. I reckon that pig
was a tough proposition. As far as I can make out
his corpse ran three miles after being killed twice.
“Mallacoota” : The idea of keeping to the
aboriginal nomenclature for names of places in this
country should be encouraged. Some of the most
euphonious native names have indecent meanings,
but few people are well up in native dialect, so that
doesn’t matter much. The Australian is weak on
geographical terms. Within a short radius of
where this is written are The Spotted Dog, Dead
Finish, John Bull, Ag’s Beacon, two Little Rivers,
two Double Creeks, Smellie’s Inlet, Cape Horn,
Dysentery Creek, Tinpot, Ti-tree Point, Horse Island,
and, a score of other equally inelegantly-named
places, all—or nearly all—-of which have original
names, spilling metrical vowels from every syllable.
“J.G.” : If we’re going to start a competi-
tion as to the hardest beast or animal to kill, I’m
round early with an Australian eagle, and anybody
can have my share of the wild eat. I had a para-
graph in this page some years ago about an eagle,
which, after having its head almost shot away, and
being nailed up as a dead bird, was found to be
alive a fortnight later. That was a true bill.
Here’s another. A cow cocky on the north Vic.
plains caught an eagle in a strong steel trap, set for
wild cats, and proceeded to kill it. He started the
operation with the jawbone of a gum, and succeeded
in making several dints on the bird’s head. The
next weapon brought to bear was a pitchfork, upon
which the bird or fiend was impaled, and the cocky
then proceeded to batter its (the fiend’s) skull to
pulp. This took 20 minutes, and the language of
the parties to the argument was like to a debating
society in Sodom and Gomorrah. Finally, a
feathered corpse was thrown out, and immediately
upon striking the ground, gave the lie to the state-
ment that the age of miracles has passed, by rising
On the wing and Hying in the face of the setting
sun. There need be no argument about this. I saw
it.
“Off-Sider,” re the home life of turkeys:—
I take it that “Bert P.” (B. 20/7/’ll) speaks from
hearsay concerning the habits of scrub-turkeys.
They do not “choose a spot free from sticks and
stones.” On the contrary, tlieir nests are mostly
built against logs and trees. The. same nest is used
by these fowl year after year. I have known one
to be in constant use. for 20 years, and it is now
30ft. in diameter and Bft. high. Every year, about
September, the birds scratch all the rotten leaves
out of the centre of the nest, and fill up with new
ones. I have watched . them . building, and they
neither face the nest nor use their wings to shift the
leaves—all the work is done with their feet. Do
not know how many use a nest, but. I have seen 60
eggs in one. As the chick can fly as so<sn as hatched,
I don’t think it would be much use ftp enclose the
nest with wire-netting, unless you put a roof over
it. Turkeys do a lot of damage to green .bananas.
In the afternoon the banana is there. At dusk the
turkey is there too. By dark the banana is gone.
Presently the turkey leaves, and then there only re-
main two holes in the atmosphere.
“Loco Driver : Goats are the hardest
animals to run over. Horses are the easiest, except,
perhaps, humans. Goats invariably dart at right-
angles to the rails, after eating the rust off the
metals till the last possible moment. A horse will
run between the rails for miles if not caught. He
seems afraid of the rails. Thinks they are shafts,
maybe. But we don’t run over a horse if we can
help it. The results are broken under-gear, even de-
railment, to say nothing of ballast amongst the
“motions,” and a horrid mess generally. Early in
my railway career we ran over a goat, aged about
one hour. He had been born between the rails, and
his mother watched by. We came along. Mother’s
frantic goat appeals to her little one were of no
avail. She stepped aside as we rushed by,
giving us a look that clearly, meant “You
fiends.”' I have never forgotten that look.
We returned an hour later. Mother goat had coaxed
her little one outside the rails. I have missed men
by a hairbreadth, saying, “You d—d fool!” as I
passed. Didn’t feel that way about the goat when
it cut things fine trying to save its kid. It wag too
pathetic for words, obscene or otherwise.
THE TOWN OF GOD-FORGOTTEN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
God-forgotten (300 miles west of the insanely-
congested port of Sydney), where the news comes
through that no trucks are available to carry the
grain to the mad anthill on the vast stretch of
coast.— Bulletin, 21/9/’ll.
There’s a town I know that slumbers in a sort of
dumb despair,
Where the Chow who grows cucumbers cries
“Whaffor?” within his lair!
For the Christian and the heathen hear the Door of
Progress slam,
Whilst in cities large and seethin’ no one cares a
single damn!
In that hamlet things are lifeless, and no mills and
foundries roar
In that village calm and strifeless, which in sleep
exclaims “Whaffor?”
’Tis the town of God-forgotten,
Where all things are slumber-sotten,
And the jackass on the gable seems to groan the
word “Whaffor?”
There’s a town out west where farmers curse the use-
lessness of farms ;
Where the mortgage leaps and clamors and the in-
terest-bill alarms ;
There the people, tired and scopeless, seem to wait
the Judgment Day—
They are. jaded, sad and hopeless, so they drift the
time away!
There they do not drain the flagon, for the pub is
tired as well;
And the wheat rots on the waggon, and all things
resemble Hell!
’Tis the town of No-One-Knows-It,
Where no hearty voice says “Prosit!”
Where the landscape quakes in mirage, and all things
resemble Hell 1
There’s a town out there where Sunday seems the
ghost of Saturday,
And where Thursday looks like Monday, and all
earthly things decay!
’Tis a place where weary Woman cooks the meals
for shabby Man —
Yea, a place accurst, inhuman, and it lives beneath
a ban. j
There the children sit reflective, tired and vimless
on the floor ;
And the earth with voice collective seems to ask the
stars “Whaffor?”
That’s the town of God-forgotten,
Where the heart of things is rotten,
And the universal spirit seems to shout aloud
“Whaffor?”
There’s; a town where nothing alters, where the world
looks dull and mean —
Where the Younger Manhood falters as it dreams of
Might-Have-Been !
There the girls each year grow older, but the mar-
riage-trade is done;
And the church roofs sag and moulder, and there is
not any fun !
One by one the young men, drifting, leave that
broken town behind—
’Tis the Younger Manhood shifting with a vexed,
uneasy mind !
From the town of Given-Over
Drifts the hopeless, : jobless lover,
And the devil Disappointment stokes Gehenna in his
mind !
There’s a town that groans and ponders as it leans
beside the plough—
“Jones and Smith both promised wonders, but will
Smith Do Something now?”
Thus the elders sadly question, But no answer echoes
there;
For the Party of Congestion holds its member by the
hair !
Jones -and Smith in dull succession walk the legisla-
tive floor—
Yea, they maunder through each session whilst elec-
tors groan “Whaffor?”
In the town of God-forgotten,
Jones and Smith seem sloth-besotten.
And the man behind the wheat stack asks his per-
ished grain “Whaffor?”
There’s a town where Death is weary, where the
tombstones seldom rise—
And the undertakers query, “Tell to us why no one
dies?”
There the hearse begins to crumble, and the plumes
droop in despair ;
Whilst the coffin-makers grumble and accuse the
healthy air !
“Somethin’s Wrong,” they groan together—which,
indeed, is very true ;
But the blame is tied a-tether with the Great Con-
gestion Crew!
’Tis the distant Trafßc-Fakir
Who destroys the coffin-maker —
All are tied in dreary bondage to the Great Conges-
tion Crew!
There’s a town —ah, well, it slumbers by its wheat
stacks in despair;
And the Chow who vends cucumbers yells “Whaffor?”
each summer there!
Nothing moves and nothing matters—Sydney’s deaf
and far away ;
So the population scatters, and the pleasant girls
grow grey!
In that village Love seems worthless, and each
woman at her door
Hears the landscape’s echo mirthless to her heart’s
refrain: “Whaffor ?”
Joyless town of God-forgotten —
What a crime is here begotten,
When the women’s hearts are broken, and their dead
hopes cry “Whaffor?”

“Mallacoota” on noises of the farm : Bush
poets and other irresponsible persons oyster to the
idea that all noise is confined to the lanes of cities.
An Australian farm in full blast can keep its end up
for discord. I put in a few weeks on a selection re-
cently. The “cocky” family consisted of Pa and Ma
(a little brown woman who went about from dawn
to dark snapping at everybody’s heels), Bill, George
and Andrew (whose total length—laid end to end—
was 18ft. 94in.), and Sis, Liz., and Vic. The work
of the farm began at 4.30 a.m., with the loud chop-
ping of wood, the hoarse crowing of roosters and the
quacking of the duckery. Then came the cracking
of whips and the yelping of the oblong mongrels, who
assisted to get the cows into the milking-yard.
Ferocious bellows from milch ma’s in the bail to
their offspring, and the long, pathetic, heart-rending
replies from segregated calves, lamenting generally for
mother, kept the sound-waves vibrating wildly. The
sharp, petulant voices of the two younger girls
scolding Strawberry and Jane for slapping unclean
tails across their faces, or getting their feet in the
milk buckets, broke the metre of the pastoral, and
introduced emphasis. The feeding of the pigs fol-
lowed. This was Andrew’s function. He had a voice
like a Swiss mountaineer’s in everything but sweet-
ness. He would stand at the fence, with a sour-milk
pail in either hand, and call till the wild boar in
the mountain, two miles away, heard and snuffled for
the pots of civilised hogdom. The pigs, with grunts
and snorts, came coarsely from all quarters. Andrew
killed the pigs when necessary. Theatres being far
away, he prolonged the amusement as much as pos-
sible. Breakfast over, the old man would wave his
whiskers at the paling gate of the horse paddock, and
roar back until his saddle-horse came to him. Then
would arise the voice of George summoning his
cattle-dog with shrill whistles and curseful threats.
Sis would go to feed the ducks, and the poultry-
yard was summoned, by distinctive calls and cries,
loud and generally inharmonious. On the whole,
there is a good deal of raucous vulgar row about
mornings on the farm.
THE ONE INFALLIBLE SIGN.
AAGLER (instinctively) : "Something tells me that there are fish about here!"
“Pick” : I have arrived at the conclusion
that there is a little of human nature in bush birds.
Poverty tames them speedily, but in affluence they
hold aloof. When I first pitched my gunyah on the
landscape, a magpie family, without encumbrance,
held a homestead area in a neighboring spotted gum.
They regarded me as an intruder upon their grub
run, and asserted their pre-emptive rights by throw-
ing off slurs and challenging me to fight. Early in
winter a hoodlum murderer, out with a gun, fired at
Margaret's husband, and shot away half of his upper
bill. For some days I observed the poor fellow using
the lower portion of his mutilated frontispiece like a
shovel, in vain endeavors to scoop in worms that
wriggled aside and rolled away, laughing heartily at
his discomfiture. Then one frosty morning he perched
on my ridge-pole, looking sideways hungrily at my
ration wallaby, and appealed to me to help him
through the hard times. I offered him some of my
steak on the end of a fishing-rod, and we became
friends. Later on he fed plentifully out of my hand.
The truncated beak gradually grew, and when long
enough to assure independence, he returned to his
selection in the gum-tree. When nesting season came
round again he knocked off my hat as I passed under-
neath, and otherwise displayed the aggressiveness of
old.
“Mallarapoonie” : Unlike the forests of the
other States, Westralia’s karri and jarrah country-
carries little or no undergrowth. This is accounted
for by the fact that both the Westralian brands of
vegetable contain deleterious acids in their sap. Yet,
though it has no growth except Westralia’s tall
whiskers, an imposing sight is the “King” karri
country of the Leeuwin Peninsula. Vistas of such
real magnificence, as may be seen but once on a con-
tinent, unfold to the eye where the giant karris rear
their straight, bluish-white columnar trunks a clear
100 ft. above the fallen leaves and lichens. Aisles of
such a cathedral as man has not yet dreamed of are
to be wandered through for miles in these sou’-western
forests; but, alas, these cathedral columns, coated
in smooth white bark, as clean and clear as any
polished marble, are in great demand by men, and
the ring of axes and the scream of steam-driven saws
are loud in the Land of the Leeuwin. Here some of the
longest piles in the world are won by foresters from
the jarrah belts. Jarrah, the karri’s twin brother, is
one of the most demanded of timbers on earth. Because
it is termite-proof, the railway builders of some parts
will have nothing else for sleepers. Also, it is one
of the rare timbers of the world which resist —that is
to say diddle —the bugs in the waters of the tropic
seas. (Toredos is, I believe, the proper scientific
term, but we’ll let it go at saltwater bugs.) Hence
it is in demand also for piles. Age and dampness
only serve to toughen it, and weathered jarrah is
another word for adamant. Westralia supplies the
world with this particular brand of hardwood —
sleepers for the Cape to Cairo railway; bridge girders
for Argentina ; paving blocks for promiscuous places
like Liverpool, Auckland, and Buda Pesth. Karri is
more used for building construction, as it suffers
from cold feet if left out in the wet, though it is a
tough and accommodating timber if shown a little
consideration. In their sawn state the twin brothers
are even more alike than when they stand in the
forest, and it takes an expert to distinguish between
them. Owing to this peculiarity, some low-down
practices have ocaasionally been indulged in by
Westralian timber exporters, who have “rung- in”
karri for its much more serviceable relation. A
rough-and-ready method of distinguishing the sawn
timbers is to take a splinter of either, and hold it
over a lighted match. Burnt karri leaves a little
white ash ; jarrah gives none. In this respect jarrah
is unique—it is absolutely ashless. Let The Bulle-
tin keep its matches handy.
“T.V.T.” : For deep-rooting I’ll back the
swamp-reed, or wild sugar-cane, as it is sometimes
called, that flourishes in Carrum, Koo-wee-rup and
Moe swamps (Vic.), against all nominations. It is
no uncommon occurrence when well-sinking, to find
the toenails of the plant 12ft. or more below the
surface. The rest of the body generally pushes itself
skyward for about 7ft., and anything in the way is
fatally stabbed. Frequently I have seen potatoes, ti-
tree roots and sheets of thick messmate bark
pierced by the reed in a determined manner, which
left no doubt in my mind that the armor-plate of a
Dreadnought, or even the hide of a Tory editor
would be similarly drilled through.
“Willis Wilga”: Where has “Pick” (B.
21/9/’ll) seen his lady emu and her friends? He
speaks about “the usual family of four.” Perhaps
his lady belongs to the fashionable class, and large
families are a hindrance to her social duties. Out
West it is quite common to see her with a family of
any number up to 13. Saw one a few days ago with
this collection. She did not stop to explain; but
probably she, in addition to rearing her own family,
was acting as a wet nurse to “Pick’s” lady.
