“Malbon” writes: “Red Page” (B.
14/9/’ll) says, in connection with S. P. Hyatt’s
“Off the Main Track”: “He came to grief when he
rode into the bar of the Grand Hotel, and insisted
on being served on horseback.” Can’t see why he
should have come to grief unless he insisted on being
served many times. Remember Croydon (N.Q.) in the
halcyon days, when my old friend Tabby (that’s not
his name exactly, but all his contemporaries will
recognise him by it) was Mayor of the city. One
day his Worship instructed one of his minions to
proceed, under a by-law, against a citizen for riding
his horse on the footpath, said citizen being haled up
before the beak and fined 10 bob without the option.
Next morning the injured victim turned the corner
of Sircom-street, and beheld with wild and unholy
joy that Tabby was seated on his charger in Joe
Temple’s bar, whence he had gone to buy a packet
of cigarettes. “Ah!” said the despoiled, “now for
r-r-r-revenge! ” He took up his stand opposite the
front door to await Tabby’s exit, in order to collect
evidence against him of committing the same offence
that had cost the aggrieved half a jim, even if he had
to stop there till 11 p.m., when, according to a
misty and unauthenticated legend, the pubs closed.
After the First Magistrate of the City had lit his
sixth glass, he became aware of Nemesis on the side-
walk. Did he blanch and mutter in his beered?
Not by a pewter full! He hauled his fancy Mazeppa
round, and rode out of the bar into the dining-room
and down the backstairs, guiding his faithful Arab
steed through the side gate, and out into the street
over the authorised crossing. Then the weary watcher
on the corner ground his new set of false teeth into
powder, and his disgusted silence was so loud that
he was arrested for making two separate disturb-
ances.
“Mallarapoonie”: Given due warning of
the shark’s apjmoach, the average black is more than
a match for it in shallow water. The game is to keep
below the shark and jab it in the waistcoat with
a sharp stick; and so long as the nigger can reach
the floor of the sea, the defeat of the shark is pretty
certain. In deep water Binghi is at a disadvantage.
By endeavoring to keep below the shark he occasion-
ally gets so deep that he hasn’t time to come up
alive. One vagary of the marine jackal is its pro-
pensity to fool round instead of attending strictly to
business. Out of a shipload of mares consigned to
India, I once saw a splendid animal which had died,
consigned to the sharks in Townsville Harbor —
one of the worst infested places on the coast. The
dead body of that mare, nosed continually by raven-
ous sharks, floated for days untouched. Again, I
have seen the Kimberley cattle-shippers drop dead
animals overboard in shark-infested waters off the
Westralian coast, and invariably the defunct beef
floated for days. On the other hand, during the
process of unloading a shipment of nor’-west cattle
at Robb’s jetty lately, a fine bullock leapt over-
board, and was torn to pieces by five huge sea-
wolves within a chain of the shore.
“Ivoreki”: If that snake-infested locality
discussion has not had the lid closed, and if Papua
isn’t outside the limit, I nominate Kanosia, near
Port Moresby, for the snake-skin belt. Snakes, par-
ticularly death-adders, are so numerous there that
they almost emptied the treasury of the Rubber Co.
that owns the property. The eo. used to pay 3d.
per head for reptiles, and in 12 months over 3800
were accounted for. Farther inland, the rock-
python is fairly numerous, and I have seen speci-
mens of 22ft. or thereabouts frequently. When one
is captured it is Christinas in that village, and the
odor of cooked snake lingers in the atmosphere for
a day or so.
“J.S.N.”: Reckon my old sheepdog was
the last word in animal intelligence. Dunno who
bred him; he came to the hut one day, footsore, and
looking as if he had deserted a sinking drover, and I
took him in. I suppose he felt like asking for a
job, for, when I got up the next morning, he had the
killing sheep in the yard. After that, for a while,
I used to rattle the knife on the steel when I wanted
them brought up, but he soon got to know the kill-
ing days himself, and would always have them ready,
lie learned to close the gate, too. He would nose it
over till it closed, and then put the peg in with his
teeth. One day the peg was missing, and he stuck
his tail in the hole to keep the gate closed till I
came down and made a new peg. I was teaching
him to play crib when he died.
THE WRONG HALF.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Jim came into the Mission House,
His nose was badly cut,
Beneath his eye there lurked a mouse,
A red and angry rut
Was rippling down his cheek and chin.
Now Jim had been from beer and sin
By Brother Smith —whom Satan hated—
Most wonderfully rescued, and then quite re-
generated.
“James!” cried the shocked Evangelist,
“Oh! what is that I see ?
Again you’ve raised your horny fist
In horrid enmity.
We thought at home must grace accrue,
And bless your better half and you.”
“If that’s your game,” then James asserted,
“You’ve made a bloomin’ error — it’s the wrong
half you’ve converted!”
VICTORIA.
S.S.
Papua Wants to Stretch.
From a Papuan correspondent:—
When do Australian statesmen, who profess so
much admiration for citizen liberties, and particu-
larly for the franchise, propose to re-frame the Con-
stitution they should have been ashamed to fasten
upon Papua, the Commonwealth’s first colony? As
thing's are now, the territory is governed by an Exe-
cutive and Legislative Council, composed of six
official and three non-official members. The latter
are nominated, which means they are chosen, by the
Lieutenant-Governor, so that thfi white population
is disfranchised altogether. Further, we are deprived
of trial by jury, except on a capital charge. Even
then, only four jurymen are empanelled. When a
bunch of Senators visited the Possession recently,
these, and. many other unfairnesses, were explained
to them by the whites, and a demand made for re-
dress, though*- what will come of it I do not pretend
to know. But I do know that whatever is done
should be done quickly. The worst form of govern-
ment known to history is despotic government, and
by the sole powers of promotion or dismissal, which
the Lieutenant-Governor holds over his executive
officers, he is practically a despot.
The white population, taxed up to £46 a head,
as shown by the Papuan Estimates for 1911, have no
voice in the expenditure of the revenue, which pro-
bably accounts for the fact that so little of the money
comes their way. The Commonwealth Minister for
External Affairs said, some time back, that “Papua
was governed primarily for the natives.” I believe
him. On the year’s estimates for the Chief Secre-
tary’s Department and the Public Works Department,
£23,467 was set down to provide black brother with
some of the benefits of civilisation. For medical
attendance a sum of £6578 was granted, of which
only £440 will go to the European hospital. Mean-
while, the white man, when he is sick, has to pay
very heavy medical charges to private doctors, or go
into the hospital and pay £4 4s. a week for the
privilege. As a matter of fact, the hospital is
simply a private institution, subsidised by the Gov-
ernment from the taxes raised out of the white popu-
lation. And after paying the taxes, the white popu-
lation (which is about 1000 men, women and
children) are bled again when ill-health drives them
into their own hospital. Could anything be more
atrocious ?
Another Papuan requirement is a preferential
tariff with Australia, in order to save the growing
trade to Brisbane and Sydney. At present, tropical
suits, costing 30s. in Australia, can be landed in the
territory from Singapore for 10s. 6d., plus the Papuan
duty. Same thing applies to furniture and other
goods, and if the Commonwealth doesn’t take some
steps to prevent it, the Papuan trade will go to the
North ; and once it is lost, it will not be recovered
for a long, long time.
A rebate should be arranged, too, between Australia
and Papua upon produce grown in the Possession.
At present, prohibitions and heavy duties are choking
enterprise upon the soil. Take, for instance, the
start made by some people to cultivate coffee.
Thousands of pounds had been sunk in that industry
when the Commonwealth put on a crushing duty, and
the scheme dropped dead in its tracks. The position
is, that there is a double tax on all goods coming
into the Possession from Australia, and a prohibitive
tariff waiting for the goods we would like to send
into Australia. I don’t know how you expect your
baby to grow on the diet. We ought also to have
wireless installations at each of the three principal
ports, and a better mail service. In existing circum-
stances Papua is farther away from Australia than
Siberia. There are a few other little requirements,
such as an improved Civil Service, but I’ll pass them
over just now. If the other grounds for complaint
are removed, I’ll be satisfied for a week or two. So
would every other white person in the Possession.

I 1 rank Whysall, who goes up to the head
ot the Post and Telegraph service in Wes-
tralia, has been most things in the N. S.
Wales department, from telegraph messen-
ger to acting superintendent of mails Be-
tween times, he was head of the telephone
branch, and left about i>st. behind him when
he was sent on up to the Mails ; for to Why-
sall his work is the most serious thing in the
world, and a contrivance like a telephone,
which, so far, is a makeshift at the best, is
Gehenna to a man like that.
It seems a pity that the new Westralian
Premier is named Scaddan—John Scaddan
It is suggestive of Salem Scudder, of “The
Octoroon.” There are some names which
always sound comic or undignified—names
like Snooks, Bloggs, ldogsflesh, Smike, Job-
son, AVhistleshaft, Rabbit, Diggory, Dodery,
Higgles, Sloper, Short, Scully and a multi-
tude besides. Scaddan is better than most
of these, yet it is a misfortune.

Colonel Airey, the small, hard-bitten,
lightweight fighting-man whom several
generations of Australian warriors knew so
well, has just died in S’Africa. He was born
in Yorkshire, of military stock, in 1844.
First saw service on the N.W. frontier of
India. Came to Australia in 1866, and in
1879 joined the R.A.A. In 1885 he went
with the local contingent to the Soudan.
Next year, war having broken out in Bur-
mah, he took his sword there, served during
1886-7, was wounded, received the thanks of
the Governor-General, and was mentioned in
despatches. Returning to Australia, he ex-
pended his surplus stock of energy and cour-
age in the hunting field. No one ever fol-
lowed the Sydney Drag with more dash and
skill than Airey. Pie was Master for some
years, and those were the palmiest days of
that somewhat wilted body. When the Boer
war broke out he was given command of the
Ist Regiment of Australian Bushmen, and
served till 1901. He was a D.S.O. and a
C.M.G., and owned enough medals, bars,
ribbons and things to sink a dinghy.
From the office psychologist:—
Walter Hall, whose memory the S. M. Herald
went near to making ridiculous, came of poor parents
and had known poverty himself. Amongst ..is friends
were men who had dropped from the status of mil-
lionaires, or thereabouts, to genteel indigence—-
wholly the result of being what Hall was not. Like
so many of our rich men, he lacked the capacity for
life. Material comfort didn't mean much to him.
He found the smoking-room of an hotel or a club as
pleasing as the most luxurious resort that the mind
of man could conceive. Whisky tasted as good to him
as the rarest of the world’s vintages. Until quite
lately, when he bought a motor-car, he got about the
city—when he did not do it on foot—in any old cab
that came hand}'. In short, with an income that
would have allowed him the best that the world had
co offer, he possessed the tastes of the average bank
clerk or floor-walker.
Being uncultivated mentally—another respect in
which he resembled 9!H per cent, of our rich men
—the thought of buying pictures or books on a big
scale, or patronising local art or literature, or
endowing a University, or helping in the beautifica-
tion of the city he inhabited, never occurred to him.
On occasion, he would seize his cheque-book and
spend money more or less freely, according to his
lights. The trouble was, his lights were so dim.
Like the vast majority of his class, he lacked the
capacity for independent thought on nearly every
question unconnected with business. This type
thinks, socially and politically, according to formulae
supplied by the nevVspaoers. No other theory
serves to explain and condone such mammoth exam-
ples of misdirected lavishness as Walter Hall’s gift
of £lO,OOO to the imbecile Dreadnought subscrip-
tion fund.
His case suggests, yet again, that the millionaire
is one of the things they still do better in Britain.
By the death of Norman Selfe, the Ma
State has lost an eminent engineer and a
good citizen. _ lie came to Sydney, with his
father, in 1855, at the age of 15 years, and
was at once articled to the engineering firm
of P. N. Russell and Co. lie pushed on
fast, and for seYeral years before the
partnership was dissolved was responsible
lor all the engineering work carried out by
the firm. After that, he was the superin-
tending engineer to two shale and oil cos.
Then, under engagement to the Gas Co., he
erected the new head station, and designed
several of the prominent buildings still
standing. lie left the Gas Co. to become
chief draftsman and engineer to Mort’s
Dock, and when, with the help of the late
T. S. Mort, lie started business on his own
account, he visited 70 cities in Kurope and
the United States, and made himself ac-
quainted with the world’s great engineer-
ing works. During his long career, he de-
signed the hulls or machinery or both, for
over 40 steamers, including the Australian
torpedo vessels; the double-ended screw 7
ferryboats were introduced by him to Syd-
ney Harbor, and he produced the design for
the North Shore bridge which won the
Government’s prize, and is shortly to be con-
sidered by the Works Committee.
“B de D” counts up the other Debretters :
Australia is always bumping against a baronet or
something, and begging his pardon, and telling him
it didn’t know he was there. It bumped like that
against John Bonnet Lawes-Witteronge the other
morning, and .John bumped against the baronetcy in
an equally casual way. He is a tutor, and was loaf-
ing at Brighton (Vic.) when somebody told him of
the death of his father, an eminent artist and athlete.
The new baronetess was a Miss Cox ; her mother lives
at Mosman (N.S.W.). 11. O’Brien Blake, who died
a few days, back, was also one of us. He was the
second son of the 13th Blake baronet, of Galway.
After a career of adventure, such as befits second
sons, he settled as a cockv in the Wirnmera, and
made some money. There is, or was, another Blake
in, Vic. —Louisa, daughter of the 13th baronet, who
in 1862 married Thomas Ulick Burke, of Smythes-
dale, then a roaring mining camp. He died in ’67,
and his widow married John King. Then there is
Sir Henry Castres Kellett, of liew, who was last
week made a J.P. —the only baronet J.P. on the Vic-
torian list, though there is a count there—the
amiable Von Horn, once of Germany, but now tilling
the soil in the Wodonga district. " The heir to an-
other baronetcy was committed for trial in Westralia
a while ago charged with improner behavior towards
a girl; but though the girl’s mother was gib-
beted in the papers, I never saw any mention of what
happened to the gentleman. Perhaps the papers were
too awed to tell.
Ilev. E. S. Hughes, the
tall High Church parson of
St. Peter’s, Melbourne, has
been elected a canon of St.
Paul’s Cathedral. Hughes
thought he had reached the
end of his career when, a
few years ago, an attack of
influenza left him quite
blind. However, he retired
hopefully to Medlow, in the
Blue Mountains, and there,
after a restful interval, he
gradually got back his sight.
Hughes, clear-eyed and alert
as ever, is never likely to
forget that dark time.
Reminiscences by “0.1 v.”
John Bennett Lawes-Wittewronge, the Melbourne
resident who inherited a baronetcy the other day,
ought to be something of an expert in agriculture.
His grandfather, the original John Bennett Lawes,
was so pre-eminently the first of scientific farmers
in poor old England that the Government bart.-ed
him for his laboratory labors. Within 25 miles of
London he established the model farm that is now
managed by an incorporated society. It was J. B.
Lawes’s first-born, just dead, who tacked Wittewronge
to his honorable family name. He was the champion
amateur oarsman and athlete of his young days, and
his misguided ambition in life was to become a
famous sculptor. He did achieve celebrity, not for
his artistic work, butfor his costly feud with a suc-
cessful rival —one Richard Belt. Belt, it was said,
took credit for some rather fine bits of sculpture
which had been performed by “ghosts” in his employ.
He was a “self-made man” with a good business head
and an unscrupulous talent for self-advertisement.
The high-toned members of the cult hated Belt, and
Charles Lawes called him a blighted and wormy im-
postor, or words to that effect. Result, a terribly
expensive libel suit —Belt v. Lawes, which ended in
a verdict for the plaintiff. Indirectly, it ruined
Belt. A few years later, his old enemies got to hear
of him taking somebody down in a jewellery trans-
action. The “mug” was incited to charge Belt with
fraud, and he came to grief in the Criminal
Court. Charles Lawes, plus Wittewronge, figured of
late years as President of the Society of British
Sculptors, an empty, but sounding distinction. His
only son, the new baronet, has been a rolling-stone
seemingly, and there is no mention of any family
estates that he has succeeded to.
Beknighted Findlay, M.L.C. and Attor-
ney-General, has decided to resign from the
Maoriland Leg. Council and seek election to
the Reps. Nine years ago, he was induced
by Richard Seddon to contest a Wellington
seat at short notice, and was turned down.
This time he will hunt the elusive vote in
Parnell (Auckland). If lie doesn't win the
seat he says he will retire from public life,
which is obviously all that will be left for
him to do. Findlay is one of M.L.’s native-
born clever men, and rose from a poor clerk-
ship to his present position without any aid
but his own brain. But what he gains in
brnin he lacks in strength of character, and,
to-day, witn the exception of a few fancy
side-lines, is little more than a fluent bar-
racker for lou Ward and his policy of “lend’s
a quid or two. - ’ His ambition is abnormal,
and he doesn’t know how to get there, which
was the case of the brilliant dog that tried
to climb a tree.
Walter Russell Hall, who died in Sydney
last week, will probably be found to
have been worth uist about as much as the
late Samuel Horaern. Mount Morgan shares
are not what they were a few months back ;
out, even so, the Hall estate will most likely
pan out well over three millions. In the T>o’s
and Ws, the deceased was one of three who
ran Cobh and Co.’s business in N. S. Wales
and Queensland, and that took him on to
the squatting business ; but his big rise came
through Mount Morgan, out of which he
must have made, one way and another, much
over a million. He wasn’t profligate; and
he had no children to he profligate, either.
Kelso King, of the Mercantile Mutual In-
surance Co., helped him to nurse his money;
and with pastoral investments, and as much
Sydney city property as was in any one
man’s hands, the fortune rolled along. The
company-promoter of recent years didn’t get
much out of him, except brief and sharp-
pointed remarks, and naturally didn’t reckon
that he was doing much good with his
money; still he dropped a few big lumps
into Australian enterprises, and he got a lot
of satisfaction in helping to show that the
Australian youngster could do as well as
anybody else. He was 81 when he died, and
he probably wouldn’t have died even then if
the recent Randwick race meeting hadn’t
been a bit too much for him.

“Ped.” writes:—
The heart failure that Labor Minister Batchelor
succumbed to must have been due mainly to his'
visit to Britain during the Coronation hustle. He
died, as Dick Seddon died, of the eternal round of
speeching, travelling, and compulsory feasting inci-
dental to what the Tory press described as a “holiday
jaunt.” The fact that he.was quite out of condition
became apparent to Batchelor as soon as he began to
climb a hill. He had gone a whole year or more
without walking a mile at a stretch, he said, but in
that year he had given his willing brain and re-
luctant stomach twice as much work as they wanted
to. keep them in form. The duties of a political
Minister are as hard or as easy as he cares to make
them, and Batchelor made strenuous labor of his job.
The duties of a delegate politician in a foreign
country at a Coronation time ought not to be im-
posed upon such workers. What is meat to a George
Reid, Tom Bent, John Forrest or Jack Murray is
poison to men of nervous temperament. The ’ first
notable result of Prime Minister Fisher’s trip to
Britain was his painful breakdown in the House, some
three 'weeks ago. In Batchelor’s case the result was
death.
Gave up the spectre, a few days back, in
Adelaide, William Thornley, for many years
the engineer associated with W. Sandford in
the plucky struggle to establish an iron and
steel business at Lithgow (N.S.W.). Thorn-
ley, who had previously made a speciality
of railway interlocking, was engaged on a
job of that sort, in connection with the Ade-
laide trams, when the whistle blew for the
knock-off.
THE LATE HARRY RICKARDS.
It’s a long- while since Westralia had an
Attorney-General who knew what a brief
was when he got the law billet. The
Scadden Government hasn’t an Attorney-
General at the moment; but it is understood
that Tommy Walker, who has studied law,
will be It as soon as he is “admitted.” Mean-
time, he is Minister for Justice. Nanson
was Attorney-General for Wilson and
Moore, and he also had only been “admitted ’
late in life, and hadn’t had time to knock a
business together. Rason, in his short reign,
pulled through without, an Attorney, Dag-
lish having set the fashion in his Labor
Ministry, for the very good reason that he
didn’t have a lawyer on the premises. Law-
yers, in fact, were scarce in the earlier
days of the Australian Labor party. There
are four Labor Governments now, and there
isn’t a law-Minister in the lot who was a
lawyer when he joined the party. Hughes
mended umbrellas and things, and had been
a school teacher; Holman was a cabinet-
maker ; Denny, of S. Aus., was a journalist;
and Tommy YValker was too many things to
mention.
“Rad” speaks:—
According to a cable in Melbourne Age last week,
the Unionists have adopted “Bob” Sievier for their
candidate in the Hoxton division of Shoreditch.
Sievier is a racing owner, and editor and
proprietor of the Winning Post—a weekly
which serves up crude Tory political views,
miscellaneous anecdotes and sporting items, in
about equal quantities. He has a ready, if
unlettered pen, and no vestige of a sense of the
ridiculous. The latter asset enables him to talk
down as from the. summit of the Matterhorn, or other
great height, to reputable persons, such as Prime
Minister Asquith, the Chancellor of the Exchequer,
Winston Churchill, Augustine Birrell, etc., in the
phraseology which the village Squire employs what
time he rebukes a larcenous stable-hand.
Seven or eight years ago, Sievier was brought
into the Gazing Boom of the Raleigh Club for a
few minutes. The Raleigh is a “pot-house” frank
and unashamed ; and, anyhow, the Gazing Room (a
sort of annexe overlooking Regent-street, where mem-
bers and others gather to consult the “tape”
and absorb alcohol) is no more part of
the club proper than is the Strangers’ Room at
one of our local nstitutions. Yet, it was consid-
ered that the Raleigh had been defiled, and one
of the members—Sir James Duke —said so. Sievier
brought a libel action, and, in the ensuing trial, his
strenuous career was made a subject of comment.
A parson’s son, he embarked early on the Frivolous
Life. In Australia he was a ready-money bookie,
and some paralysing tales (or l’es) were told of his
doings during that period, and on his subsequent
return to England. After hearkening to very vivid
evidence of this kind for about a week, the Court
found, in effect, that Sievier was not qualified for the
Archangel Michael’s job, and recorded a verdict to
that effect, or thereabouts. Sievier was next in
trouble at Newmarket Heath —the same sort of thing
which once happened to the heir to the British
Crown. Financial embarrassment sat at his
door after the manner of that Norse lady
of whom it was said that “Precipice was
her threshold; Care was her thorny bed.” At this
exciting moment he started the Winning Post, and
is now a rich man. And the Tory party evidently
considers that he is eligible for what used to be “the
best club in Europe.” As Sievier, who is strong
on cheap classicism, would put it: “Eheu
futjaces! How is thy glory faded, O Israel! It was
not thus in my hot youth, when Plancus was Con-
sul.” And so forth.
Apropos; the late “Swish” Broadwood, sportsman.
roue and mammoth piano-manufacturer, was kicked
out of the Badminton Club some years ago, owing
to his exasperating habit of hurling the smoking-
room clock into Piccadilly, whenever he got tight. A
week later, he wrote playfully, inviting the com-
mittee to dine with him at the Carlton Club, to
which Mecca of High Toryism he had just been
elected. British Conservatism doesn’t worry much
about its votaries’ characters in these days of its
desperation.

National prosperity helps a trustee co. in several
ways. First, such a co. gets its income from two
main sources—(l) annual commission on the earn-
ings of the trust estate and (2) a lump commission
on the corpus or capital value. Prosperity means
a bigger income for the trust estate, and, therefore,
a bigger commission for the trustee; also the capital
value is swollen and the commission on the corpus
swells too. So its two main sources of revenue
benefit. Prosperity also makes many a man feel
that his estate is worth handing over to a trustee
co. And, finally, when revenues are moving up, and
properties are fetching more than they were valued
at, the trustee co. doesn’t stand much risk of being
shot at; it is when things go the other way that
disappointed beneficiaries fossick round for some
cause of action against the trustee. All these and
probably a few other things help to explain why
the profits of this and other trustee cos. have im-
proved so much during the last few years; and they
also suggest that, when things in general sag, their
profits will sag too. No doubt the normal tendency
will be upwards: the trustee co. is, as a rule, so
well equipped for the special job it has to do, and
the private trustee is often so badly equipped, that
it seems inevitable that the professional trustee
will more and more displace the other sort. Doubt-
less, however, a Commonwealth or State trustee will
arrive in N.S.W. presently, and enter into competi-
tion, as in Great Britain and elsewhere; and that
must make some difference to the co.’s prospects.
Still, in that connection, this has to be remembered —
the fatter the citizen and his estate, the more likely,
as a rule, will he be to feed the Private Enterprise
concern, if he can’t get the State to do the work
for nothing or thereabouts. This particular co.—
one of only two in N.S.W. —is making rapid pro-
gress. Ten years ago the estates in hand were
valued at £1,365,337 ; now. though circumstances
have lately favored rapid winding up, the total is
£3,130,932. Its own paid capital is £25,000, with
another £975,000 uncalled (a nice little guarantee
fund that the co. will do its duty) ; and practically
the whole of the funds are represented by public
securities ( £20,000) and mortgages ( £39,391). As-
suming that these earn on the average only a shade
over 4 p.c., they now produce the entire 10 p.c.
div., leaving the whole profit from the business to
go towards the strengthening of' reserves. And
strong reserves mean—(l) Better security for clients
and beneficiaries; (2) a guarantee that the div. will
be maintained even though unpleasant things happen
to the community, and, consequently, to the co.’s
business profits.

After referring shareholders to the accounts, the di-
rectors remark: “You will note that ample provision
has been made for depreciations, etc.” They will
not note anything of the sort unless they have very
much better eyesight than this paper owns. It is
true that the gibbering skeleton of a Profit and
Loss account mentions —“Salaries, wages, commis-
sion... depreciations... etc, £10,807,” but the de-
preciation allowed for may be £5OOO and ample, or
eighteenpence and wretched. Yet, without informa-
tion on the point, no reliable opinion can be formed
about the year’s operations. Perhaps it doesn’t
matter much, because the past year’s transactions
were abnormal, anyhow, inasmuch as the Brisbane
breweries spent eight or nine months in clawing at
one another, and cutting prices, and the mania
wasn t ended until June. With harmony restored,
profits will probably go back to their old level —if
there was a level; the figures suggest something more
like a precipice. Perhaps things might look better
if one could get under the surface; but, on the sur-
face, there is certainly nothing at all that is attrac-
tive. The paid capital is £39,289. Other items
can be grouped this way:—

The Bellambi Coal Co. (N.S.W.)—the last
few years of a remarkably prosperous concern;—
To Re- Re-
Profits. Dividends. serves. serves.
1907.. 17$ p.c. = £30,625 £77,351
£25,000 transferred to Capital .. 52,351
1908.. £61,936 17$ p.c. = 35,000 £26,936 79,287
1909.. 53,879 15 „ „ 36,250 17,629 96,916
1910.. 57,345 12$ „ ~ 31,250 26,095 123,011
1911.. 73,961 17$ ~ ~ 43,750 30,211 153,222
These people, who will be making money presently
if they are not careful, have had an amazing run
of good luck and good management. Formed in
1888, the co. moved very slowly for some years.
But it was in the right hands; and, when the high
value of the coal for bunkering purposes was
demonstrated, those hands were strong enough to
push the business under the right noses. Malcolm
McEacharn, of the big shipping firm, was a director;
so were (and are) James Burns, of 8., P. and Co.,
and Adam Forsyth, of the A.U.S.N. ; so, in his day,
was old David Syme, of the Age. Among them they
whipped up the most valuable shipping connection
in Australia. On top of that came the coke in-
dustry, which turned the small stuff into money;
the Broken Hill Prop, ovens are fed by this co’.
The directors were far-seeing men, too, and, before
the full possibilities of the case were apparent, they
added the South Bulli to their original holding, for
a comparative trifle, and brought their principality
up to 200,000 acres.
For many years the co. was in 175,000 shares.
Then £25,000 was transferred from reserves, and
£50,000 was got in, though it wasn’t wanted; and
thus the total was brought up to £250,000, the full
amount authorised. Before there are any more dis-
tributions, etc., there will consequently have to be
a change in the articles. The little list printed
above shows £73,961 profit for the year. Part of
this—£2s,ooo, to be exact —is, however, an amount
added to the fund for the insurance of steamers and
to cover depreciation of plant and mine. But it
does not follow that that is the only provision made
for depreciation; on the contrary, there is plenty
of evidence that, in some years at least, large
amounts have been written off. Large improvements
and plant additions were made last year, yet the
book value of collieries, jetties, steamers, railways
and plant was increased by only £2532. They
probably cost at least £lO,OOO more. Compared
with 1905, the grouped balance-sheet figures have
changed like this:—•
1905. 1910. 1911.
Liabilities .. .. £39,445 £12,017 £13,458
Assets —
Mines, plant, steamers 209,345 265,069 267,601
Stores and stock . . 4,591 7,334 7,760
Liquid assets . . 55,183 131,375 166,319
Between 1905 and 1911 £50,000 of new capital was
got in, but that was more than absorbed by new
steamers and plant. The cash position has become
bloated to this extent:—
1905. 1911.
Liquid assets .. .. £59,774 £174,079
Liabilities . . .. 39,445 13,458
£20,329 £160,621
That is £140,292 in six years, after paying fat
dividends. And practically the whole of the liquid
assets are cash and public securities. Certainly this
co. can’t be accused of eating its cake and putting
nothing in the stocking towards buying another
one.

There is nothing very cheerful about that. Mel-
bourne has been growing, but this co.’s revenue has
been rather dwindling than otherwise, no doubt as
the result of electrical competition. Still, things are
far from bad. In 1908 it was agreed that it would
be necessary to put asfcie £3020 a year to provide
for the return of capital by the time the co.’s rights
expired, and to provide for the upkeep of plant
meantime. An 8 p.c. div. calls for £6300, so the
agreed-upon contributions and the dividend together
make £9320 a year. The average surplus on
working account after paying exes, in the past three
years has been £10,647; so there is a very fair
margin, after paying the 8 p.c. div., to provide for
contingencies. The sinking fund, by the way, is
invested in public securities, except for a few
pounds sunk in land.
The Australasian Motor Cab Co.—
For 12 months past Australian shareholders in this
concern have been seeking information, and now that
it is available they probably wish it wasn’t. The co.
was promoted in London early last year, with an
Australian advisory Board consisting of J. H. Car-
ruthers and Arthur Allen; and the prospectus was
one of the most impudent ever issued in the Com-
monwealth. Altogether, 194,000 preference shares
(7 p.c.) were offered, of which 55,000 were under-
written “for a commission payable by the co. at the
rate of 7p.c. in cash and 1 p.c. in ordinary
shares.” Thus, for underwriting about one-fourth of
the issue, the underwriters (the Straits Syndicate,
Ltd.) got £4125 in cash and 1 p.c. in ordinary
shares. Now, these ordinary shares were Is. each;
so the 1 p.c. meant 11,000 such shares, and ordinary
shares were entitled to one-half the profits after the
pref. 7 p.c. was paid. Besides getting this exorbitant
payment for underwriting, the Straits Syndicate,
Ltd., got £7850 and the right to take a big swag
of ordinary shares for paying certain preliminary
expenses. Further, the Straits Syndicate, Ltd.,
agreed to supply 25 motor-cabs and 275 chassis at
a price. In a circular issued by the managing
director, it is now alleged that, of the 194,000 pref.
shares, only 55,931 were applied for, including only
5837 in England. The Straits Syndicate applied for
some of these shares, but couldn’t, or didn’t, com-
plete payment, and these shares have been forfeited;
also, the S. Syndicate let a sub-contract for the
supply of the vehicles, and the contractors also ap-
plied for some shares, and they were in the same
leaky boat. The co. paid 25 p.c. deposit on the
vehicles. Says the managing director: “The climax
came with the financial failure of the manufacturer
and the consequent inability of the Straits Syndicate,
Ltd., to make good the deposit paid to the manufac-
turer. The manufacturer’s assets were shown to be
insufficient to cover the secured creditors, conse-
quently it would be futile to put into operation any
legal remedy.” This suggests either that the
directors made an appallingly bad contract with the
Straits Syndicate, Ltd., or that the Straits Syndi-
cate, Ltd., was in a shockingly bad way itself. Any-
how, the co.’s deposit went up the spout to the extent
of £3900, and altogether the 55,000 shares only
gave £35,400, after various London sharks had had
their bite.
To add to its woes, the Motor-Cab Co. proceeded
to get ready its organisation in Sydney; and then
the taxis didn’t arrive. Under the contract with the
Terrible Straits crowd —into whose financial position
nobody seems to have inquired—loo cabs should
have been delivered from June, 1910, to March
1911, whereas none at all were available until Oc-
tober, 1910, and then they dropped in casually until
by the end of September, 1911, there were 47—a daily
average of 26. Under the circumstances it isn’t very
surprising that whereas “the gross revenue amounted
to £9307, the working exes., excluding directors’
fees and London office exes., amounted to £8268.”
The idea now is to bleed the present shareholders
for another ss. per share—to wind up the present co
and issue a 15s. share for a £1 share. Those who
don t choose to pay up will get what they can out of
the liquidation, and it probably won’t be much. The
new co. will, of course, be registered in Australia—
there seem to be too many hungry wild animals
roaming in London to make it safe for the co. to
live there. The scheme would probably bring in
about £13,500, which it is calculated would com-
plete the co. s equipment. Simultaneously, it is
proposed to issue one pref. share for every five or-
dinanes, though why these new pref. shares shouldn’t
y c equ !p? (l to P a T up their ss. per share isn’t ob-
vious. They ought to do something for their living.
Most of the ordinaries (against which it is proposed
lssue u V em) We , re lssued in return for “services”
performed by people who made such a howling mess
of things that it would be difficult to find a parallel
“ r c ' r ! n loca i history; and the less consideration
be encouraged ThiS SOrt ° f thin = Wouldn’t

Profits are stated gross, without any deduction for
depreciation ; and a great part of the amount written
oil has been actually spent on renewals. The re-
serves therefore include the provision for special re-
newals; which means that they are to some extent
merely a set-off against depreciation, and are not
true reserves. The actual surplus, after paying
dividends, has thus been probably a good deal less
than £5OOO in the past seven years—and that can’t
be regarded as a very liberal lay-by. The business
has, however, been increasing rapidly. In the first
half of 1905, the total sales were £4262; in the
past half-year, £BBO3. Certainly a bit of new capi-
tal has been got in: the total paid up is now
£59,943, as against £44,667 as at June, 1905. But
that wouldn’t double the turnover; so it is pretty
certain that the capital previously invested has been
giving a much better return; and this ought to mean
improved profits presently. So far, as shown above,
there hasn’t been much of a margin, after paying
the 6 p.c. dividend. The co.’s financial position is
easy: its liquid assets exceed its debts.

Down to about seven years ago this co. went on
paying gay dividends, and didn’t trouble about a
stupid little thing like depreciation of plant and
other assets; and that really meant paying divi-
dends out of capital. When the auditors struck
there were only two things to do: the co. might write
on capital, or it might knock off or reduce divi-
dends, and thus make good the old losses out of
future profits. It adopted the latter course; hence
the large amounts written off during the past few
years. The profit thus undivided has been used
mainly to reduce liabilities:—
June, ’O4. June, ’ll
Inabilities .. ..£26,702 £13,981
Liquid assets .. .. 6,124 6,266
Deficit.. ..£20,578 £7,715
The co. has thus improved what may be called its
cash position by £12,863 in seven years. That it
has got so much to the good is, however, improb-
able, because no doubt there has been further depre-
ciation during this period. Still, it is going the
right way. The trouble occurred chiefly in connec-
tion with the electric light department, which is still
barely able to pay its own exes., without providing
anything for wear and tear.

* * * *
The Country Producers’ Selling Co.
Sydney. These are a few belated remarks about a
more or less co-operative concern, formed in 1910, to
sell the farm and station —chiefly station —produce of
its shareholders_ and other people. It seems to have
got off with a jump, for in its first nine months it
stood third in the tally of sheep sales, and fourth
in the tally of cattle sales at Flemington. No doubt
on that account some people expected that it would
show a profit instead of a loss of £660 in its first
accounts; but as the Profit and Loss account shows
that “legal, preliminary and establishment expenses”
haye all been wiped out, the result may be viewed as
quite satisfactory. The co. paid nothing for good-
will ; it set out to establish its own business, and
the fact that it handled more sheep than any other
agents except two, and more cattle than any others
except three, suggests that it got the business at
astonishingly small cost. Many another co., taking
over a private concern, has paid several tens of
thousands for a smaller connection. ' Of course no-
thing can be said about its future. Almost every-
thing depends on its management. But it certainly
starts off exceedingly well.

This co. runs a wholesale market, and lets out stalls
at a daily or weekly rental; and in 1905 it provided
wRh new freehold premises for the purpose,
ibis little comparison shows the progress it has made
since then:—
t , 1905. 1911.
Debentures and other debt ..£15,182 £11,020
Liquid assets .. .. .. 9 3,046
Excess of liabilities .. .. £15,173 £7,974
So in six years the position, from this point of view,
has been improved to the extent of £7199 after the
payment of very substantial dividends. It is not
certain, however, that the co. is £7199 better off:
all depends upon whether the principal asset, the
freehold, has maintained its original value of
£29,680, at which it still stands in the books.
Market premises are subject to heavy depreciation,
and, though upkeep has been charged to ordinarv
workmg exes., nothing has been written off the item.
Also, the upkeep of the premises will increase with
age. But while that would mean a larger bite out
of future revenue, it has to be remembered that the
co. is making a big saving in interest. Still, while
depreciation is one probable caterpillar in the cab-
bage, municipal competition may be a bigger
trouble. The City Council has established large
markets, and, seeing that the council’s money costs
only 3% P-c., there must be something wrong if a
private concern can successfully compete with the
council and still charge enough to pay 20 p.c. divi-
dends.
* * * *
Central Zinc Co., Ltd.—This co. was
promoted in 1905 by the Sulphide Corporation,
mainly to handle the latter’s output of Broken Hill
zinc concentrates. The Corporation had raised just
about as much money as it could ; hence this new co.,
to which it guaranteed 5 p.c. p.a. for 10 years. The
capital was £150,000. Works were established in
Durham (Eng.), and for a couple of years the for-
eign experts made a mess of things, largely, it seems,
because they couldn’t handle the workmen available.
4n 1.908, when collapse looked highly probable, the
Sulphide Corporation recommended the co. to try
what J. C. Moulden, a young Australian manager
and metallurgist, could do. He was put in charge,
and things have been improving ever since. The
Profit and Loss balance and other debit items un-
represented by assets, totalled £43,881 in March.
1909. Up to that time the trading account showed
a loss of £28,030. The next year showed a trading
account profit of £2157; last year a profit of
£5701. The amount of capital unrepresented by
assets has been reduced from £43,881 to £38,689",
and the prospects are now said to be cheerful, though
it appears to be still extremely difficult in Freetrade
England to get satisfactory labor for this highlv-
technieal business of zinc-distilling. Last year’s
input was 6357 tons of zinc concentrates, and the
output 1967 tons of spelter (plus, of course, the
silver-lead residues) ; this current year, with six
furnaces available, the input is expected to be
9000 tons. The fifth and sixth furnaces contain 128
retorts each; the others 96. The co. fortunately
started with a very solid capital, so that, after pro-
viding for all liabilities, it still has about £12,000
in hand. The Sulphide Corporation has paid it
£22,763 by way of guaranteed dividend, and appar-
ently that total amount has been collected by this
co.’s shareholders.

The undisputed benefit of asbestos covering for
steam-pipes and boilers has been exemplified by a re-
cent scientific treatise of the principles of steam
conveyance. Tests made have established the fact
that 100 square feet of pipe covered with this
material, at a pressure of 2001 b. per square inch, and
coal at 16s. per ton, will save £7 per year, and
prevent loss of pressure equal to 13.84 B. Th. U. per
square foot per minute. Bell’s Asbestos Non-con-
ducting Composition, supplied in cwt. bags, or

The most modern, simple and effective Vacuum
Slimes Table is the “Wilfley.” It is continuous, and
consists of a revolving drum which is partly submerged
in a tank of slimes or material to be filtered. The
drum is covered with a filter cloth supported on
grooves on the drum, which are connected by means
of holes to the interior to allow the liquid to be
withd 'wn. Through the trunnions at the ends of
the c am the vacuum pipe passes to the interior.
This pipe connects with the interior of the drum,
through which the liquid and air are withdrawn by
means of a vacuum pump. The filtered slime is re-
moved by a simple apparatus placed at or below the
level of the pulp in the tank. So that directly the
cake is removed, the surface of the filter cloth as it
revolves is immediately re-immersed in the pulp
ready to receive a fresh cake, thereby preventing the
sucking in of air, and consequent loss of vacuum
between the time the cake is removed and a fresh
one is forming. The residual liquid in the slime
cake is removed by distributing water over the sur-
face of the semi-dried cake. Owing to the fact that
the filter is circular in form, it will be seen that it
is continuous in operation, and has no complication
of valves to get out of order; furthermore, work is
being done continuously during the whole revolution
of the filter drum. The Austral Otis Engineering
Company, of South Melbourne, have the sole manu-
facturing rights of this, filter.* [2
Extract from The Finance Chronicle, London
(15/11/1910). —“Colonial Mutual Life Office: We
have been favored with a copy of an exceedingly
business-like prospectus, just issued by this well-
managed Life Assurance Society, setting forth the
superior advantages of their Combined Life, Accident
and Sickness policies. On comparing the rates
charged in the tables shown in the prospectus, we
find that at most ages the premiums quoted are
exceedingly moderate. In fact, the rates are in
many instances about the same as the average pre-
miums charged by most offices for endowment life
insurance only. The Colonial Mutual Society is
well known for its low whole-life premium rates, but
this new combined policy, covering, as it does, those
extra risks at the exceedingly low rates quoted, only
requires to be known to be largely applied for.”*

was the receipt of advice of “certain negotiations for
“(1) the acquisition of your co.’s patent rights; (2)
the acquisition of certain iron and steel works in
England; (3) the absorption of the English Co.” In
June the co. wired its chairman in London, and was
told in reply that “delay entirely caused hos-
tility experts in discouraging supporters,” but very
strong parties now moving to provide necessary funds
during next month.” Apparently the very strong
parties fell down the same crack that has swallowed
various other dukes and such-like, who were alleged
to have taken up the process. Anyway, two or three
next months have happened, and the process seems
to be just as far from adoption as ever. Curiously
enough, the hostility of experts seems to be still re-
garded as something to which shareholders are en-
titled to pin their faith.
* * * *
Customs Minister Tudor gave a mostly
non-committal answer to a deputation which
asked for an export duty on copper ores,
and a commission to inquire into the wisdom
of applying the same treatment to other ores. Ap-
parently the Minister didn’t know much about the
highly-teehnieal business beforehand, and he evi-
dently wasn’t much wiser after half a dozen speakers
had rattled through a few remarks to a gentleman in
a chair, with a watch in his hand, while another
deputation was waiting for him on the doormat.
“An export duty on ores,” he remarked, “might im-
mediately cause a deputation to wait upon me to
ask for an export duty on greasy wool,” etc. Many
a Government has done worse things than impose an
export duty on greasy wool. Still, the two cases are
not much more alike than an elephant and a straw
hat.
No nation can become great without metals; yet
many a nation has become great without having a
wool-washing plant on its premises. The Govern-
ment to which Tudor belongs is sound on the evils
of dumping as applied to imports; it sees that an
Australian manufacturer could be knocked out by
a foreign rival dumping a lot of goods in this coun-
try at under cost price—a thing which it would pay
him to do if he could thus kill the local industry and
secure the local market. But Tudor apparently
doesn’t see that the same thing can be done by the
foreign manufacturer outbidding the local manufac-
turer for his raw product, and thus leaving him
stranded ; he apparently doesn’t see that it would pay
the foreigner to do that, inasmuch as, once the
foreigner had the market to himself, he could get
the local raw goods at his own price. That this is
the caper is shown by the statement made bv the
Mount Kembla smelting and refinery people: “When
the Kembla refineries were started the American
refineries reduced their charges on Australian blister
copper by 30 p.c. They gave to Australian pro-
ducers lower rates than they gave to their own
people.” And it may be accepted as a foregone con-
clusion that, when the Australian works are knocked
out, up will go the charges again—which is the
dumping evil exactly. On the other hand, the
foreigner wouldn’t be fool enough to offer a special
price to get greasy wool, because he would know
that he could never recoup himself. You don’t have to
put in £200,000 worth of plant to start a wool-
scour, and, if the foreigner tried to get his money
back by offering less than a fair thing for washed
wool, the stuff would promptly go to the local scours,
which could be restarted at about three days’ notice.
* * * *
Those who shake their heads over the
prospects of the spelter market being permanently
maintained by the Zinc Convention, point to the
fact that there are now well over 100,000 tons of
rich zinc concentrates hung up at Broken Hill,
Port Pirie and intermediate stations. Delivery
has been taken of these, so the European zinc
smelters who are in the convention must be paying
out a heap of money in interest, etc. The yarn,
of course, is that the stuff isn’t sent on because
freights are so high. Perhaps so; but, even if that
is the explanation, it doesn’t alter the fact that this
current production is being kept off the market.
* * * *
McLean’s Mallee Hen, 35 miles from Wy-
along (N.S.W.), crushed 25 tons of picked stone for
6250 z 250 z. per ton. The show is being de-
veloped at the 90ft. level, and there is a fair quantity
of quartz at grass not considered good enough to
send such a long distance to the battery.
* * * *
The Brass Knocker, near Pambula
(N.S.W.), reports a big formation 20ft. between
walls. The bulk appears to be poor, but some rich
specimens have been got from a narrow quartz vein
which is going under foot. The strike was made in
deepening an old shaft from the 50ft. level. A good
deal of prospecting is being done in the locality,
which hitherto has proved itself very capricious.
TFestralia’s gold production for the first
nine months of 1911 amounted to 1,018,700 fine oz.,
which is exactly 76,0000 z. less than that for the
corresponding period in 1910, and over 178,0000 z.
short of the output for the same fraction of 1909.
As stated here, over and over again, the continued
shrinkage is primarily due to the depletion of some
mines, and the gradual fall in grade and returns of
other big producers. Unfortunately, the aggregate
of new mines unearthed is not equal to the number
that have gone under. Besides the usual aids to
prospectors and development by means of transport
and water facilities, the best way for the new
Labor Minister to set about giving the industry a
leg-up would he to (1) complete a geological sur-
vey linking up all centres where any gold has
hitherto been found, and (2) map out belts of new
country that might repay genuine prospecting. The
Bulletin is credibly informed that, if this were
done, plenty of money would be forthcoming to
back up such prospecting. The work, however, must
be done by competent geologists, and what the new
Government must realise is that it will have to pay
men of this sort handsomely. Existing agencies and
methods are wholly inadequate.
* * * *
Word comes from London via the West
that the Bullfinch is to be equipped with a- 20-head
battery and accessories, and that the milling grade
is somewhere near the half oz. This is almost the
first attempt that has been made, outside this page,
to paint the show in something approaching its true
colors.

The Bulletin said at the time that shareholders

Great Finch (W.A.) has turned down its
option on the Eclipse, at Marvel Loch, and is now
on the lookout for something else.
* * * *
Managing director Pettigrew recommends
a five-head mill and trimmings for the Pine Hill
(W.A.), and backs up his recommendation with an
estimate of nine months’ ore in sight. But, in case
a five-head battery should not be enough, and for
other reasons, the Board had better get some inde-
pendent person to furnish a report.
Cumberland (W.A.) is now leaving the
worry of getting gold out of its Norseman derelict
to tributors, and is focussing its attention on the
Abbotshall option. Water lately brought about a
suspension of underground work in this property,
but the search for payable values at 100 ft. has since
been resumed. The co. still has over £6OOO in
hand, so that, while there’s cash, there’s hope.
Adelaide and other places are being but-
tonholed to find money for the flotation of the Lake
View lease at Payne’s Find. Reports on the prop-
erty speak of the existence of four gold-bearing reefs,
but, so far, there is nothing to justify the assump-
tion that any one of them is payable in depth. _
That virtue remains to be proved.
* * * *
Ida H. (W.A.) reports payable ore in a
bore at a depth of 822 ft. The find is in hitherto
untested ground, 700 ft. south of existing workings,
which makes the total length of proved reef over
2000 ft. But the average width is very small.
* * * *
Hidden Treasure at Cue (W.A.) still
scintillates. The last three weeks’ run gave a return
of nearly 1000 oz., which makes a total output of
about 16500 z. since £30,000 was paid for it by an
enterprising local syndicate. The balance of the
money is said to be in sight—or nearly that much.
* * * *
“Underlay”: “The Man from Tanami”
(B. 7/9/’ll) gives an amazing description of the
field. I pass over the fact that he refers to the
reefs as having the same strike and underlay, which
I’m afraid is impossible, and get on to his yarn
that there are no “jeweller’s shops.” The patches
are “jeweller’s shops”—when you can find them. I
can put my hand on a piece of Tanami “stone,”
4oz. weight, which was bought for £l6. That’s
pretty good proof that there are “specimens.” The
contributor also mentioned two bores, with plenty of
“cold, clear, fresh, sparkling water.” There are no
bores on Tanami, but there are two shafts down to
the stated 170 ft. These were sunk with the windlass,
so you may guess the water was not too strong.
The supply is only a soakage, but it is certainly
good. One is sunk near the reefs, and another three
miles away. “The Man’s” geology, also, is a trifle at
fault, when he says he believes the water has come
through the limestone. The nearest limestone is
six miles from the field. Finally, Tanami isn’t a
“desert,” as most people seem to think. It is very
fair cattle country in anything like a decent season,
and the seasons have been very decent ever since
Tanami was first worked.
* * * *
The little Charters Towers crowd that re-
volves around Miles’s office, where Joe Milliean is the
king-pin, looks like making another pot of money out
of Brilliant Deeps. Only a month ago Deeps were
worth (paids) about eighteenpence; now they are in
the neighborhood of 9s. When they were worth a
few pence a Towers man who mixes law and specula-
tion, and had been backing 20,000 of the shares
through the co.’s long struggle, got tired and wanted
to pass the property on to a neighboring co. But the
Milliean people hadn’t lost faith, and bought the
other man out at the price offered by the neighbor-
ing co. —which meant a few pence per share. They
won’t get dividends yet; but there are big hopes, and
the Towers sees in the incident another justification
for pegging away.
* * * *
It was through his brother Tom that
Walter Hall became interested in Mount Morgan.
Tom (T.S.) was at the time manager of the Q.N.
Bank at Rockhampton, and to him one of the three
Morgans, the prospectors, handed a couple of pieces
of stone. Tom Hall tested it, and he and W. K.
D’Arcy, a Rockhampton solicitor, drove out to the
show a few davs later. Pattison, a local grazier and
butcher, stood in with them, and they agreed to put
up the money for plant, etc., for a half share.
Walter Hall stood in with his brother. Next, the
three Morgans divided their half into five shares, and
Tom Hall bought one of the fifths (really tenths in
the mine). Fred Morgan bought his brother out for
£lO,OOO, and thus owned three-tenths of the mine.
The Halls, D’Arcy and Pattison next bought this
three-tenths for £62,000, but they sold one-tenth to
John Ferguson (local merchant and M.L.A.) for
£26,000. They, however, about squared things by
buying the last one-tenth from the Morgans for
£31,000. The Hall-D’Arcy-Pattison syndicate thus
owned nine-tenths and John Ferguson and his friends
one-tenth. Walter Hall had, however, given his old
coaching partners a chance, and John Ferguson had
bought up a few shares; hence when the co. was
formed with a million shares, the lot were cut up
this way—
Tom Hall 158,332
W. K. D'Arcy . . . . 358,334
W. Pattison . . . . 125,000
W. R. Hall .. • • 125,000
John Ferguson .. .. 116,667
O. O. Skarratt .. .. 58,334
W. A. Robertson
and John Wagner .. 58,333
Total .. • • 1,000,000
The dividends paid by the co. up to the end of last
month totalled £7 10s. 7d. per share, equal alto-
gether to £7,729,164.
* * * * .
The Vulcan (N.Q.) is showing lower-grade
ore in too many places; and on present appearances
the best that can be hoped for is that the recent
development at the 600 ft. level will provide sufficient
ore to merely sweeten up the returns for a year or
more.
* * * *
Gilmore (N.Q.) mine crushes at the
Gurrumbah mill, at Reid’s Creek. The mill is only
working one shift, for the tin shows in the vicinity
are mostly abandoned. The plant was put up seven
years since to treat ore from the Dalzells mine, which
turned out a deceptive surface show. Gilmore’s in-
tends to sink the shaft to 450 ft. The development
that revived the market recently was at the 160 ft.
level. The vein of ore is high grade, but generally
only a few inches wide, and a winze will have to be
put down to determine its value. * *
A big Westralian battery would lick up
the Central Red, White and Blue’s half-year’s out-
put of 9344 tons in a month and not strain a bolt.
But Bendigo won’t have that, for it likes its mining
sweetness long drawn out, so that it may have time
to poke down from one saddle to another. Even
Catherine Reef, with its big formations, is satisfied
with 15,000 tons a half-year, and the new battery
and plant the Central Blue is putting in won’t ex-
ceed those figures by much. To find cash for this
plant and the extra leases £9OOO will have to be
got The prudence of the Board in not cutting up
all the profits enables it to draw on the reserve for
most of that sum without checking the divs. As
the mine has what manager Kingsley cautiously
calls large bodies of payable stone to stope, it is
still in the fairway of the market. Last half-year s
milling campaign averaged over an oz. per ton, the
co getting £42,494 for its gold. Expenses of
every sort took as much as 20s. per ton; but, then,
1608 ft. of sinking and driving was paid for. With
the new plant costs ought to come down shillings.
A slide has disturbed the formation south in the
bottom level, but north good stone is exposed,
though it is not expected that the grade will be as
high as that of the quartz broken at 318 ft. The
little mine has yielded £67,000 in divs. on £1593
of cash capital, and has saved Bendigo from partial
paralysis by giving an interest in side-line work.
The vacuum filter on the Magdala-cum-
Moonlight slimes heap at Stawell (Vic.), is moving
again, after the installation of several improvements
calculated to decrease costs and increase extraction.
The process in vogue is an interesting innovation
in Victorian cyanide practice. Briefly, the system
adopted is to sluice the slimes from the face by
means of a high-pressure jet of cyanide solution. After
passing through two vortex mixers, the pulp is sent
through a tube mill, to reduce the sand and mineral
and clean the gold, after which it runs into the agita-
tion vats, and from, there into vacuum filter vats. The
plant represents about £lO,OOO of Sorth Aus. and Wes-
tralian capital, and those connected with it deserve
success, if only for applying modern machinery and
up-to-date methods to the ore of a field which, in
common with Victorian fields generally, is much
given to regarding any collection of antediluvian
bric-h-brac as an up-to-date plant.
* * * *
Jimmy Lewis, of the Anchor mine (Tas.),
keeps his costs as diminutive as he is himself. Last
12 months he punched through 103,000 tons, and
wasn’t content because he spent 2s. 7id. per ton to
get out his tin. His margin of profit is narrow
enough to frighten anyone but an M.0.E., for the
ore only runs 2.381 b. of metallic tin per ton —3s. 9d.
cash, taking the metal at £lBO. A drop of £3O a
ton would hit Lewis like a blizzard on the Blue
Tier. And because he knows that a fall to £l5O
is not impossible, he yanked the old Australian mine
in to help up the average. If he had more cash he
might rake in some of Frank Gee Duff s Blue Tier
leases, since the Mt. Lyell has turned them up for
ever. J. Bull has forked out £25,000 of -deben-
tures for the Anchor apart from paid-up capital,
and, in view of that load, Lewis sadly needs a good
patch, either in or out of the Anchor holding. His
latest feat was to drag £5OOO out of the Tasmanian
Treasury to keep his mine going and his staff to-
gether. One who could achieve that in ’Coota-land
has done well with his sft. lin.
* * * *
Radium Hill Co., whose show is near
Olary (S.A.), intends to put up radium-extraction
works on the Parramatta (N.S.W.). Kadcliffe, of
the Bairnsdale School of Mines (Vic.\ gives an en-
couraging report on the strength of the experi-
mental treatment of 31 tons of crude ore. ’She
most definite statement is that radium bromide
can be extracted successfully, and if the Parramatta
works can extract a gram (15.43 grains) of radium
bromide in a year and obtain the astonishingly high
price of £15,000 per gram, everybody concerned
should be happy. There are at present about 10
grams (154 grains) of radium in the whole world,
and,, it is only reasonable to suppose that even a
moderate increase in supply will reduce the price.
There are 5600 tons of uranium ore developed at
Radium Hill, and so far only a fragment of this lot
has been treated. No definite statement is made as
to the percentage of uranium oxide present, but as
5600 t0n5=12,544,0001b., and the estimated quan-
tity of uranium oxide is 162,4001 b., there appears to
be lib. of uranium oxide to 771 b. of ore.
The convict industry in New Caledonia is
in a dull way now, and the colonists have been
driven to pay more attention to local resources. Not
too soon, either. With the stoppage of deportation
the outlook was getting gloomier and gloomier; for
even convicts die and leave off wanting victuals and
other attention.
Nickel ore production may now be said to almost
keep New Caledonia on the map. Some say that, had
convicts not been introduced, New Caledonia would
to-day have controlled the nickel markets of the
world. Others say, had convicts not been intro-
duced, nickel would not have been discovered (or at
least worked) at all. Either may be right. While
the convict business boomed, the industries were
practically neglected. Gradually the convict became
a libere, and, ere long, there was' a lot of him. He
went into the hills, which means he went every-
where, for New Caledonia is all hills. He dis-
covered the nickel, the cobalt, the chrome, and has
since been allowed to work the discoveries. But
ownership is in larger hands. Meanwhile, the
Canadian people became busy upon their nickel and
cobalt deposits. Prison labor was not one of their
cards, and to-day New Caledonia, with all its won-
derful resources, is “allowed to produce” so much
nickel ore per annum, and no more. It is allowed to
produce no cobalt at all. Such is the foreign con-
trol. In chrome it holds and exports its own. At
Tiebaghi is what is stated to be the biggest chrome
mine in the world. The product is of very high
quality. The mine is managed by an Englishman,
T. R. Archbold, who, on past records, will not dis-
grace his country nor his profession.
Probably no country in the world has been more
intensely prospected than New Caledonia. The
liberes from lie Nou were glad to get away into
those hills; it was the next best thing possible to a
return to France. Consequently, one sees practically
hundreds of miles of close prospecting, and, what is
of more importance, hundreds of miles of open-cut
working. New Caledonia is literally scarred with
the marks of the miner’s pick—but only on the sur-
face. Until recently the island shipped all its nickel
ore to Europe for treatment. Its copper ore it sent
to Australia. By this practice the country has
gradually been skinned of all its rich ores, and the
poorer stuff was left in the ground. The French
Government decided that, in the interests of the
country, this should be stopped—and therefrom Aus-
tralia 'might take a lesson. Without any ceremony
an export duty on all ores was at once introduced.
In addition, as the export duty was not a heavy
one, the French Government offered a bonus of a
free grant of ground for the works, free fresh-water
rights, remission of harbor dues, etc., to the company
which would first produce nickel matte. The Gov-
ernment achieved its object. Smelting works were
built at Noumea, and are now running successfully.
Additional smelting works are being pushed on with
at Thio, and a copper furnace has been running
for some time at Dilah, which no longer exports ore
to Australia. The export of nickel ore still goes
on, but to a less extent. Ore which used to be sent
to Belgium for treatment is now smelted locally,
and ore which hitherto was left in the ground is
now converted into a marketable product. _ New
Caledonia imposed its export duty and bonus just m
time. Had it been left a few years longer, the
mineral resources of the island would have been
wholly under foreign control. The French people
saw their position; they acted, and, as a result, to-
day they have a say in the nickel markets of the
world It is not a predominant say, certainly, but
it is a spoke, and that is better than the nothing
which would otherwise have been the island’s lot.
French mining engineers in New Caledonia should
know all that there is to be known concerning aerial
tramways. The country is so mountainous that a
railway agitation is unknown. Aerial transporters
do the work, and do it very effectively and economi-
cally. Le Nickel had hard luck with the transporter
which it built out into the sea at Thio. A hurri-
cane wrecked a vessel that had just finished loading
at the transporter, and, in turn, the sailing ship
wrecked the transporter.
* * * *
Following hard on the heels of the sale
and proposed partition of the V.D.L. Co.’s mammoth
estate (admittedly due to the Federal land-tax),
comes the offer of the Emu Bay and Bischoff Rail-
way Co. to sell its line to the Tasmanian Govern-
ment for £232,000 —half cash, half in 34 p.c. in-
scribed stock. The price is quite high enough, but
as the railway cuts the State system in two, and
collects tribute on all the freight between the mining
fields of the West Coast and the port of Burnie, the
Government will have to buy sooner or later, or con-
tinue to lose heavily on the State lines. So, in all
probability, Private Enterprise will get what it de-
mands, or thereabouts.

QUALITY—BUCHANAN WHISKY
“BLACK & WHITE.”
A WOMAN'S
LETTER
Sydney, October IG, 1911.
My Dear Arini.—
From three different sources comes the fol-
lowing vice-regal crumb, which may have a
grain of fact in its fancy trimming. I’ve
lopped off the beginning and end, and two-
tlurds of the middle, and send you what’s left.
At the recent spring weeping, it is alleged
that the affable Gee-Gee sent one of his British
warriors in pursuit or an attractive-looking
damsel believed to have been a guest at a
recent Government House leap. Ilis Ex.
first investigated her name. The Ancient
Briton, not to be bowled out in a kan-
garoo chase as the possessor of a de-
cayed memory, said (or is alleged to have
said) : “Oh, that's Miss G—.” “Present her,”
says his Ex. They surrounded the beauty on
the lawn, but she didn’t recognise either the
G. or his warrior. “I think you were
at a dance at my house the other evening,”
says liis Ex. “I don't know you,” says the
ladv with an icy manner. “Oh, but I’m sure
.you were there —you are Miss G —.” “Par-
don me—l really have not the pleasure of
your acquaintance,” or something similar
was the freezing reply. It was a total
stranger who had never been to G. House in
her life. The Gee-Gee and his lighting man
retired in good order.
“Arethusa,” who is inclined to be saucy, fires
off a belated popgun at the social spikes that
fenced off the new Denman Excellencies dur-
ing the Pill and Amputation Party’s recent
sprint through turkey, Italian icecreams
and other excitements in this village. She
says: “At the President’s ball, as at all other
functions, the only persons allowed near the
Sacred Presence of Lord and Lady Denman
were those approved of by the Yerney clan.
One eminent professional man is said to
have insisted on his wife crossing the chalk
line, and Lady Denman was then reluctantly
informed that the eminent one’s wife was
‘quite a nice person.’ But the chalk line
isn’t always passed so easily. When his Ex.,
at the ball supper, invited a lady of some im-
portance to present to him the wife of one of
Sydney’s leading surgeons, the presentation,
it is said, didn’t come off. The frozen sil-
houette person is blamed for this.”
A garbled story has floated in re three of
Lord penman's large men-of-war, who fol-
low his Ex. officially through the shoals,
breakers and jagged rocks of “Society”
scraps. The noble three, having lost some
section of their hearts to one race-week
beauty, set out (also in sections) in pursuit
of a pale pink frock and crimson roses
(the same having been indexed for their
assistance by a lady friend), and, after a
long and exciting quest, suddenly met each
other and the pink frock, which contained
the wrong lady. Something seems to have
dropped out of the middle of this story, but
I send you the edge.
Though he was a millionaire multiplied
by three or four, Society, which spells itself
with a big S, won’t greatly miss the late
Walter Bussell Hall. The childless old
money-spinner had a very few friends, and
he treated them well; but he could get no
joy out of entertaining mobs, so he never
entertained them. They called him mean,
yet in many respects he wasn’t. He had a
bigger say in the Mount Morgan mine, of
many millions, than anybody else, and min-
ing men tell you that it is the best-paid ser-
vice of the kind in Australia. One of the
adjuncts of this Mount Morgan is Carlton
House, a sort of well-kept club, maintained
for the greater comfort of directors and
“distinguished visitors” to the mine; and it
was Walter Hall’s habit, whenever he occu-
pied Carlton House, to give “the boys” a
good _ time. “The boys” were not the local
bigwigs, but mine officers of all grades.
And, without exception, those officers are
Australian. In that respect, the big con-
cern with which the dead man was connected
is almost unique. If it wants some in-
tricate thing done, and there isn’t a man
in Australia to do it offhand, Mount
Morgan says : “Well, we’ll wait till he grows,
and, meantime, he can have a few hundred
pounds of ours to grow on.”
H. P. Airey, formerly Colonel in the
N.S.W. Ancient Armory Department, has
hung up his dinted battle-axe for good. In
other words, lie is dead, somewhere in the
Transvaal. The late Colonel Airey, with
only one arm to his credit —I fancy he lost
the other in a Burmese scrap—was a dash-
ing horseman, and the chief instrument of
war in promoting, or heading up, the now
defunct “A” Battery. That organisation
was pushed off the earth in a sudden spasm
of retrenchment. After the S’African
squabble, the Airey family migrated to the
Transvaal, and went on the veldt with agri-
cultural implements, but I think the imple-
ments were better than the veldt, and the
African farm was a great deal worse than
the new African farmer.
Concerning a recent party at Baumann’s,,
given by merchant William Yinson Lee and
his Chinese wife, it’s curious to note how
many and varied are the roots of the family
tree in the land of the dragon and fan. Yin-
son Lee, born in Australia, has only spent
nine years out of it, in the home of the tea
leaf. He is officially known in those parts
as Li Wai Lam. He is a Highland cousin,
or something less remote, of the late Li
Hung Chang, and he cut his teeth as ward
of a gentleman of Hongkong, whose name,
Wei Yuk, was heavily defended by the titles
Hon., C.M.G., and M.L.C. Merchant Lee
did an exploration sprint to the Paracel
Islands with Admiral Li, but is otherwise
content to navigate the Milson’s Point tram
service. He married a Honolulu merchant's
daughter in 1905, and intends to live happy
ever after.
Mr. Richard Brasch has just scaled my
eyrie to say he’s engaged to the girl of his
heart, and her name is Miss Thelma Trador,
whose father was formerly Postmaster in
his Majesty’s stamp and swear department,
Maitland.
The Littlejohn evening party, at the Rose
Bay Golf House, the other night, was chiefly
remarkable for its floral outbreak in salmon-
pink and crimson, and the superior quality
of the band music. It was one of those
dark, wet nights that calendars and
municipal councils describe as “moonlight”
nights when there is something in the
heavens like a brownish phantom at the
back of a horn lantern, and the belated
traveller in remote suburbs explores his
nearest street, gutter or drain-pipe with the
light of a wax vesta. “The Blue Danube,”
“Carmen” extracts, and other joyous sounds,
were caged into the Golf House with a high
wall of tarpaulin, and the Golf Club gas-
meter took the place of the moon.
The engagement of Mr. Neil McEacliarn,
only son of the wealthy shipping knight Mal-
colm of this side, to Miss Marie Raphael
(London) is one of the gilded alliances that
eventually keep the wolf from the step with
a quarter of a million of money. Knight
Malcolm left coffers heavily ballasted with
gold when he crossed the bar, and made his
tair-liaired dame a widow. Henry Raphael,
the girl’s late grandfather, is mentioned as
having been one of the Thames encamp-
ments richest financiers,” and probably
lent, his aid at more than two per cent.,
with a neat margin for profit. The bride-
elect is likely to add something considerable
to the McEacharn money vaults, and any fear
of this particular capitalist being taxed off
the business map is a remoter contingency
than the next look-in of Halley’s comet.
Major George Wynne, surrounded by a
warlike display of brass band and smart-
looking Coronating cadets, marched up
George-street on Saturday morning, very
brown and brisk, with no outward sign of
wasting away in consequence of strained re-
marks on this, that and the other torn
tatter of red tape.
In spite of black-edged rumors to the con-
trary, the Durbar diamond display seems
t°. be a fixture that can be backed to tran-
spire at an early date. Mr. and Mrs. J. O.
. air fax are, therefore, amongst many other
inhabitants of this township, packing’ up for
“ e Indian fray; and the lady’s mother,
Mrs. Hixson, is also getting her bonnet-
box fatted up for the great elephant spree.
Last year the (then) newly-fledged Soci-
ety ot Women Painters gave its first show
somewhere in the region of York-street’s
sottgoods selection. It was a poor show,
with a few gold specks amid the mullock of
its drab mediocrity. Some of the mediocrity
was more than drab, and wanted renovating
with an axe. A large following of friends,
enemies and relatives, however, conspired
together and encouraged the infant society
to go on hanging itself. But if the 1910
effort started in the cellar, the 1911 show has
climbed up several flights of stairs, and bears
no outward resemblance to its shabby ances-
tor. The ordinary traveller takes the lift to
the R.A.S. rooms (76 Pitt-street).
Artist Florence Rodway throws a streak
or genius into her batch of pictures in this
display of women painters’ brush work.
Hie. Japanese Screen” is a weird com-
bination of a small Spanish face and a
heathen screen, so cleverly treated that it
would make the name of an unknown
painter; but Florence has been doing big
things for some time, and her name is
known already.
The recent sitting-down of artist Ethel
Stephens in Etaples and other foreign paint-
ing places, has lifted her work right up
aloft out of the still-life ruck of a few years
back. A group of pictures, including the
‘Steepest Street in Montreuil,” is worthy of
a woman who is dashing forward with easel
and brush. The red roofs of Montreuil dangle
near Edith Cusack’s prime lambs, and are
only a few frames away from Amy Yale’s
inflammatory red bricks and blazing green
shutters.
Bessie Pring modestly asks only five
guineas for “Nature’s Gilding,” though the
timber alone—a well-grown herd of poplars,
just losing their summer skirts in frost-
bitten shades of russet and red—is worth
more than that, but not for the woodheap.
Gladys Owen’s clever “Vallambrosa” is
hung in the odor of sanctity, being almost
adjacent to Winnie Elliot’s plunge in church
architecture with a “Timber Yard” (North
Sydney) to keep the building boom to the
fore. Nell Rodd’s series of illustrations
(water colors) of the evergreen fairy tale,
Snowdrop,” are full of youth, imagination,
good drawing and charming color. Violet
Teague’s “Artemis in the Drawing Room” is
overcrowded with a large, wooden-looking
beauty in fashionable evening undress. The
lady occupies a strongly fortified position
between a table and some potted magnolias,
and is snatching 40 winks with both hands.
