The yachting season opened with a large
wind ;and a garden party at Kirribilli, in
the R.S.Y. squadron’s club grounds. These
grounds slope gently—with a big drop in the
middle—to the water’s edge, and any per-
son who feels that he prefers drowning to
garden-partying, can slip off the lower lawn
without annoying the water police. Lady
Denman, one gentleman help. Lady King-
Hall, with a Cocked Hat-in-waiting, Mr.
Alfred Milson, who is something of import-
ance amongst white wings, Mrs. Alfred Mil-
son —indefatigable in a good cause —and a
generous consignment of “Society” arms,
legs, etc (the wind rather insisted on the
presence of the legs), in a top-dressing of
spring chiffons, were bidden to the land and
water party by Mr. James and Mrs. Fairfax.
Bird Day preened its plumage in N.S.W.,
for the first time, on October 11. The as-
tounded soarrow was then hand-fed with
fragments from school lunch-bags, and kin-
dergarten exercises included vows of total
abstinence from the delirious joy of bird-
nesting. willy-wagtailing and similar juven-
ile crimes. The infant mind lias been
tuned up to see feathers in a new form, and
to _ observe the Australian warbler, or mere
chirruper or whistler, as not necessarily
contraband of war. Perhaps some small,
reformed pirate, who acquired bird-lore, in
song, from Miss Simpson, at Blackfriars
Kindergarten or the Tech. College, may be
a budding Gould or an immature Audubon.
Sterne’s starling and Poe’s raven, like
Shelley’s skylark, are immortals, and the
Australian kookaburra is now waiting for
hit; poetic perch amongst the gods. Also, the
emu is looking for an allotment on the
ground floor of immortality. In the mean-
time, BGd Day in N.S.W. has robbed even
mgeon-pie of its flavor.
Prof. MacCallum’s recent lecture on
Elizabeth Barrett Browning, to the Sydney
Lm.’s blue-stocking brigade, put a spark of
new life into the Browning ashes. Eliza-
HH I Barrett, who “wrote of Virtue with a
V, ate and drank Greek, read Homer in
the original, was weary of life at 39, lived
on air-cushions, etc., and digested Hebrew
in the raw, “from Genesis to Malachi,” was,
in spite of her genius, one of the most unex-
pected objects of honey-sweet love trills from
a male songster that judicious editors have
ever potted for posterity.
The Girls’ Realm Guild chased its last
farthing into an audited account the other
evening, and served up the same to Lady
Denman behind a strong encampment of
arum lilies in the King’s Hall. Seaforth
Highlander Captain Stewart Richardson was
the Feather Duster in attendance on her
Ex., but luckily left his great, plumed toque
at home, tied up in moth balls, or similar
fortification against the wild animals that
chew up this kind of foliage.
Organising sec. Grace Burrows has a
trick of business habit that boils down an
annual meeting into mere tabloids. At
five past 9. the last Trust Fund penny had
been laid in the dust. Lady Denman’s
wheels had been ordered for a later hour, so
the Highland raider had to snare cab-rank
cattle, and her Ex. bounded home in a han-
som., The roof _of the A.D.C. fconveyance
didn’t need taking down for repairs, the
ostrich-farm headgear (already mentioned)
having absented itself from the Girls’ Guild
corroboree.
The Sydney Jumble depot, started just for
love by two girls amongst the Surry Hills
—take the Crown-street tram—gathers up
a vast number of fat sacks full of the clothes
the prosperous girl is tired of. The damsel
of means, who belongs to the Girls’ Realm,
is not only pleased to contribute her cash,
half-worn garments, etc., to the Jumble
sales, but purloins her father’s boots (when
she thinks he’s worn them long enough),
demands her brother’s shirts, collars, socks,
etc., and doesn’t leave him a stud to call his
own if she can get hold of it.
The enthusiasts who stand for hours, as
saleswomen, at the Surry Hills depot (again
for love), retail these clothes at nominal
prices. An undertaker recently drove his
Stygian four-horse chaise up to this place of
roaring business, and, for sixpence, bought a
serious-looking top-hat. (Some girl had ob-
viously torn this garment from her parent’s
garden-party millinery.) The Jumble depbt,
which “always has a sale,” is now putting
on considerable frill, for, in spite of its
alarming sacrifices, it cleared £l5 out of
last year’s clothes’ bags. This sum went
into the balance with which the Girls’ Realm
is prepared to face 1912, on behalf of dis-
tressed damsels.
“Carmen” was responsible for the arrival,
at Her Majesty’s, of the inmates of both
Government Houses, the other night; and
they were ushered in to their boxes by a
double-barrelled blare of “God oave.” The
Denmans filled two boxes with their
presence and their staff. The cheerful look-
ing Gee-Gee was heavily bandaged with his
large blue sash of state. Lady Denman,
clothed in raven hue, had left her diamond
mine under lock and key, and only flashed a
pair of jewelled wings on her fair locks.
The sweet chirrup of soprano Jeanne
Wayda as Tosca is hard pressed for breath-
ing time when Scarpia-Scandiani sets his
red heels clicking across the supper room in
the second act of “La Tosca.” In act one,
Polish Wayda puts on a grey gown—some-
thing alluring in a soft shimmer, flings a
cerise scarf, a mere cloud of glory, across
her pretty shoulders, and puts her corn-
colored hair into the bonnet —the one which
explains in its appearance how it came about
that this form of headgear was once con-
sidered the last word in fascination. Its
shell is cottage bonnet. Its trimmings are
in marble palace, or old Versailles, or Vienna
court fashion, and they flaunt a distracting
flutter of white plumes that go to the head
of Cavaradossi McCormack, and put him off
his painting. This sweet bonnet is put to
bed before the supper sceae, and Wayda
gives Scarpia his dessert —on the blade of a
knife —in a white satin gown with a frosty
white glitter in its chest measurement. Scar-
pia dies to delightful music, in great agony.
In a first-night audience, at “Tosca,” Dr.
Stewart McKay was in his usual box.
Mr. J. S. Brunton was in his accustomed
patch, a yard or so away from the Angelini
baton. Mrs. Fred Orr showed a trail of
white blossom in her well-groomed locks, a
stall or two behind, and Mrs. Jamieson and
Mrs. Macdonald —also stalled —exploited
the egret’s tail. One lady’s osprey dropped
down dead on top of her head, while the
other lady’s black tail stood straight up on
end. Melba, not looking quite so well as on
the Sabbath morn when she hopped off the
southern express, was in the manager’s box.
In this box, Chamberlain Nevill, the pretty
profile of fair-haired Lady Barttelot, and
the early Roman matron deportment of Jus-
tice Geoi'ge Bowen Simpson’s dame, came
between the side seats and the dead body of
McCormack in the final tragedy. Mr. and
Mrs. Will McDonald were in their usual
first-night perch—front seats in the dress
circle.
Mrs. Anthony Hordern and Mrs. Taylor-
Young, incurable first-nighters, are also
faithful to the rather snubbed D.C. At Her
Majesty’s, the D.C. and the front rows of the
gallery are as superior to the stalls for oper-
atic purposes, as the eastern gallery of the
Town hall is better than the dreary obses-
sion of front seats at any big orchestral con-
cert. So the music-loving Shepherd Laid-
leys—father, mother and pretty black-eved
daughter—have stuck to their first-night
allotment in the D.C. right through the
present flutter of singing birds.
Ola Humphrey and her Egyptian prince
have parted hurriedly, for the same fierce
reason that rages round so many matri-
monial quarrels—“incompatibility of tem-
per.” Even pale sienna is several shades too
dark to run in double harness with white,
and the gentleman from Cairo, recently
billed as “cousin to the Khedive,” may toddle
back to the Nile as the cousin of no one in
particular, without any serious damage to
the scenery.
. When tenor John McCormack gets off
his singing perch, he’s a confirmed cricketer
and scatters a hurried ball over Rushcutter’s
Bay oval, or some other handy grass-plot,
with Charlie Macartney and Victor
Trumper. McCormack is suspected of har-
boring a secret notion that he is teaching
Trumper how to dodge duck-eggs.
The return of the crinoline has been
threatened for some months, and the Cold
Land press is now wiping its inky brow in
some alarm in consequence, for the tube
skirt was the only good fit for the tube train
in which a percentage of Londoners bore
their way to business. The old globular
offender tried to sneak in as “La Hoop,” but
the male critic ran it to earth in Bond-street
in one glance, and shrieked for help to the
venerable Lady Dorothy Nevill, who had
once worn this wild beast, and knew its
tricks, signs and earmarks. Lady Dorothy
waves the crinoline to perdition as “ridi-
culous.”
May you remain unhooped for the term of
your natural life, my dear Arini.

Chunder Loo,
Of Akim Foo,
Down the hall goes
Skating too.
Not a scratch
He leaves behind
That a microscope
Could find.
Smiling always—
Thin and tall,
Swiftly up and down
The hall.
Of his skates
He’s very fond,
And the safe old
Polish Pond.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, October 16, 1911.
My Dear Tarrie, —
The string of motors that brought the
guests to the Brett-Young wedding festivi-
ties, on Thursday, was long enough to con-
siderably hamper the ordinary traffic outside
Menzies’. The marrying was got through at
Cairns Memorial Church ; but the subsequent
reception occupied several hours at the tall
hotel, the lounge of which was decorated
with wedding bells, pink ribbons and roses.
The leading people sat alongside a crosswise
table on the platform behind the wedding
cake. Speeches were numerous, and several
women singers, amongst whom was Maggie
Stirling, poured out song for the refresh-
ment of the guests, who ted and listened at
small tables. The bridesmaids, in pink and
white, looked like cherry trees in blossom;
and the fair bride scored a heap of points
with her simple, well-cut dress and tulle mob
cap and veil. Mr. and Mrs.. Young took a
regretful farewell of their last daughter, the
others, with their respective husbands, being
present as guests. Dr. Brett had brother
medico Tucker to best-man him, and at
least 50 other medicine men came along to
drink his health, and remark that he was a
jollv good sawbones. The bride went away
in smart grey apparel, and under a well-
feathered hat; but she smuggled on a crush
hat and a coat on the way down to the sta-
tion, so that the crowd at the Sydney ex-
press might give their attention to somone
else. The busy bridegroom just managed to
see his patients, and get away from the last
case in time to get to church.
The long list of recent Toorak marryings
has been further lengthened by the wedding
of Herbert Westcott, of Westralia, and Jessie
McCallum, daugnter of Major McCallum.
The Major spent his days of peace in gar-
nering oil from the eucalyptus near Dim-
boola. He now lives close to Melbourne,
when he is not busy with horses at Wilson’s
Promontory.
The fair young widow Butler, whose
mourning only serves to make her more at-
tractive, is recovering from her most
recent motor accident, in time to get her
Cup frocks. Her casualty record so far is
three broken ribs and one ditto arm, with
an encore fracture of the same limb.
The eldest of the John Grice gii-leens
nearly completed a tragedy in high life last
week, by tripping during an exciting game
of tennis. She fell on the asphalt court,
with such force that she sustained slight
concussion, and had to be put to bed. The
patient is now convalescing at the family
mansion.
Mrs. Frankie Oliver Stuart Murray is
giving a dance at the St. Kilda Town Hall,
on Oaks Night. There are two Mrs. Stew-
art Murrays in society—as all the world of
Melbourne doesn’t know. One. Mrs.
Frankie Oliver, lives at South Yarra, and is
unrelated to tbe dance hostess, who dwells
at Sandi-ingham (where the golfer smites
and pursues, the small white globule). Mrs.
Frankie Oliver, etc., is a relative of the
Western District Robertsons. Lorpa Rob-
ertson colls her aunt.
In December or January next, the Lan-
dale-Clarke wedding will come to pass at
Sunbury. The marriage feast is to be
spread in the ancestral halls of Ruperts-
wood, which is now really Russel’s Wood,
the Clarke of that name having purchased
the family mansion from his baronet brother.
The Grey-Egerton-Clarke-Cumming lady
has cancelled her proposed trip to Australia,
but the youngest daughter, it seems, is to
make her debut in her native land, under
the wings of Sister Phyllis • Power and
Grandmamma Cumming. Sister Phyllis has
booked Steeplechase Sunday for her turn as
hostess.
The Victorian Artists’ management is
sticking to the more accessible, but less pic-
turesque, Athenaeum Hall for this year’s
show, in preference to the rooms on Eastern
Hill. The old haunt, besides two good rooms,
had odd lengths of corridor and stair land-
ings, where you could loiter and yarn cosily
with friends and be out of the picture-
gazer’s way. The Athenaeum merely offers
four walls. The men on the hanging com-
mittee who had “big” pictures have got good
positions; but there is considerable wailing
from other exhibitors. This hanging com-
mittee has certainly earned a vote of censure
by putting all the miniatures within a foot
of the platform surface. Enthusiasts could
examine them well by going down on then-
hands and knees, or lying flat on their
chests; but, somehow, people dislike doing
either of these things in public—unless, of
course, they are being presented to Royalty.
All the Governors missed the chance of
opening the show, G.-G. Denman being in
Sydney, and sub-Excellency Fuller in the
Wimmera, receiving mayoral medals at Nhill
and other distant parts. So Speaker
McDonald, of the Federal House, who is
himself something of an amateur with brush
and palette, passed a few appropriate re-
marks to the crowd that flocked round the
paintings.
The tea-and-scone industry is flourishing
these days, and the bunshop bosses can
afford to turn down good temporary stands.
Tne Victorian artists offered the balcony
landing as a good place to catch the thirsty
picture gazer on tne hop; but not one food-
ery chief would bring along her boiler, staff
and crockery. Such a chance would have
caused a stampede in the old days, and
cream puffs would have been trampled to
death; but the times have changed, and the
hot pie is a scarce animal. The independence
of the cake specialists is matched by that of
the furniture hirers, who intimated that they
could not spare furnishings for a whole fort-
night. “A day or two” was the limit they
offered. However, Mrs. McCubbin, who is a
capable person, rose to the occasion. Within
an hour or so, she had discovered and
dredged some chairs from the depth of the
Athenaeum Club, ordered cakes and tea,
found attendants for herself, and started a
brisk business.
At the St. Kilda Town Hall, last week, the
Misses Hyams gave their annual grown-up
pupils’ dance. Some 400 advanced students
of toe-culture were in the frisk, clothed in
fancy dress or otherwise. Two of the sets
were refreshingly original. One lot wore
small monoplanes on their heads; the other
party looked fresh and fetching in white
boating costumes and cornflower hats. A
French cotillon and a grand march pro-
vided entertainment for balcony-gazers. At
supper, the senior pupils presented the
Hyams firm with an oak writing desk. Coun-
cillor Stapley, a tall, dark man remarkably
like Marshall-Hall, handed over the furni-
ture on behalf of the grateful two-steppers.
At the Arts and Crafts Club, the other
Tuesday, the visitors found enamels and
basket-work bidding for attention, with bits
of sculpture. Energetic Margaret Basker-
ville gave an absorbing demonstration of
modelling, and hung a cheeky little terra-
cotta head christened “Sue” on the wall for
general admiration. A number of Carmichael
lace models served as a reminder of the cap-
able Excellency’s useful and beautiful hobby.
The enamels on view are partly of local
origin and partly old examples which have
been lent by various artistic people. Among
the curious is an inkstand, centuries old,
which would infuse inspiration into a care-
worn poet even on washing day. Some
baskets woven by Dorothea Adams are quite
as nice as any Maori or other work I have
seen. A race-time exhibition is to be held,
and, at the end of November, a sale will
scatter most of the desirable things all
round the compass.
Dr. Karl Kumin is a disconcerting per-
son. His lecture posters show dreadful-
looking savages, and one of his latest successes
is a striped crocodile, such as convivialists
dream at the end of a Cup carnival. The
doctor can only see one way of escape from
the threatened Mohammedan deluge—that
is, to turn the fanatical hordes into Chris-
tians. This is a large order; but, even if it
could be done, it wouldn’t do away with holy
warring; it would merely transform the
harmless vestment-and-candle squabbles into
tremendous upheavals, for the" brown person
takes his religion seriously, and would not
be satisfied with ecclesiastical pillow fights.
Another charity amateur theatrical per-
formance, with Lesly Madden at the steering
desk, is looming on the horizon. The com-
pany is the same that successfully played
“The Liars” and “The Case of Rebellious
Susan,” except that the Vallanges will be
absent this time. Mrs. Yallange was good
from an amateur standpoint, and will be
missed ; but her spare husband just walked
through his parts as if somebody had left
the gate open, and he had blown in. The co.
now rehearsing includes among its stars
artist Bunny’s sister, Mrs. Donald McKin-
non, the two Madden maidens and Marjorie
and Athol Levey. The men are Lawyer
Hector Macdonald, Judge Holroyd’s barris-
ter son, “Tommy” Cochrane, Peter Schuler
and one of the Grice clan. This time they
are to attempt Oscar Wilde’s “The Import-
ance of Being Earnest,” and the effort will
be made at the Royal, on Friday (27th).
Harry Hill is producing the play, and the
proceeds are to be poured into the money
boxes of the Prahran Crhche and Toorak
Kindergarten. Secretary Lesly Madden, or
any of the performers, will supply intending
witne c, '-~~ with tickets on application.
Caulfield Guineas proved a brisk day soci-
ally. Sassiety lias not quite emerged from
the burrow where it slept away the winter,
but it is rapidly getting ready for flight (the
burrow figure of speech doesn’t quite seem
to fit here), and dressmakers’ lives are be-
ginning to be burdens. A few wise virgins
and dames have their Cup clothes all ready
and packed away in tissue-paper wrappings
before Guineas Day: but. these are scarce
in the land. The great majority make a wild
sprint of it. There were a few advance bows
visible on Saturday—amazing things that
stood up from the summits of conical hats,
or swung like loose aeroplane wings from
flat roofs.
Two bright yellow travelling gas engines
in the motor paudock showed that the Ful-
ler Government House was represented. The
visitors they had oiled out with were mostly
men, and they troubled the reserved pen very
little. Occasionally, a top-hatted aide flut-
tered in feverishly through force of habit,
lingered a moment, and departed in an
absent-minded way. Governor Fuller came
along without his lady, and wandered between
the paddock and the bookmakers, when he
was not amongst the black frock coats and
race glasses on the lawn. The John Mad-
dens looked in at the vice-regal coop now and
then. The Chief Justicess wore a black hat
over a very white face; but John beamed
happily, and had a red rose in his button-
hole. The revival of the buttonhole is re-
joicing the hearts of the old beaux, but the
younger generation follows the fashion re-
luctantly. Ernest Brooks was a gracious
spectacle in pale dove-grey clothes and a
cornflower.
Nowadays the fashionables who do not
sport a bow the size of a yacht’s mainsail
get the showy effect by a much more expen-
sive method— i.e., by supporting an immense
ostrich feather. Mrs. Prout Webb, in purple,
was visible beneath an out-size specimen.
The pretty little Evans girl, who is just
back from somewhere or other, wore a black
linen dress and hat, and had her lovely au-
burn hair done in tight, shining rolls. Mrs.
Phyllis Reggie Power’s hat was decorated
with pink bows that made one think of the
spring poet’s dairymaid, and the Frank Mad-
den lady was gowned impressively in black
with a wonderful network of black velvet on
the front.
H. B. Irving glode about the lawn, and
managed to look almost like an everyday
racegoer under the influence of a win on
Malt King, but his remarkable clothes saved
him from becoming an undistinguished ac-
tor. His coverings seemed to belong to the
Dickens or First Henry Irving period, and his
collar looked like a combination of the Glad-
stone and King Charles styles. He con-
versed long and earnestly with Dorothea
Baird Irving, who wore a pretty hat
wreathed with violets, and a voluminous
black cloak. Essie Perrin appeared in a
natty white linen rig-out, with a tight, round
turban set on her fair hair. The weather
did its level best to give both linens and furs
a fair show with alternate sun baths and
cold breezes. Tussores won in a canter
standing up equally well against heat and
cold. Mrs. Dr. Joyce wore a fine specimen
of the tussore order, and conversed with a
tea-party while hubby was away mending
Horizon's injured jockey.
Jester Bert Gilbert has had to put by the
cap and bells for a while, one eye having re-
fused duty. It is now obscured by bandages;
but the other shines gaily and works over-
time to make up for its tired brother. The
doctor has recommended rest and quiet, and
the stage humorist is getting it somewhere
on the Blue Mountains.
The Quamby Club gave Dorothea Baird
Irving muffins and strawberries last week.
Some members swatted over their Shaks-
peares for days beforehand, in order to be
able to thrust and parry conversationally
with the guest. Evidently, they labored under
the delusion that Mr. aud Mrs. Irving earn
their living by playing “Ilamlet,” and hor-
rors of that sort, rai her than "The Bells,”
“The Lyons Mail." XL” and the like.
One evening last week I stumbled over a
small, but select body weltering in its intel-
lect at the rooms of the Australian Litera-
ture Society. The body had roiled up to
hear Willie Moore, a leading local disciple of
“Art for Art’s Sake” discourse on “Local
Color in our Art aud Literature.” Willie
said he objected to the prevailing note of
gloom in Australian literature, and made an
appeal for more sunshine. lie also implored
Australian artists to cease painting pictures
of “Cousin Nellie in an Armchair.” After-
wards, poet Bernard O'Dowd made a few
impassioned remarks: and Dr. Mclnerney,
and artists McCliutock, McCubbin and Shir-
low put a bead ou the pint pot of eloquence.
All those present tried to look as if they
understood every word, but I went away
agreeing with W. J. Locke’s observation,
made in his latest novel, that “Art is long,
but talk about it is longer.”
Esta d’Argo and Ben Taffy Davies met a
large audience with a strong leaven of wildly
enthusiastic Welshmen at the Town Hall,
on Saturday night. The concert opened with
the inevitable piano solo. Edward Goll,
despite his melodious growth of hair, seemed
rather uncertain in his attack on the box of
music, and the seriously critical section of
the crowd might have complained that he
varied his Chopin, Brahms and Liszt per-
formances too much as regards time. The
accompaniments were entrusted to William
Conway.
Davies, from behind a broad fortress of
glittering white shirt-front and waistcoat,
warbled “Waft Her Angels ” and a recita-
tive of Handel’s with mild effect, but warmed
up considerably with three Schubert songs.
The house rose at Mm and insistently de-
manded more; whereupon the capricious
tenor sang of Maud and the garden whence
the black bat Night had aviated. Esta
d’Argo, who used to be Hetty llolroyd, is the
same handsome dark srirl as of old, with a
methodically trained voice. It is pleasing,
but would not burn the Thames to the
ground. She was clothed like a delectable
strawberry ice. Some wonderful lace was
moulded on squashed raspberry pink silk,
with a flounce of rich Devonshire cream
satin. Her little songs from Schumann,
Haydn and Herman Lohr won carefully
modulated applause from myself, but two ex-
tracts from Verdis “Ernani” were brilli-
antly sung. The soprano finally won her
audience and about two barrowloads of vege-
tables with a duet, “O Wondrous Joy,” with
tenor Davies.
“The Chocolate Soldier,” at the Royal,
was partly surrounded by a regiment of
Irishmen on Saturday night, the Celtic Club
having monopolised circle and boxes for the
theatre party tendered to the Irish envoys.
The visitors are fine-looking, serious young-
men, with thoughtful brows and curly hair,
and the “young” party in Melbourne enthuses
over their very modern views and calm,
business-like methods. “They never mention
Parnell or the old gags,” I heard one man
explaining to a friend.
Quite a lot of citizens have had their faces
scarified by hatpins without anything being-
done; but, recently, a careless damsel with
steel porcupines in her hair had the bad
luck to furrow the face of a Board of Health
officer. The result is that war has been de-
clared by Dr. Ham, chief of the Depart-
ment. There is one possible remedy that I
can see for the hatspear danger. Every
trinket-seller in town has lots of “shields”
the small, neat, metal catches : that clip
over the points of the head bayonets. They
are to be had for 6d. apiece, and, if every
man bought two and presented them to his
best girl with the request to wear them for
his sake, she might do so, and thereby dodge
the wrath of Ham. Most probably, though,
she wouldn’t wear them. Or she would
accept and lose them witn her usual promp-
titude. So Dr. Ham might as well think out
a by-law and a suitable penalty.
Talking of poor Willie Judkins, a Mel-
bourne stockbroker remarked that the first
syllable of the sick man’s name was pro-
bably an incentive to Christian endeavoring.
The man of stocks spoke pensively of a Jud-
fieui and a Judleigh who gave him Sunday-
school instruction in his days of comparative
innocence. They also were called respect-
ively, “Jud” and “Juddy,” for short, and
they prided themselves on being part-
namesakes of Yankee missionary Jud-
son—Adoniram Judson —who took the
Bible to Burmah a hundred years ago,
and translated Holy Writ into Burmese.
Adoniram, it seems, stood the local climate
and diseases pretty well, but it killed two of
his devoted wives in succession, after which
he married a third. He was the Grand Old
Jud of the American Baptist persuasion.
From my Adelaide correspondent: —
Politicians foregathered at St. Peter’s, last week,
for the wedding of Minister of Education Coneybeer’s
daughter. The toast-list looked as much like a Parli-
amentary notice paper as anything else. The bride
had to listen to 11 speeches before she escaped on her
honeymoon.
Another wedding of the week was that of Miss
Annie Sandford, of Fitzroy, to Mr. Hayward, whose
business it is to send messages on their way for the
Eastern Extension Co.
The Woman’s Rights movement continues to forge
firmly ahead. A suburban hotel, recently renovated,
bears this legend: “Private Entrance; also Ladies’
Bar.”
Derby Day (Saturday) was a dreary affair. The
weather was mournful, and racegoers put on their
most archaic clothes, and looked mournful too. The
big race of the day fell to a comparative outsider in
Sanskrit, owned by Dr. A. H. Bennett. Ladara sup-
plied the biggest bleat of excitement by giving
backers £2O 6s. for second place in the Morphett-
ville Plate.
On Wednesday things looked up, and the vice-regal
box held Lady and Miss Bosanquet, as well as the
men of the household. Mr. A. P. Bowman secured a
double win with The Greek and Monarch.
The latest engagement is that of Leslie Bickford
to Miss Hope Murray. The Murrays are squatters;
the Bickfords do not require to work either, and have
been among the mainstays of our Hunt Club for ages.
“Manufacturers’ Week” was responsible for some
saucy window displays. A big drapery firm made a
specialty of feminine underwear of the most gorgeous
variety. One result was a masculine crowd that
threatened to block the traffic. A patriot of the
grocer breed filled his window with S. Australian
ham and bacon, the which he lavishly decorated with
festoons of roses and colored electric light. It looked
like a Mayor’s ball gone mad.
At present on their way to Colombo are the Frank
Irwins, while the Alfred Simpsons are thinking of
booking their return passages. Miss Simpson set out
for Westralia, but, on getting so far, she found the
call of palms and the tropics too strong, so she went
on. Mr. “Bob” and Miss Addie Baker have booked
passages for England by an early boat, and on
Monday a few of Mr. Baker’s friends travelled to
Glenelg, where, at the invitation of the Law Smiths,
they’d a chance of bidding him “good-bye.”
The Society of Arts embroglio still rages —if an
embroglio can be said to rage. The peg on which the
squabble is hung—if a squabble can be said to hang
—is apparently Miss Rose MacPherson, though why
any Art Society shouldn’t jump at the chance of get-
ting her as a member is a mystery. Pictures which
the local Show refused were hung in Paris and else-
where ; but that's a detail!
Political feeling was suspended for the moment
when the news of the death of Representative Batch-
elor came through. He was widely respected and
liked by all parties, sects and creeds.
Amongst those who have booked passages to leave
us are the Stockbridges. About a year ago he arrived
from to help in the making of our trams.
” We’re sad at the temporary loss of the Gordons.
The Judge’s work on the Sugar Commission will keep
him in Melbourne for some time, and his family in-
tends to share his Siberian exile. They are now under-
stood to be plodding over the trackless, snow-clad
wastes that lead to Collins-street, where there are
Cossacks and bears on the horizon.
Wayside advertisers have a deal to put up with
from the cheap humorist. Travellers are puzzled by
frequent intimations that “All things perish—Sinners
last for ever; “Sinners lead the way,” etc. At
last light dawns, and it is seen that these are merely
advertisements for a certain brand of sewing-machine,
some wildly mirthful person having dug out the cen-
tral “g” and replaced it with an additional “n.”

O.T. LIMITED.
An action was concluded in the First Civil Court,
Melbourne, on 11th October, before Mr. Justice
Hodges, in which O.T. Limited sued J. B. McArthur,
licensee of Hosie’s Hotel, Elizabeth-street, on the
ground that defendant's servants had passed off
cordials not of the company’s manufacture as being
O.T. The defence was a denial of the offence. Mr.
Duffy, K.C., and Mr. Starke (instructed by Mr. F.
B. Waters) appeared for plaintiff company, and Mr.
McArthur and Mr. Cussen (instructed by Messrs.
Iledderwick, Fookes and Altson) for the defendant.
Evidence having been given on both sides, his Honor
said that as soon as it was clear that the evidence
of the witnesses who were employed for the purpose
of finding out whether other liquid was being passed
off for O.T. was to be believed, it was apparent that
plaintiff must succeed. The evidence, which had
been most fairly given, was enough to show that
there had been an intention on the part of one of
defendant’s employees to pass off 0.K.0. as 0.T., and
he did this on more than one occasion. Judgment
was given for plaintiff, with costs, and an injunction
was granted restraining defendant, his servants and
agents, from passing off as O.T. any liquid not of
plaintiff’s manufacture.*

WITH INDIGESTION.
♦
A Constant Sufferer tells how
She was Cured*
After suffering severe attacks of Indiges-
tion, after almost every meal for years, Mrs.
M. Shorter, of Toowoomba, Queensland,
tried the tonic treatment, which aims at in-
creasing the strength of the stomach so that
it can digest food properly. Her experience
was remarkable, for sue found speedy relief
and can now, for the tirst time in man)
years, eat an ordinary meal with comfort.
Mrs. Shorter, who lives in Mort Street,
stated : , . , ...
“For many years I had been troubled witn
Indigestion. As sure as 1 took a meal i
would get a suffocating sort of pain in my
chest almost directly after, and I would tee
a weighty pain right through to my shouldei
blades. I would be quite weighed down with
it, and hardly able to breathe. I could not
bear my clothes to be at all tight on me. T
never could find any appetite for fear ol in-
digestion coming on after. I could hard!)
bear to even cook the food, the smell would
upset me. I had such dreadful headaches,
too. All my head seemed whirling, and tne
ache would press over my eyes till 1 could
hardly see. I would be afraid to stoop, tor
the blood would rush to my head and make
me dizzy. I turned awfully yellow and
sallow. Sometimes, too, a regular mist
would come over my eyes. I would wake “P
every morning with a sour, acid taste tnat
stayed on me for hours, and my tongue vas
always furry and dry. My system would(be
full of wind. All the time I would be des
pondent and wretched, but I made “P 1 u
mind to give Dr. Williams Pink lills
trial, and since I finished the course ®*
health has been splendid. Now I can eat
anvthing I fancy, and the constant load and
the weariness have all gone. My colo
come back, and I feel a different being alto
ge Don’t let any dealer talk you into taking
something which he says is Just as good.
If you are pestered to take a substitute,
send 2s. 9d. for one box, or 15s. 3d. for six
boxes to the Dr. Williams’ Medicine Co. ot
Australasia Ltd., Sydney.

POLITICAL
POINTS
BIGJOHN FORRESTS BACK AGAIN.
The Aft. 8 ½in. gauge seems out of all proportion.
“Adenoid”: They now call it the Gibberal
Party.
Premier John Weary lias at last sagged
back to Victoria, and been interviewed. He
said (1) that something ought to be done
about the better distribution of Australian
produce in England; (2) ditto about ship-
ping matters ; (3) about immigration. Then
lie sat down heavily in his untidy clothes and
said that something really ought to be
done.
Lack of time was Acting-Premier’s Watt’s
excuse for refusing the request of Mel-
bourne’s “public” chauffeurs for a Wages
Board. By all accounts the youths in gog-
gles, leggings and determined countenances,
who drive taxis and other cars for hire, are
sweated most outrageously, their hours run-
ning up to 16, or even 20, a day, without any
Sunday off, and their wages running down
to 20s. a week. An instance is given of a
driver going for two days and a night with-
out taking his leggings off. The motor
seems a Juggernaut car with a vengeance—-
deadly alike to those who drive it and to
those over whom it is driven.
Item of evidence given before the N.S.W.
Public Works Committee :
If there were two or three ports away from Sydney,
they would not know the place in 15 or 20 years.
At present, Sydney Harbor was not constructed to
offer facilities to the shipment of heavy material, and
it was taxed now to cope with it. The Harbor was
certainly capable of being made to cope with all
classes of heavy shipping, but two more additional
ports along the coast for shipping purposes would
make a great difference. Once a jetty was put out
at Jervis Bay for shipping, the surrounding country
would go ahead very quickly. He had great hopes
for this district, and considered a railway would
open it up more quickly than some people thought.
The Vic. Railway Commissioners aren’t
exactly sweaters, but some of the economies
they 'practise might not satisfy a Wages
Board. Thus, the Government lately in-
structed them that, in future, Sunday work
must be paid for at the rate of time and a-
quarter. The Commissioners bowed, of
course, but they put their own interpreta-
tion on “Sunday work.” Thus, the crew of
a goods train that leaves Spencer-street for
the country at 11 p.m. on Saturday, gets
no time and a-quarter, even though the men
run the caravan around the Backblocks the
whole blessed Sabbath. Their train is called
the Saturday north-south-eastern-perpendicu-
lar goods, or whatever it may be, and, even
though it ran for a week on end, it would
still be the “Saturday N.-S.-E.-P. goods.”
Premier Verran lately told the junior
members of the S.A. civil service that his
ideal is a minimum wage of £2OO a year for
42 hours weekly. The vision was received
with enthusiasm. But unless Verran bor-
rows less and manages to pay for a few
more things out of revenue the reality, by-
and-bye, is more likely to be a reduction to
425. a week for 200 months, or the retrench-
ment of 200 civil servants after 42 years
service, or the offer of 2d. in the £ on a debt
of £42,000,000, the balance to stand for 200
years at two per cent. Or, again, it may be
that 42 months hence the maddened civil
service will give 200 cheers when it hears
that Verran has been thrown out. Or when
the bad times come, and the debt can’t be
renewed, the House may reduce Verran’s
own pay to £2OO, and do it by a majority of
42, after he has vainly endeavored to borrow
£200,000 at 4.2 per cent, to go on with.
The Fisher-Hughes Ministry already finds
that it made a mess of affairs over the Aus-
tralian capital. It hadn’t the courage to
do its duty to Australia, and insist on get-
ting a proper and sufficient area for the
Federal capital, and getting it in a suit-
able place, so that it could do some-
thing big in the matter of public ownership
of land and industries. Rather it did an
abject backdown to the Tory Wade and his
Private Enterprise gang, and took what they
were prepared to give. Now it is dodging
humbly round the offices of the State Min-
isters, begging for a little more elbow-room,
and some more water catchment area, and a
few other appurtenances of civilisation.
Neither of the two far-apart bits of land
into which the absurd capital is divided is
large enough for the purpose, a matter
which The Bulletin pointed out 100 times
over; but, unfortunately, Hughes was too
busy trying to placate the Sydney Tele-
Grapll to listen to any but Tory advice, and
Fisher doesn’t seem to count for much when
his dodgy lieutenant is around.
From S. M. Herald (7/10/’ll) :
Colonel Onslow said that the Liberal party did
hold a “caucus,” but when it came to every member
being bound to follow the decision of that caucus
that was another thing. (Applause.) Every member
of the Liberal party was free to vote against his party
if he wished. (Applause.)
William McMillan [speaking from the same plat-
form to the same gathering] said that in every con-
dition of life in which a man has to do his duty
there was one unforgivable sin—-disloyalty. (Hear,
hear.) Lord Palmerston had described an independent
man as a man who could not be depended on.
(Hear, hear.)
Liberal “applause” for Onslow’s yarn re
“every member of the Liberal party being
free to vote against his party if he wished” ;
and Liberal “Hear, hears” for McMillan’s
absolutely contrary sentiment that “an in-
dependent is a man who can’t be depended
on,” and the committer of an “unforgivable
sin.” You take vour choice as to whether
Onslow or McMillan is lying concerning the
attitude of Liberalism in the matter. As to
the rank idiocy of the “Liberal” crowd,
which cheered both views, there can, how-
ever, be no question.
From the S. M. Herald’s valedictory to
Egerton Lee Batchelor :
Mr. Batchelor was not only trained and ready for
responsibility. He was still an eager student of
public questions, and was earnest and enthusiastic in
his pursuit of knowledge and experience. This is
what counts most in public affairs as in private life.
We have no time to spare for the flabby personality.
What is needed everywhere is the grip of big things
and a lively sense of the importance of details. The
Labor party is a great forcing-bed for men of this
type. It evolves them in the early stages, and brings
them forward at a great pace in the later periods.
And, at election times, the Herald is accus-
tomed to voice the stock “Liberal” lie re
the independence-slaying, character-blight-
ing, personality-destroying effects of “caucus
domination” as strenuously as any other old
sheet.
Though hampered by constitutional re-
strictions, the Australian Government is still
trying its best to kill the infamous 400-lb.
wheat-sack, and has now about reached the
limits of its powers. First, the importation
of bags of a size to hold more than 3001 b. was
prohibited ; then the use of such heartbreakers
was forbidden in the import and export
trade ; and lately that regulation was applied
to the inter-State trade. The purely State
trade is beyond Commonwealth control. All
this bother and expense could have been
avoided by a simple regulation of the State
railway departments; forbidding the con-
veyance of inordinate sacks: but onlv South
Aus. though it worth while. The fact
that the Australian Parliament con-
demned big bags was enough to “nark” the
provincial Ministries into favoring or ig-
noring them. The State Frighters would
view the Angel Gabriel with disfavor if he
were a Federal Minister.
Unless t’otherside press is a prevari-
cator, Westralia’s Labor Premier Scaddan
is going to make a bad start. According to
the published yarn :
He emphatically [at the Perth Show] denied the
statement that the Labor Government would not be
in favor of the farmers. They intended to do all
they could to foster farming. It was intended to
raise money in London to assist farmers over the
present dry season.
Westralia’s old political crowd spent bor-
rowed money in “repairs to a glasshouse for
breeding pest-destroying parasites”; but
“tucker for drought-stricken farmers” will
look worse than even that uproarious entry
in the State’s Loan Account. What sort of
a permanent and reproductive undertaking
is the tucker-or other assistance which the
Government proposes to offer? And how
many immigrants does Scaddan reckon he
will attract by the announcement, in London,
that the State’s farmers are so hard up,
owing to drought, that they have to be fed
on the State’s I O IJ’s?
A PILLAR OF SOCIETY!
The president said the Federation saw in Mr.
Wade the emblem of honesty.... And the fact that
he stood for the interests of the masses made the
Employers’ Federation look up to him as a leader. —
News item from the Master Builders’ dinner.
N.S.W. lias been informed, at a meeting
of the Shopkeepers’ Defence Association, that
Mr. George Turner (whoever he may be)
and Mr. T. C. Bradley (whoever he may be)
are organising a new political party. Its
object is to fill Parliament with “men of
business capacity,” also “men who have
had experience in the diplomatic world.”
Seeing that Australia’s crop of diplomatists
is rather short this year, the shopkeepers will
probably have to fall back on “men of busi-
ness capacity.” And in the end that will
doubtless prove to mean the same old cus-
toms-dodger at the corner shop.
The N.S.W. Premier has been making
quite a loud fuss about the concentration of
the quarantine business at Sydney, whereby
a few disease germs may be thrown at the
city. Really, that is nothing, by comparison
with other possibilities. For instance, if the
naval station, naval arsenals and Federal
shipbuilding yards are crowded into Sydney,
a day may come when an enemy, in his
anxiety to destroy these properties, will
throw 75,000 shells at the place. Then con-
flagration will follow upon ruin, and pesti-
lence, by reason of the dead bodies lying
around, will come after, and there will in-
deed be strenuous times.
THE BLACK AND WHITE HOPE.
One of the few remaining footprints of the
late Thomas Bent is being wiped out. Vic-
torian Minister for Railways Billson is ex-
tinguishing all the disqualifications, forfei-
tures, discharges, losses of pension, etc., im-
posed on the railway strikers of 1903. In
other words, Billson, the undertaker, pro-
poses to bury the remaining bones of the sad
old episode and shake hands with all parties
concerned over the grave. So, if the peace-
makers are really blessed, Billson should
have a big pair of white wings in the Here-
after —though his name is a rather curious
one for an angel. Still, there are doubtless
angels named Smith.
SUCCESS!
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The intolerance displayed in the attempt by a minor-
ity to stifle free speech . . . contributed more to. the
success of the meeting . . . than could the most
strenuous and cogent arguments on the other side. —
Argus report of a meeting convened for the purpose
of disagreeing with the principle of preference to
unionists (3/10/’ll).
We love the loud hoof-thunder of the roaring Labor
gods
Who sit around, and splinter all our flowing periods.
We’re not prevaricating when we say that we rejoice
If shrieks of ribald laughter greet the tangled creeds
we voice.
When interjectors great and small get fairly on the
job,
And hunks of vulgar sound among our vocal bouquets
lob,
Are we down-hearted ? Are we glum and sunk in
grim distress ?
Not we ! This is Success !
Aye, we are pleased, indeed, to hear the screams of
mirth that rise
At every old-wife’s tale we tell. We love the noisy
cries
Of “Traitor!” “Yah!” “800-hoo!” and “Rats!”
that our remarks compel—
The loud, ferocious, frenzied howl —the long, wild,
strident yell.
We like it, when the sound of feet impedes our eager
speech,
And rude ejaculations greet the gospel-truths we
preach.
When bursts of wrath and angry groans our platform
efforts bless,
We recognise success !
When, north and south, disturbance reigns, and yells
rise east and west,
And one by one our speakers die away and give it
best,
When whisker after whisker waves, and sinks to rest
unheard,
And none but the reporters hear a single blessed
word —-
When each and ev’ry one of us, amid a noise im-
mense,
Has tried in vain to give, with bold, unbustled elo-
quence,
Ilis well-thought-out, most scholarly and masterful
address,
We are a great success!
When some heartbroken orator gives forth his painful
yelp
Of how the toiler really needs some soul-uplifting
help,
We thrill with joy if hoots and boots combine his
voice to drown....
“Shall we” (loud groans) “submit” (Hooray) “to
Labor’s yoke?” (Sid-down!)
“Shall we assist the tyrant” [cheers] “to trample on
our rights,
And Win the holy fights that anti-Labor” (laughter)
“fights?”
When frenziedly the meeting rises up to bellow
“Yes ! 1”
Ain’t we a huge success?
VICTORIA.
FRANK HENTY.

ordering an extra size in hats for itself, consequent
on the fact that, at the recent big Motor Car Show
at Sydney, its goods were fitted to 50 per cent, more
cars than the next build of tyre. Needless to state
we are referring to the Continental Tyre Co.*

SPORTING
NOTIONS
How the popularity of the Warrnambool
does last. This was the 15th of the series, and at-
tracted no less than 340 acceptances. No less than
42 inter-State riders were in the district 10 days
before the race, running over the road, spying out
the land generally, and getting acclimatised. Last
year’s blue ribbon went to the sturdy J. Pianta, of
Westralia, and the gentleman faced the music again
in very tough company. Naturally, he was on
scratch, and with him were J. McSweeney, also of
Westralia, T. J. Gascoyne, of N.S.W., P. O’Shea
and A. B. L. Smith, of Maoriland, and D. Kirkham, of
Victoria. This was bad enough, especially the
presence of Kirkham, who ran fastest in the Goul-
burn, and spun over the Vic. Cycle Traders’ Assoc.
100 miles in shr. smin. 55sec. on Saturday week.
And that was not all. On the 2min. mark was A.
Birch, of Maoriland, the man who did such a bat in
a race from Timaru to Christchurch, and was known
to be able to stand a fierce run, and then come
with a fearsome spurt. There were five others, all
speedy young men, on the same mark, so it was
obvious that the back-marker who ran through to the
front would have a rough contract to hold his own.
But, of all these, Kirkham was easily the most
fancied.
* # * *
But, as usual, when the numbers went up,
the back-markers never had a look in. The glorv
and the prizes fell to the middle distance gentrv, who
were strong enough to wear dowm the long-starters
and keep ahead of the back-markers. No less than
55 dropped out, and reduced the field to 281, which
was big enough in all conscience. Variable side
winds made riding uncomfortable at the start, but,
before a quarter of the journey was completed, the
wheelers found themselves struggling against a dead
head wind, which turned discomfort to hard labor.
Five long-markers paced each other, and managed to
keep a good lead. Behind, a bunch of 60 were tak-
ing one another along in short sprints and coming
up very fast. But they were making the pace almost
too hard, and the bunch was beginning to straggle
out, when five Victorians —Collins (34min.), Tebbs
(42min.), Lessing (40min.), Hughes (34min.) and
Slaney (44min.) —cut loose and sailed after the
leaders at a great pace. The leaders were over-
hauled after passing Winchelsea, and it became clear
that, as far as any but the five were concerned, the
Warrnambool was over. Collins smote the earth
hard with his body at Geelong, but quickly re-
mounted, and fled on. At the top of Haymarket
Hill, Melbourne, they were all dead level, and going
for their lives. Tebbs once got to the front, but
Collins flashed up on the inside of him and won by
inches. Lessing, who finished third, protested that
he had been interfered with by Collins. Collins
denied this, and the matter is to be decided by the
League of Wheelmen. The back-markers were out
of it, except O’Shea, of Maoriland, who, by running
through in Bhr. 18min. 44sec., secured the prize for
fastest time. The actual winner took Bhr. 45min.
llsec. The head wind, of course, accounted for the
slow time, and for the big proportion which dropped
out by the wayside, and took to trains, drink and
other means of locomotion. Of the 281 starters, only
195 finished. One of the droppers, however, had
legitimate excuse, having broken his collar-bone near
Camperdown.
The Dunlop Rubber people, who promoted the race,
were naturally elated at the fact that the first four
prizes and fastest time fell to men using their tyres.
Collins ascribed his fall at Geelong to interference
by parties who are still at large. Lessing, who
protested against Collins’s win, declares that some-
body, believed to be Collins, gripped him by the
saddle and pulled him back, as he was in the act
of unwinding his last kick for first place. Although
the time was slow, it was highly creditable, for the
winning bunch practically averaged 20 miles an
hour, which is mighty good going for 165 miles, and
three-fourths of it against a stiff head wind. Twice
in succession this long race has provided as exciting
a finish as a track sprint match could have done.
This year, the first five were bunched together in the
space of two lengths. The Ma State riders failed
to get a look-in, and the best performance on Ma’s
behalf was put up by A. C. Woollcott, who, with
lOmin. handicap, finished 35th. Collins, the winner,
is a Melbourne youth, and has been riding only two
years. He finished fifth in last year’s Warrnam-
bool.
# * * *
The Australasian cycling championships
meeting at Brisbane seems to have been a frosty
affair, from which the public enthusiastically stayed
away. The chief event was the half-mile champion-
ship of the continent, and it was run off in four
heats and a final. For the ultimate struggle Trous-
dell, of Queensland, found himself alone against
Saleh, Beck, Burtinsbaw and Weston of the Ma
State, and, like the policeman and the 10 glasses of
beer he downed the lot. His running was described by
people on the spot as brilliant, and he whizzed over
the distance in lmin. 7 2-ssec., winning by two
lengths from Saleh, with Beck, another length away,
third. The other championship was the 10 miles.
Fourteen started, six representing N.S.W. anfl eight
Queensland. This time aMa Stater got home, in the
person of C. Cutliffe, with Trousdell only half a
length behind, and Beck a few inches behind the
Queenslander. Cutliffe did the journey in 20min.
35sec.
• • * *
ARhough “Snowy” Baker has rendered
many public services in the past, there is no doubt
that he has eclipsed them all by publishing his
“General Physical Culture.” It is a book that
everyone should buy and read carefully; especially
those who have young children. Baker does not
tell how to make champions—he does better.
While warning against undue development of any
one part of the body at the expense of the rest, he
tells briefly how to train children so that they will
attain the best physical development their bodies are
capable of; and all, women as well as men, will
find in his pages information as to how they can
improve their bodily health and personal appearance.
S.B. knows his subject thoroughly. Most books on
physical culture are dreary reading. Baker’s is not
—it is interesting, and goes straight to the point.
The volume, in addition to Baker’s instructions on
physical culture, contains special articles on “The
Crawl Stroke.” and “Surfing,” by Cecil Healy and
Harold Baker respectively, both men who know
their subjects from A to Z. A most useful book,
which everybody under the age of 70 should read,
mark, learn and inwardly digest. And. at the same
time, there is no reason to infer that anyone over
70 will come to harm by reading it. Even at 70
it is not too late to learn to breathe properly, for
instance.
* » * *
Manly, the chief water-side suburb of
Sydney, has easily the best record for safe surf
bathing. This is" probably due, not so much to
natural conditions as to the care that is taken. A
stipendiary life-saver is nearly always present, and
the local life-saving club goes to a lot of trouble to
have a number of members in attendance on the
beach at all hours. Life lines are provided by the
council, and the life-savers practise with them regu-
larly. In fact, it would be hard to find a more
purely socialistic affair than surf-bathing as carried
on at Manly. On the most dangerous part of the
beach there" are four lines, and on a recent night
these were all cut, not a length of 20ft. being left
whole. The workings of a mind that could prompt
such an outrage are beyond following, and the party
that owns such an intellect can be only a criminal
lunatic.
The Eight Hours’ festival at Adelaide
Jubilee Oval included the usual Legislators’ Race,
75 yards. Styles led till close on the post, where he
stumbled, and was “pipped” by Blundell. J. V.
O’Loghlin third. Time, lOsec. Visiting delegates
were disappointed with the attendance —under 5000.
The falling off is supposed to be due to moral South
Aus. having done away with the “art union” tickets.
The South Aus. Derby is not a big thing
—it was worth only £426 this year to the winner—
but it is a prize locally coveted. The only thing the
public could not decide was which of R. J. Mathe-
son’s colts, T.T. (by Bobadil) or Kadlunga (by
Aryan) would get it. In a field of eight the former
was backed down to 5 to 4, and the latter to 7 to 2.
Kadlunga got nearly knocked over the rails, and was
never dangerous. T.T. finished a bad third to Dr.
Bennett’s Sanskrit and A. D. McDonald’s Banffshire
—both by the young English sire Aryan, who is in
great favor just now. Sanskrit paid £9 in the
totalisator. Eight weeks before the race he had
strangles, badly; otherwise he was to have had a
shot at the Randwick Derby. The Morphettville
Plate (two-year-old), same day, went to a clever filly,
R. M. Hawker’s Tabanga. She paid £7 35., and the
second (come into the garden, Moses, and listen to
the tale!) paid £2O 65.!
Saturday, at Caulfield, produced the most
abundant crowd of punters tnat Isaac ever dealt
with on a Guineas Day. For the local “talent,”
racy of the Victorian soil, it was a pretty bad day,
though the stable of squatter Manifold had a couple
of winners which commanded the notes and interest
of a fashionable few. In three cases, however, the
local idol was downed, and Sydney visitors did
the cheering when the numbers went up. For that
matter, nobody made the welkin ring very loudly
over the result of the Debutant Stakes. Whilst
Melbourne money was pouring in for Deeside, the
backers of Randwick form thought only of Ventura,
and, seemingly, that spry filly was unlucky to get
beaten. She never approached the leaders until
Golden Gauntlet had cut down the vaunted Deeside,
when the half-mile flutter was practically all over.
In the last 100 yards, Ventura must have made up
a lot of ground, after being bumped on the rails.
Evidently, the precocious little filly was in good
heart; therefore, her failure to get quickly off the
mark, as she did in her two races at Randwick,
suggests that she must have been interfered with at
the very outset.
The Caulfield Stakes, despite the absence of
Aurofodina, was worth going miles to see. Once
again Comedy King was expected to prove himself a
champion, and this time the black horse (with
Norman Godby up) ran a fine race. But, whilst he
was gradually overhauling Lady Medallist, the more
speedy Malt King got his second wind, and, with a
long-sustained run, the son of Malster pipped the
black on the post. A great struggle. On return-
ing to the birdcage, Comedy King looked all the
better for this severe shaking up, and his Melbourne
Cup chance still holds good. The weight may stop
him at the finish, but, all going well, he should be
cherry-ripe on the day.
The Guineas only confirmed the impression that
this season’s 3-year-olds, with the possible exception
of Jacamar, are a moderate lot. Flaith, in Walter
Hickenbotham’s stable, was backed down to 7 to 4
to beat Woolerina, Radiator, Popinjay and Cisco:
the last of this bunch, though he looked
well enough, was marked “No good” on
the evidence of the touts. Flaith outstayed
Popinjay easily, but, when it came to racing home,
Woolerina made a hack of the Victorian colt, whilst
the despised Cisco was the only one of the unplaced
lot that seemed to have a gallop left. As matters
stand now with the 3-year-olds, Jacamar is likelv to
start a warm favorite for the V.R.C. Derby. Wool-
erina’s brilliancy thus far has never suggested that
she can stay 12 furlongs.
The youngsters being so poor, and the form of the
older gees having been sifted quite conclusively, in
many cases, the Caulfield Cup, at time of writing, is
talked of as a match between Aurofodina and Lady
Medallist. Wednesdav’s racing at Caulfield mav, or
may not, affect the betting market, but probably the
things that happen after The Bulletin has gone to
press won’t make much difference in Cup quota-
tions. Taking this lot to beat the field—Flavian,
Lady Medallist, Aurofodina, Jolly Beggar, Eye Glass,
Safrano and Sandbath—the pick is
Lady Medallist.
It can be reasonably argued that condition favored
Aurofodina when he beat Malt King twice in Sep-
tember. On paper, the Maoriland mare is as good as
Aurofodina, seeing that she beat him when they met
in the Craven Plate a fortnight ago, and was beaten
only half a length by the improved Malt King and
Comedy King last Saturday. With onlv Bst. 51b. to
carry, she should be in the front rank from the start
of the Caulfield Cup. Her second in the Metropoli-
tan Stakes proves her fairly capable of going the
whole distance in fast time.
* * * *
One of the most sudden events that ever
happened to a great financial institution in these
parts was the stoppage, a few days ago, by the
Postmaster-General, of the correspondence of the
Commercial Bank of Tasmania. The event took
place under the anti-gambling laws, by reason of the
business which the Commercial of Tas. does for
“Tattersall.” Australia can now congratulate itself
on having done the pious act, after a wholesale
fashion, and snuffled on a continental scale.
Records, like men and women, run in all
shapes. At a recent motor-bike petrol consumption
trial at Glasgow, Scotland, a 34 h.p. engine, weigh-
ing under 3121b5., ran 277 miles to a gallon of
petrol.
* * » *
The wind-up of Adelaide football —which
lingered longer than any other on this continent —-
was one more blow to the unkillable brigade which
thinks that all Australian sport is “arranged” in ad-
vance. West Adelaide, unbeaten all winter, had gone
a bit stale at the end, and lost two matches. In the
so-called final. West beat Port, but the latter had a
rieht of challenge, and the clever ones were sure
Port had lost on purpose to make an extra match.
They took for granted that Port could win whenever
it wanted to. The absolute final showed that Port
couldn’t. It was a great game—anybody’s game—
but West got a goal two minutes from time, and
finished five points ahead. Essendon, premier club of
Melbourne, was waiting to play the winners, and that
encounter took place on Labor Day. West kicked a
trifle the straighter, and won by three points this
time (8-9 to 7-12). So South Aus. holds the Club as
well as the State premiership under Australian rules.
* * * »
The 20th annual tournament of the Strath-
field Lawn Tennis Club continued on Saturday. The
doings at Strathfield are important, as the open events
there decide the metropolitan championships of Syd-
ney. The disturbance on Saturday was the destina-
tion of the Doubles Championship for 1911-12 ; and
“’Orrie” Rice and E. D. L. Jones proved to be the
recipients. This was expected, but Poidevin and
Sayers made a bigger fight than was anticipated, and
the winners failed to show the form expected. Still,
they showed enough. The Singles Championship will
be played off next Saturday between Horace Rice and
Dr. Sharp. A. B. Jones and Miss D. Gordon make a
fine combination. They have worked their way to the
final of the Mixed Doubles Championship, and on
form should be able to wipe out the two other
couples who are still in, although H. Rice and Mrs.
Ford will prove a tough proposition. This particular
Rice is never easily chewed.
The second round of Sydney first-grade
cricket saw Balmain, the suburb all tattered and
torn, inflict a crushing defeat on Paddington by four
wickets and 23 runs on the first innings. Noble was
not available to his club on Saturday last, and it
missed him badly with the leather. Folkard fell on
the Paddington bowlers and smote them with the
jawbone of the bat for 202, not out, thus completely
nullifying Noble’s brilliant score of 118 the previous
Saturday. Altogether, Balmain totted up 319 for six
wickets, and provided no end of a surprise. Sydney
left 163 for North Sydney to eclipse, and when R. V.
Minnett had whooped up 109 on his own account,
North Sydney declared with 278 for six wickets, thus
winning by 115. In spite of the deadly bowling of
Cotter, Glebe failed miserably against University,
which had left 353 lingering on the board. Glebe
could scrape up only 208, so lost by the ugly margin
of 145 —a bad first innings beating. Middle Harbor’s
match against Redfern was a sad example of coming
too late. Redfern won by a margin of 105 on the
first innings. When it was too late M.H. got going,
and one Gee cut loose in the second innings and made
134, while the rest of the side added 162. But in the
first innings Middle Harbor had been able to make
only 79, and by that it fell. Petersham, which had
accumulated 189 on the previous Saturday, was easily
outclassed by Central Cumberland with 311 for the
loss of seven wickets; and of this formidable score no
less than 106 were contributed by E. F. Waddy, who
hits like his name. The close thing of the round was
the mediopre fight between Burwood and Gordon.
Burwood managed to just struggle through with a
margin of 17 —183 to 166. The bowling honors went
to Cotter, Hazlitt and Folkard. Cotter took seven
University wickets for 61. Hazlitt laid out four
Petersham timber-stacks for 31. Folkard battered six
Paddington stumps for 77. The result of the round is
to place Central Cumberland on top with five compe-
tition points, while University, Waverley, Redfern and
Balmain are one point behind. The rest are nowhere.
* * * *
The Australian Board of Control for In-
ternatiorial Cricket foregathered in Melbourne on Fri-
day last, and proceeded to do things with energy.
Clem Hill was appointed captain of the Australian
XI, and Clem Hill, P. A. McAlister and F. A. Ire-
dale were chosen as selectors. Victor Trumper, of
N.S.W., and Hugh Trumble, of Victoria, were also
nominated. The whole of the N.S.W. delegates, also
two Victorians, and the Queensland and Tasmanian
men jumped on Trumper with both feet, and the
“Champagne of Cricket” will probably have to be
content with the job of vice-captain of the XI.
Players in test matches are to be “allowed” £25 per
match and 12s. 6d. per day beer money, although
there is no obligation to spend the whole 12s. 6d. on
froth. The Board decided to recommend the admis-
sion of Westralia to the cricket fold, outvoting the
N.S.W. delegates on the subject; but it is an under-
stood thing that N.S.W. Association is not likely to
withdraw its veto over a trifle like that. Anything
not settled by the Board is to be left to the juris-
diction of Clem Hill and Warner.
* * * *
The stand that the alleged 20-year-old boy
Lester made against the experienced veteran McVea
moved a crowd of his Australian admirers to com-
mission the cunning workmen, Kerr Bros., to make
unto him a diamond-studded medal. The gew-gaw
was duly presented to the rising “Hope” at Paris
House (Sydney) on Monday night, and among those
who drank Lester’s health were the great Slapin-
tosh himself; Meagher, of the Assembly ; McVea, of
the Stoush, who had so successfully put a head op
Lester; Minahan, M.L.A.; and the ancient warrior
Larry Foley. Larry was in great form, and retailed
many reminiscences. When the company had
finished pouring olive oil over where the ammonia
had splashed, Lester rose to reply. He choked
several times, and finally managed to explain that
the Australian sports were the greatest of their
kind and kindred on earth. It was a great night,
and they had a good time.
T. Carrol v. E. Mack, a lightweight engage-
ment, occupied the ring at Melbourne Athletic Club, the
other evening, and raged with spirit for six rounds. The
encounter brings Carrol into position as a busy dis-
tributor of trouble, with a great power for evil in
his rapid left hand. He placed his punches with
artistic neatness and a judicial air that gratified the
multitude. Mack stood up to his trouble, and tried
hard and gamely; but Carrol is unlike the Dutch, in
taking too little and giving too much. He won in
six rounds. Mack’s party threw the serviette at the
clouds while its man was in the chair, trying vainly
to recall the circumstances of the case.
“Bandsman” Rice, light heavyweight and
a “towny” of George Rex, celebrated his recent arri-
val in this country by cordially belting a local resi-
dent named Jack Howard on Saturday night, in
Slapintosh’s Stoush Academy, Rushcutter’s Bay,
Sydney. Rice was above Howard’s class altogether
and, but fot a desire to make a show of the
local man, or rotten ring-craft, he could have
finished the business in half the time he actually
took over it. There was nothing in the fight, or
whatever it was, after the fifth round, that called
for more than sustained pressure upon an opening.
But Rice allowed chance after chance to go untaken,
even with his seconds pleading with him to “follow
up.” In the 10th round he did follow up, after
landing Howard a holy smite, and the beginning of
next round was the end of the fight. Howard’s
seconds reluctantly flew the “wipe” to save a knock-
out. Howard was a sad, sad representative of this
land of mountain solitude, sun-kissed politicians,
long-sleevers and other great natural resources. He
fought with cow-like intelligence along the lines of
the parcels-mail system, each of the despatches being
telegraphed well in advance. After the fifth round,
few of them reached their intended destination. The
ISriton works with both hands, and seems to be
imbued with the belief that the Lord loveth a cheer-
ful giver. It should be interesting to be around
when he argues the point with Dave Smith.
* * * *
N.S.W. League of Swimmers, the rebel
crowd which swims for open orders instead of solid
hardware, held its annual meeting last Saturday
night, and whooped some. Six new clubs were said to
have been formed and affiliated with the League, and
parties of swimmers had roamed the continent and
had a good time in various places, holding carnivals
and giving exhibitions. Quite a number of “cham-
pionships of the State” are reported to have been
won, and generally there was a ring of prosperity
about the new concern which displeases the Associa-
tion barracker muchly. The League has managed to
annex some good swimmers in George Read, O. Dick-
man, and that graceful water-nymph, Miss Tate, and
there seems but little doubt it has come to stay. The
swimming season opened on Saturday, and most of the
clubs, League and Amateur, held competitions. But
it was merely a preliminary dip, and it will be a
week or two before the water-sprite is really on the
job.
* * * *
A fresh, not to say rather stiffish, nor’-
east breeze careered over and around Sydney on Satur-
day, and the open boats on both Port Jackson and
Botany Bay had a festive time of it. It was the
opening day of the Sydney Royal Yacht Squadron;
an annual affair that, somehow, always reminds one
of a hen taking a brood of ducklings for their first
swim. The charming club grounds at Carrabella were
thronged by a gay and debonair crowd in which
ladies largely predominated. Yachts were invisible,
but there were races for sailing skiffs and 10ft. and
6ft. dinghies. The real business of the day was frill
and frivolity, the sea dogs not having got their sea
legs yet.
Sydney Sailing Club was on the warpath, how-
ever, and 17 boats engaged in a tussle that had a
real Roman flavor about it. Sails were burst, and
boats were capsized or swamped. Only 10 reached the
lightship, where they rounded for home. Two more
turned turtle later on, and of the gallant fleet that
set out only seven struggled back. For the rest, “the
wind had blown them all away,” as Villon sang of
something of the sort. The winner turned out to be
Sunny South, skippered by Mr. Keddie, who had won
the previous Saturday’s race. He had three minutes
handicap, and was only seriously threatened by the
beautiful Australian, skippered by that past master,
Mr. Christian Webb, which ran into second place.
Perfectly rigged and excellently handled, Sunny South
deserved its win.
The Motor Boat Club of N.S.W. is abroad
again, and held a mild burst of speed on Saturday, in
the shape of a general handicap over four miles.
But it was only a baby affair, and the little winner,
Monettat, coughed home in 17min. 45sec. Motor-
boating is losing its punch in Sydney, since Relph,
Arthur Davies, George Whatmore and other arch-
angels of speed gave up the game. We miss the long
procession of dark sea-horses the Whatmore was for
ever springing, or going to spring, on a bewildered
Harbor.
* * * *
Maorilander Jack Arnst failed to make a
decent showing against Albany, the Englishman, on
the Thames last week. Although “big Jack” has been
in the boat a couple of years now, and is understood
'to have the animal at his fingers’ ends, so to speak,
he has shown no signs of eclipsing brother Dick, or
filching his championship. John would probably
have made more money and glory had he stuck to
his trusty wheel.
One reason why France makes such strides
in aviation is that the French recognise its possibili-
ties in the event of war, and the people shell out
their good hard brass to encourage the art, sport or
whatever one likes to call it. The well-known French
tyre people, Michelin and Cie, recently passed along
150,000 francs to be distributed in four prizes to
assist in deciding whether aeroplanes can blow up
warships ; wreck railway bridges, arsenals and maga-
zines ; dismount guns, and prevent troops mobilising.
And these prizes are reserved solely for French civil
and military

SPOTS BEFORE THE EYES.
CAUSED BY LIVER DISORDERS.
Mr. Tom Holt, whose remarkable state-
ment is published below, is another example
of what Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills have
done for one who has suffered from that
most common trouble, “A Liver.” Mr. Holt
is an old resident and property holder of
Bondi Junction, and now living at 55 Stan-
ley Street, Sydney, N.S.W., and his state-
ment is a voluntary
Mr. Tom Holt.
(From a photo.)
one and purely an ex-
pression of gratitude
for the benefit he has
derived from taking
Dr. Morse’s Indian
Root Pills. He tried
doctors and hospital
treatment to no avail,
and actually states
that these Pills saved
his life. He has been
taking them now for
ten years, and still
continues to recom-
mend them.
“W i t h o u t Dr.
Morse’s Indian Root
Pills I don’t know
what would become
of me. For a number of years I have suf-
fered with a sluggish liver, which produced
constipation to such an extent that some-
times I have gone for days without a move-
ment of the bowels. Spots appeared float-
ing before my eyes, and I almost went
blind, and got so bad that I went to the hos-
pital as an outdoor patient. They treated
me for my eyes, but with no result- —in fact,
I became worse. Someone told me my blind-
ness was due to liver trouble, and recom-
mended Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills, which
I began using with the most gratifying re-
sults. In a short time my sight was as good
as ever, and the spots which had been float-
ing in the air were gone, to my great relief.
I was in such a condition that work was
impossible, because I was sleepy and drowsy
all the time, with no energy to do anything.
This was ten years ago, and I have con-
tinued the use of the Pills ever since, taking
two or three as I need them. I believe they
saved my life, and I consider them a mosr
valuable medicine. From to time 1
have been persuaded to try other remedies,
but I never got the benefit I received from
Dr. Morse’s Indian Root Pills. In fact, the
other medicines are of no use. I am an old
resident of Bondi Junction, and now
living at 55 Stanley Street, Sydney, am well
known, and have much pleasure in making
this statement, which is voluntarily given,
and will always be ready to personally con-
firm same.
(Signed) “TOM HOLT.”
Mr. Holt’s trouble was Liver, which upset
the whole system of digestion and nutrition.
Many people are suffering from defective
eyesight due to this cause, and if they put
the Liver right they would have no need to
trouble with glasses. Defective eyesight is
only one of the many effects of a sluggish
liver, which upsets the whole system, caus-
ing Indigestion, Biliousness, Constipation
and the hundred and one other complaints
that accompany them.

THE SUMMIT OF KOSCIUSKO.
Guests' are taken to the
Snowfiefds daily to indulge in the
Alpine sports of Tobogganing and Ski-
riding.
The Kosciusko Scenery is mag-
nificent. Inspiring panoramas of tree-
clad ranges, rugged peaks, and yawning
chasms, sweep from horizon to horizon.
The Trout Fisherman finds
lively sport in the snow-fed streams on
the uplands or round the foothills of the
Kosciusko Ridge.
“ The summer attractions of the unique Kosciusko
District only need to he known to draw visitors from every
corner of Australia
Great Snowdrifts linger on the Kosciusko ridge
through the hottest summer, making it the coolest spot
during that trying season.
The Rarefied Air of these Mountains breathes
health and revivification to tired systems and over-
wrought nerves.
The Summer Climate of Kosciusko is a reve-
lation. The normal conditions are bright sunny days
with bracing temperatures.
The Hotel Kosciusko —a palat’al edifice, and
well appointed—is an ideal retreat in the fastnesses of
the Hills.

ANSWERS to CORRESPONDENTS.
No notice (except of cl hostile character) will be
taken of communications insufficiently stamped.
Name and address must appear on MSS. No
liability is undertaken re contributions volun-
tarily submitted, whether sent by post or handed
in; and no MS. will be returned unless stamps
(of any State) accompany.
L.F.J.: Don’t know. If it appeared at
all it was in the advertising columns. .. .5.8.: The
China squadron.... White Aus.: Any decent white
person of any nation can enter Australia. Any per-
son of any race who desires to enter the Common-
wealth may be required to “write not less than 50
words of a language prescribed by regulation” ; but
his dictation test “is not, and never has been, im-
iosed upon persons of European race” (Official
ycar Book, 1910). The test may be applied to
Ram Bux, of India, just as well as to Ah Sin, of
China... .J.L.: This paper wants to help Wakool
Crossing more than almost anything else in the
world; but it wants to know a bit more about the
matter before it can undertake to shove along a
railway to Wakool Crossing’s back d00r... .Berkeley:
Solomon Island notes very scattered and fragmentary.
D.M.: A gory thing with two dead men in it
and a dead dog ; and no expoundment of the wliyness
of the gore, and the two dead men, and the dead
dog.... Jack: Very conventional and stereotyped, and
we loathe remarks like: “Of medium height, there
was a very earnest look in his deep blue eyes.” Why
not: “Born of an old English family, he badly
needed his hair cut?” You recall that famous
sample of reporterese: “His name is Smith, but it is
thought he mav recover.”.. .G. 8.8.: George Stewart,
Glen Wood, Inverell (N.S.W.), writes that he was
shepherding for J. D. Aitken at Urana in 1852-3.
Possibly he is the man you wish to discover....
Literary: Can’t either remember or trace any Aus-
tralian production of Ibsen’s “Master Builder’ . ...
.303: No; Australia depends wholly on the lad.
There is no provision for the compulsory enrolment
of anybody who had his 17th birthday prior to last
New Year’s Day.... Fill: That story that
came from out Bourke way recently,” originally
came from Palestine, or thereabouts, somewhere about
the time the prophet Isaiah was remarking that the
Lord was “full of the burnt offerings of rams and the
fat of fed beasts; and delighted not in the blood of
bullocks, or of lambs, or of he-goats.” Which makes
it very old indeed. Other stories forwarded appear
to be about the same age or even older. . . - Cam:
Point about as obscure as a splinter in an elephant s
paw Slinger (M.L.): Tracings to hand. Sus-
pected them at first of being paragraphs. Have since
ascertained that they are copies of hieroglyphics,
bitten into the counter-lunch by a boozed Egyptian
actor about 700 b.c. Forwarded them on to him...

A MILD TRUTH.
There is the noise the din the roar
That we hear every day.
For that we hesitate no more
But tail upon our way
Oh! the great bustling routine
And craftsmen toiling eagerly
Oh ! toiler then with open screen
Fight for thy life and liberty
How sad the weird exniring wail
From hungry midst their daily toil
How sad the guys at pomp wassail
And thp weary sons of soil
When shall the race with shoddy science
And wantons with a title name
Cease their brotherly defiance
And repent of their shame
O’er pompous rot my heart annoys
Stil for that let the dav be done
A toiler meets a thousand joys
Where never they have one
Let the guys have acme wine
And pretendin' l- guys it too
I will strive for nobler mind
Some nobler work to do.
R.W.T.: Try a religious paper... .Bellerive: Herewith
your general notice to threepenny highwaymen: —
THE CADGER.
Give us a chew of tobacco mate
Or can you spare a bloke a Brown
Is the same old tale of the cadger bold
Whom mooches round the Town
When he score a tray he’ll smile with glee
And into the Orient pub he’ll steer
Where he greedily devour the counter Lunch
And sip a pint of Beer.
I’d give my last penny to a man
If crippled or broken down
But not to the lazy cadger bold
Whom mooches round the Town.
