(V.): No points about it 1 Wonder: So do we.
Three verses :
What good is life?
Do not our sorrows outweigh our joys
From the time we first break our toys
Till toothless age
When in senile rage
We bury the last of the girls and boys.
Oh! what is life ?
Why do we live?
Is it habit or is it the dread
Which now and then comes into our head
Perhaps when glad
Perhaps when sad
Of what will happen when we are dead.
Oh! why do we live ?
What is death?
Some say one thing and some another
Christian this and Heathen the other
Always at blows
But neither knows
Any more than did their mother
Oh~! what is death?

The World’s Standari
Valveless Engine.
Careful consideration of the prices of the FERRO
Engine will readily convince intending purchasers
that this outfit brings within their reach a plant
that has hitherto been beyond the “pocket” of many
would-be buyers. At the same time, we would like
to impress upon all that, although the Plant is low
in price, it is NOT LOW IN QUALITY. We assert,
without hesitation, that there is NOT A BETTER
ENGINE MADE than the FERRO. The low price
is accounted for by the enormous output and the per-
fect organisation of the Ferro Co., combined with
the fact that the design of FERRO Engine omits a
number of parts necessary in ordinary Oil Engines.
In all fifteen parts per cylinder are eliminated,
and all of them parts which call for greatest ac-
curacy in design and workmanship. It is evident,
therefore, that a FERRO Engine can be made of
highest class materials at a LOWER PRICE than a
Valve Operated Engine using the same grade of
material—-which few manufacturers attempt.
It must also be borne in mind that the failure of
any one of the fifteen parts mentioned will throw
the Engine out of business until it is replaced, and
the FERRO has fifteen less chances of stopping when
there is work to be done.
Don’t forget this: TIME IS MONEY.

Stationary, Portable or Marine use:

THIRTY YEARS
OF DISFIGURING
ECZEMA CURED
“Ever sinch I was a little<girl, thirty years
ago, I lia<} suffered tortufes from eczema in
one of its worst forms. The disease runs in
my family, \and mine was of a scaly and
most disfiguring kind. The eczema formed
in round rings, and then scales all over
V
thy face- and limbs. I
have been smothered
from head to foot. 1 was
born in Leamington,
which is famous for its
sulphur baths and pump
waters, and one would
think that after taking
. the treatment there reg-
ularly as I did, the
disease would long ago
have been cured, but it
was not. I attended the
Hospital for years
and years, so you will see
I gave it a fair trial.
Everyone in the town knew of my case. My
face was disfigured very badly. A doctor
told me that I should never get rid of it. I
spent pounds in doctors' bills, and 1 attended
several hospitals, but nothing did ipe any
good.
“Then one day an uncle of mine recom-
mended the Cuticura Remedies. I took his
advice, and commenced to use the Cuticura
Soap. To my astonishment an improvement
at once set in, and my hair, which had been a
complete mass of scales and scurf, soon
began to look in splendid condition. Then
1 bought a box of Cuticura Ointment, and
this cleared my skin wonderfully. In a few
weeks' time all traces of the scaly eruptions
had completely disappeared. Now my skin
is clear and healthy, and thanks to the Cuti-
cura Remedies I am completely cured of
eczema. All my neighbours were astounded;
they knew what a sight I was, and the manner
of my cure by Cuticura Remedies was nothing

822
Business, Bobbery, etc.
“The Hack”: As one who had eight years’ experi-
ence as a cash newsagent, let me put my oar into
the argument between “Huffin-Guffin” (B. 3/8/’ll)
and “Billy Mug” (B. 31/8/’ll), regarding what the
former calls “paper-boy slavery.” As a start,
“Huffin' 1 acknowledges that his attention was first
drawn to the matter through his paper arriving late.
It would be interesting to know what time he ex-
pected it. Is he one of those who promote the
“slavery” he denounces, by insisting on his paper
being on his doorstep at, or before, daybreak ?
“Muffin” is, I believe, not making the most of his
case when he cites the agents’ profit at 3d. per dozen.
This is the profit allowed “cash” agents; but, I
understand, the “authorised” agents receive, some 4d.
and others sd. per dozen, the amounts varying with
their sales. I may be mistaken in this, as the
Authorised Newsagents’ Association keeps its secrets
closely ; but my informant was a business-man, who
had an “authorised” agency for sale. If this be so,
they should certainly pay better wages than they
do. I can bear out “Billy Mug,” however, in saying
that, with a profit of 3d. per dozen, a newsagent can-
not pay his boys more than, at most, 3s. 6d. per
week, nor, considering the nature of the work, and
that the boys are only engaged in it for about an hour
per day, are they badly paid at this rate, in com-
parison with those in other callings. If, after allow-
ing for rent, carriage, bad debts (unavoidable in
this trade), and dishonesty and carelessness of the
“slaves” championed by “Guffin,” he can pay better
wages, and make more than a bare living for him-
self, I’ll award him the Australian .boss financier’s
belt with pleasure. For my part, I never once
cleared £1 per week on my daily papers (the re-
strictions hedging a “cash” agent make it next to
impossible for him to supply more than 200 cus-
tomers). I wouldn’t recommend the newsagency busi-
ness to my worst enemy.
* * * *
“J.G.”: Re the Maffra (Vic.) beet sugar factory,
which The Bulletin (31/8/’ll) tipped as likely to
be one of the best of the Weary Government’s So-
cialistic ventures. I’m waiting for the howl of pro-
test from the Anti-Sosh cockies who have been asked
to be parties to this accursed Socialism. John
Weary has actually had the audacity to invite these
right-minded men, the backbone of" the country, to
accept, free of cost, enough sugar beet seed to plant
half an acre of land; also, free of charge, full in-
structions-as to culture. Further, the unholy scheme
provides that when the beets have been grown, they
will be hauled over the Sosh railways to the. Sosh
factory at Maffra, and a Sosh cheque will be paid
over with thanks. In fact, all that Private Enter-
prise has to do is to drop the seeds in the ground,
and Sosh will do the rest and pay for the privilege!
Of course, it can’t be done, as Anti-Sosh has re-
peatedly pointed out, and no doubt the eocliies are
reaching for the seed with both hands, for no other
reason than to prove to Sosh that it doesn’t know its
business.
“Sand Boy” : I’m satisfied. that the Commonwealth
Government has power to cater into business against
the Sugar Trust, and if there is nothing about the
power in the Constitution, it is because nothing is
required. The right to refine sufar is not a State
monopoly as law-making was previous to r ederauun ;
it is the ordinary right of the individual citizens,
or residents for that matter, and the. people as a
whole can exercise it in a corporate capacity if they
want to. The Commonwealth would merely have to
register as a corporation in Queensland, and conduct
its business in accordance with State laws, the same
as the C.S.R. Of course, it might be possible for a
State to amend its Company Law, so as to shut out
the Commonwealth; but that’s another matter. Al-
ready, the Commonwealth engages in trade, such as
the buying and selling of land, military stores, etc.
Generally it avoids open competition with traders,
but • not for a want of power to compete. Very
shortly the Australian Government will be trading
on a larger scale in connection with the Federal
capital and the Northern Territory. It will be
dealing extensively with land, and, probably, with
buildings, electric light, water supply and other
things. Also, it will be doing business in a big way
as a bank, and here it will be in direct competition
with private bankers. Some time ago it proposed
to build Commonwealth steamers to be run in the
English trade, and there was no suggestion of con-
stitutional difficulties. The most extraordinary
feature of the friction, caused by the C.S.R. mono-
poly, is the attitude of the Age. When the Labor
Government proposed to buy . out harmful combines
. on fair terms, the Age was aghast at the iniquity of
the proposal; now the same paper is howling for the
Government to enter with all its resources in com-
petition against the big sugar monopoly, and wine it
off the lists of trading concerns, or even the face of
the earth.
* * * •
A cable the other day mentioned that “the Sunder-
: land Co-operative Society had suspended payment
owing to the withdrawal of £24,000. The directors
' state that the society is solvent.” This ought to
make one Or two of the older Australian co-operative
1 societies think hard. The original “co-ops.”' had an
idea that a member ought to be entitled to with-
draw his capital on giving short notice. Also, seeing
that the shareholder of to-day might not be a share-
holder to-morrow, they cut up all the profits as they
went along, and never had any reserve funds worth
mentioning. It was a shockingly dangerous system,
because the shareholder’s capital might really be re-
presented by soap or clothes pegs, and as there, isn’t
a bank that will cash soap or clothes pegs on de-
mand, the society was always liable to have to tell
the shareholder that he couldn’t have his money yet
awhile. Apparently, this happened at Sunderland,
and those few Australian societies which work on
the same rotten system had better change it before
the same trouble looks in upon them.
“Stingaree”: I had come to the. conclusion that
Bung had arrived at the place where there was no-
thing further to be learned in the way of faking
liquor, when a nublican in a N.Q. township intro-
duced me to an original idea. By placing a couple
of cakes of scented sohp in a cask of even the flattest
beer, he found -that the fluid when served out would
foam over the top of the glass and make the eyes
of the most ancient and blase beer-chewer sparkle.
He did not say that he had patented the invention
yet. so I hasten to request that the matter be kept
a dead secret.
UN’HATCHED.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Government could do nothing for the schools,
but the budget shows that £ll5O could be spent on
the hen-house at Burnley. Was that not statesman-
ship worthy of recognition? . . . The late Mr. Bent
had said he could not meet a cow without taking
his hat off, and now Mr. Graham would not meet
a hen without cackling.— Warde, Victorian M.L.A.
The Ministerial bird sat tight
Upon an egg extremely pale,
And, cackling, nursed by day and night
A hope forlorn,
That something, some fine morning, might
Break through its pallid shell, and hail
The smiling morn!
Alas! That egg-of-promise still
Remains plain china, bald and chill.
No Ministerial warmth or wit,
Tending that spheroid night and day,
Can bring whatever "is in it
Up to the scratch. ..
Sure, when mad hens on china sit,
Blue-ducks are dashed well all that they
Can hope to hatch.
Blue-ducks it is—the pale egg still
Remains unchickened, bald and chill.
No cackling can give life to clay,
No care turn china into cliook,
Though hatch the hatcher night and day
With every care ;
No miracles are on the way,
Nor for prize poultry do we look
From crockery ware.
The Ministerial setting's still
A take-down dummy, bald and chill!
VICTORIA.

Gout and Rheumatism.
These complaints arise from the poisonous waste
matters accumulated in the blood, consequent on the
kidneys, through temporary disorder or through
some disease, being unable to carry out their work.
Dr. Sheldon’s Gin Pills rapidly reach the root of the
trouble, and soon afford relief. Price, Is. 6d. and

CHRISTMAS EDITION of
“THE BULLETIN,” 1911.
A Special and Enlarged Issue
Published on DECEMBER 14.
It will contain the best Stories, Verse and
Sketches, grave and gay (mostly gay), con-
tributed during the, year by the foremost Aus-
tralasian writers.
The illustrations, by “Hop,” Alf Vincent, Nor-
man Lindsay and other leading representative
artists, will be the finest, Black and White work
obtainable in Australia, and will reflect the
national humor.
The Bulletin Christmas Number is the annual
reflection of the life and thoughts of our country,
and therefore useful as well as interesting.
Agents will be supplied at the usual rates, but,
in consequence of the heavy outlay on this Special
Edition, NO RETURNS will be accepted.
ORDERS FOR EXTRA COPIES should reach
us not later than the 18th NOVEMBER to ensure
supply.
Single copies post free to any part of the Com-
monwealth States and Maoriland, 7d.; Foreign
addresses, 9d.
COMMON AMONG MINERS.
“Like many other miners I contracted a severe
cold through coming out of the hot mine into the
cool air,” says Mr. W. Crouch, of Tabina Street,
Broken Hill, N.S.W., “and at night I would get an
awful tickling in the throat and chest, but Chamber-
lain’s Cough Remedy was the means of giving im-
mediate relief from it, and quickly cured my cold.
No other medicine was able to do this, and I had
many bottles from the doctor.”*

fendant
Take notice that on the fifth day of June
1911 a writ of summons was issued in the
above action which claimed £43/4/6 for the
price of goods sold and delivered and take
notice that by an order dated the sixth day
of September 1911 it was ordered that the
service of a copy of the said order and a
copy of a writ of summons in this action by
sending the same by prepaid post letters ad-
dressed to you the said Jay Amos at Pitts-
worth Queensland -land to you care of Mr.
Stewart Pittsivorth aforesaid and to you
care, of the General Post Office Sydney New
South Wales respectively and the publica-
tion by advertisement of notice of said order
in the Sydney “Bulletin” Newspaper pub-
lished at Sydney aforesaid and in a news-
paper circulating in the town of Pittsworth
aforesaid should be deemed good service of
the said writ of summons on you and take
notice that in default of you causing an ap-
pearance to be entered for you at the Regis-
try Supreme Court Brisbane within three
months after the last of such advertisements
the plaintiff may proceed in the said action
and judgment may be given in your absence.
Dated this sixth day of October 1911

IN BANANA LAND
To a recent civil service deputation that
wanted its lunch-time extended, Premier
Denham remarked: “Your hours are 38 a
week—no great strain on your constitu-
tions.” The Brisbane dailies have been
howling with indignation for weeks because
some Labor orator down South advocated
42 hours a week for a working-man; but
they couldn’t find a single word of rebuke
for the privileged pen-drivers who thought
38 hours a week too much.
A “conscientious objector” the
other day interviewed a Defence
officer, and declared that, no mat-
ter how often he might be fined, he
would never allow his son to have
military training. “Tell me,” said
the officer, coaxingly, “what would
you do if a foreign foe swooped
down upon Australia, and took
your land from you, and did un-
speakable things to your women
and children? Would you sit on a
fence and look on, because it is
against your religion to figbt?”
“No, sir.” said the “conscientious
objector” decidcdlv; “I should look
to you, gentlemen, to defend me.”
Then a bomb burst on those pre-
mises, and the “conscientious ob-
jector” fled for shelter.
Amongst North Queenslanders
who will have big funerals when
they die, old Dr. May, of Bunda-
berg, stands well up on the list.
He is much like that other North-
erner. Dr. Koch, the friend of
broken-up miners from Papua and
of poor folk generally, who burned
his account books when he knew
he was dying. Some years ago, to
do away with a lot of book-keeping
work that seriously interfered with
the time available for the cross-
examination of insects, Dr. May
reckoned he would just call every-
square. Some time back the
Hospital gave him a holiday and
£5O to have a good time with. He
spent the holiday watching other
doctors and surgeons in the big
cities, and the £5O in buying ap-
paratus for the public hospital at
Bundaberg. Commercially,, he is
a hopeless proposition, but he will
discuss the intimate family affairs
of a ceratodus or a bit of gossip
from the Jurassic period with avidity.
A Mrs. Begg, of Childers, has established
what must be close-up a birth-rate record.
Within 12 months she has given birth to
five children—twins and triplets.
“Condot”: The Queensland Times, of
Ipswich, was 50 years old on October 8.
When it started, Queensland was an infant of
two years’ growth. The Times began as a
bi-weekly, and cost 3d. a copy. It is now a
penny daily. Its early editors were J. C.
Thompson, a surveyor, who laid out the
town of Gympie; George Hamilton Hall, a
famous shot in his day; Edward Butterfield,
an enlightened and scholarly man, travelling
representative of the Melbourne Akgus in the
digging days of Victoria ; Joseph Butterfield,
his brother; and Dr. W. Carr-Boyd. Jack
Woolly now pushes the weary editorial pen
of the Conservative old sheet. Some good
Australian ink-travellers have had their
press beginnings in the Times office, includ-
ing Dave Watterston, now manager of the
Wilson share in the Argus. Hugh Parkin-
son, who died full of years in 1909, was one
of the progenitors of the sheet, and his sons,
Hugh and William, are directors of the co.
that owns it.
The white man and his wife, the white
woman, still do themselves fair credit
in the tropical North, according to the evi-
dence of the old age pension list. The pro-
portion of pensioners who qualified last
financial year by reason of poverty
and longevity was 1710 for every
1,000,000 inhabitants of the Commonwealth,
and those qualified by poverty and sickness
were 169 per 1,000,000. They were divided
out in this fashion: —
Old Age Invalid Total
Pensioners Pensioners per
per 1,000,000. per 1,000,000. 1,000,000.

Apparently, they grow a fair proportion of
old people in the North, and but for the re-
cent extensive immigration the proportion
of tough old battlers would be still higher.
And they either grow an amazingly small
proportion of premature wrecks-Hfar smaller
than in N.S.W. and Tasmania —or else
they are affluent wrecks, and don’t require
pensions. And if the explanation is that
they are affluent wrecks, then the North
must be a climate where men have energy
enough to acquire affluence before they be-
come wrecks, for affluence doesn’t grow on
trees in Queensland any more than it does
elsewhere. It is a notable fact that the
three States with the fewest wrecks are the
three that spread into the tropics—S.A.
having included the Territory at the time
when the list was compiled.

“X.P.D.”: At the back of Cairns, along
the Barron Valley, there are great areas of
country which, because of its steepness, is
unfit for agriculture, and is never likely to
be selected until some method is invented of
ploughing up and down the walls of the
globe with a perpendicular horse. These
precipices carry good soil, and are handy to
the railway ; also, they are the natural home
of the cedar, though the big trees have been
cut out long agq. Mostly it is State land.
By planting that country with young cedars,
and caring for them on scientific principles,
a good profit might be earned out of some-
thing that is now almost valueless. Accord-
ing ,to an experjmeritJ'niade in a small way
some' years ago, the ebst of planting, etc",
would be about 3d. per free. No clearing is
needed; the young trees could just be shoved
in along the miles and miles of disused tim-
ber tracks which form a 'network over the
country.
If Only.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
If only you could run the Earth
And I could rig the Bun,
On what a scheme of maddest mirth
This Universe would run !
The gales that gallop wild and grim
Would then so softened he
That, gently as a vesper hymn,
They'd sweep along the sea.
The course of Love that will go wrong
Would never turn askew ;
'l’he pretty girls who chanced along
Would smile on me and you ;
Our chronic state of dollar-dearth
Would mighty soon be done —
If I could only run the Earth
And you could rig the Sun.
Our wealthy aunt would make her will
And then depart in peace
To where the choirs eternal trill
Their songs and never cease;
As soon as e’er a wish could dawn
’Twould straight accomplished he.
And ev’ry Ivory Gate would yawn
At “Open Sesame !”
We’d drown the voice of ev’ry dun,
We’d stifle ev’ry bore,
We’d pot with swift, unerring gun
The pests of ev’ry shore;
We’d cage the prima donnas all
And bid them pipe away
Until they turned the nightly pall,
With song, to merry day.
And all this crazy quilt of things,
The common things that are.
Would whirl away on sudden wings
And leave us on a star —
A star that flashed a-down a firth
Of endless joy and fun—
If only you could run the Earth
And / could rig the Sun.
QUEENSLAND.
F. BORN.
QUEENSLAND GOSSIP.
Brisbane, October 14, 1911.
My Dear Thargomindaii,—
The clan Macgregor is still making sorties
from .its lastness upon public gatherings, and
wielding the trusty platitude with more or
less effect. This afternoon, the Chiel motored
to Sandgate, and laid tire foundation stone
of the new town hall; at least, he tapped
the stone down where the other bricklayer
had laid it, and declared it to be a work-
manlike job. In return, his Ex. was handed
on« burnished trowel and a quantity of
rusty oratory. The local inhabitants con-
gregated in all the glory of her new motor-
veil and skin-tight dress, and moved through
P ro^® e dißgs with the grace and elegance
of H. G. VV ells’s party of Martians who
conquered the earth in “The War of the
Worlds. Amongst the visitors to the root
or the new building were the Mayoral
Diddams.
Humanitarianism in woman is not alto-
gether dead. The Denham Sisters the other
day handed out tea in exchange for recipes
with which to lighten the lot, and, incident-
a,Ry* chest, of the male contractor of
the forthcoming Docker-Milson alliance. A
sister (married) of another bridegroom re-
cently compiled a neat little booklet, in which
she warned her new sister just how Alger-
non Reginald liked his curry, and what to
give him for breakfast on the morning fol-
lowing lodge nights. The new scheme is
meeting with the hearty approval of the
Divorce Court, which sees in it some pros-
pect of a much-needed rest.
On Wednesday last, the City Fathers and
their femininity gathered in great array at
Kangaroo Point, to witness the launch of a
new municipal ferry-boat. After Miss Ena
-tietherington (the daughter of last year’s
• a ju° r h eave d a bottle of champagne
in the eye of the cruiser, the party adjourned
to the workshop, where the roast turkey
had the time of its life, and the bottled grog
went on its way rejoicing.
Maidens are still rushing in where the di-
vorced fear to tread. Miss Gladys Marie
Spowers, an offshoot of our Surveyor-Gen-
eral, donned 1 white satin last week, and as-
sured Mr. Gerard Gore of her perfect
wilhngness to accompany him to Assam and
spend her future days watching him plant
tea. On Tuesday, another of our Very Best
was enticed away from the city by Mr.
Alistair Gordon, of Nanango. He placed
th 6 proprietorial plain band on the ring finger
of Aileen Fahey, who was upholstered for
the occasion in beautiful old Limerick lace
and satin. E ather gave the girl away, in a
manner of speaking, and barrister brother
Bernard acted as referee.
Rumors of another engagement float in
from the West, where one of our dimpled
city daughters has gathered in the proprietor
ot many acres and much mutton, with whose
(the proprietor’s, not the mutton’s) mater-
nal relative she had sojourned awhile.
Home,” at the Art Society’s show
°? 1 . T hursday, produced a weird jumble of
alleged hats,_ Paris (perhaps) frocks, Brum-
magem earrings, beaded shoes and impres-
sionist complexions. Lady Cowley and Mes-
dames Stqdart, Robin Dods and Blundell
distributed the cup that cheers when there
is nothing better to be obtained.
Nellie Nicholson, a lady with a figure like
an early Norse goddess, and a voice which
makes one hear an overloaded cash-box in
the distance, gave a successful concert re-
cently. She draped herself for the occasion
m rose-pink satin filled in with moonlight
sequins, and two bullock drays were hired to
carry home the floral remarks of apprecia-
tion. Arthur Crane, of comic opera fame,
who is enthusiastically engaged in putting a
polish on the lady’s top notes, added his
share to the music of the evening.
Those North Pine inhabitants who have
petitioned and deputationised their objection
to having the name of their railway station
changed to “Petrie,” have been squelched,
ihe ukase declareth that they shall alight at
Petrie, anyhow, and reside at North Pine if
they like. Now I have it on good authority,
that another railway station may shortly
have its name changed in order that Digby
D. may alight at Denham to get to Yeronga!
Miss Margaret Bailey, who attends to the
pruning of the young idea at the Rockhamp-
ton Grammar School, leaves per Orient boat
at the end of December for distant parts
Miss Cornwall, Another lady of scholastic
leanings at the same academy, will accom-
pany her for one year, to patch up her
si ightly-frayed jiealth.
Our tennis champions are feeling that life
has its recompenses sometimes. Following
upon the fright they gave the N. S. Wales
cracks some little time ago, they have re-
ceived an invitation to nominate players for
the forthcoming big tournament at
Manila.
Yours affectionately.
AUSTRALIA JANE.
“Quan” : How’s this for a ride? Ernest
Rumbel, a youth of 18, recently rode a horse
from Salisbury, on the Williams River
(N.S.W.), to Crow’s Nest, near Toowoomba
(Q.), a distance of 633 miles, in 14 days.
The nag looked very little the worse for his
long trip, so well did the youngster nurse
him.
CAB RECRIMINATION.
LEAN : “ You're a beautiful bloomin' specimen ,
you are."
F AT: "Well, ye see, we can't all be heavenly
Romeos like you, Bill. ”
The Denham Government has just put a
Sugar Works Bill through the Assembly. The
measure provides that, as soon as the money
advanced by the State for the erection of a
mill is repaid, the property passes entirely
into the hands of landowners and cane-
growers. The State thus takes the risk of
failure; the private landowner only takes
over the mill when it proves a success. Dr.
Maxwell (late Comptroller of Sugar Mills)
condemned this principle most vehemently
—but Maxwell has departed. Still, the
Queensland Labor party might have been ex-
pected to make a decent fight against the
grossly unfair, double-head'ed-penny business,
seeing that “nationalisation of the means of
production, distribution and exchange” is
still supposed to be its objective. But the
Queensland Labor party suffers from very
defective vision at times, and can't see its
objective as it might.

This photograph gives an idea of the saving in
bulk which results from printing the new En-
cyclopaedia Britannica on India paper, which
unites strength and opacity with tenuity. A
prospectus will be sent on application.
The India Paper Impression.
INDIA paper is the name given to a very thin and light, but tough and
opaque, kind of paper used for making books of which it is desirable to
reduce the bulk and weight to a minimum without impairing dura-
bility or affecting the legibility of the type. The name was originally
given in England, about the middle of the 18th century, to a soft absorbent
paper imported from China, probably by way of India.
How India Paper First Came From the Far East.
The name of India paper is now chiefly associated with European (es-
pecially British) machine-made, thin, opaque printing papers used in the
highest class of book-printing. In 1841 an Oxford graduate brought to
England from the Far East a small quantity of extremely thin paper, which
was manifestly tougher and more opaque, for its weight, than any paper then
made in Europe. He presented it to the Oxford University Press, which, in
1842, used it for 24 copies of the smallest Bible then in existence.
Re-invented in England.
In 1874 a copy of the India paper Bible fell into the hands of Henry
Frowde, and experiments were instituted at the Oxford University paper-
mills at Wolvercote with the object of producing similar paper. In 1875 an
impression of the Bible, similar in all respects to that of 1842, was placed on
sale by the Oxford University Press. This feat of compression was regarded
as astounding, the demand was enormous, and since then hundreds of thou-
sands of these India paper Bibles have been printed.
Tests at the Paris Exposition.
The marvels of compression achieved by the use of India paper created
great interest at the Paris Exposition in 1900. Its strength was as remark-
able as its lightness. Volumes of 1500 pages were suspended for several
months by a single leaf, as thin as tissue, and when they were examined
at the close of the Exposition it was found that the leaf had not started,
the paper had not stretched, and the volume closed as well as ever.
The paper, when subjected to severe rubbing, instead of breaking into
holes, like ordinary printing paper, assumed a texture like that of chamois
leather, and a strip 3 inches wide was found to support a weight of 281 b.
without yielding.
Adoption by the Encyclopaedia Britannica.
The adoption of India paper (with flexible bindings) for the
New Encyclopaedia Britannica marked a veritable revolution in
the making of large books. The public of the English-speak-
ing world has been quick to see the advantages of the new
format. Eighty-nine per cent, of the 35,000 orders so far
received for the new Ilth Edition have been for sets on
India paper, and subscribers have shown the greatest en-
thusiasm over the light, thin, beautiful volumes of the India
paper edition. These volumes, though they each contain about
1000 pages, are only 1 inch thick. The whole set occupies only
30 inches of shelf space, whereas the edition on ordinary book-
paper requires nearly secen feet.
Far-Reaching Effect of the Innovation.
It is the confident belief of the Syndics of the Press of the
University of Cambridge that the new Britannica, in the light,
flexible, easily-handled India paper format, will not only be re-
ferred to more often than was its cumbrous predecessor, but
that it will henceforth be read and studied with far greater
ease and comfort, and, therefore, much more profitably.

Another Australian Record
on the “Alcock” Table.
TO the remarkable series of record breaks by Gray, Roberts, Williams and
Lindrum, already performed on the “Alcock” Table, is added another record
by Lindrum. His latest achievement was in the Melbourne match with
Reece, when hesecured the phenomenal break of 1239—1230 coming off the red,
establishing two Australian records. All these champions have expressed their
high opinion of Alcock Tables, for huge breaks are only possible where the table
is “ true to angle,” with cushions, jaws and every other part absolutely perfect.
ti Gray and Lindrum owe their present reputations largely to the
“Alcock” Table. It is on these tables they practised and made many of their
largest breaks. We will gladly post you our illustrated Billiard Catalogue.

Says Cheery Cholly: Sure fretting is folly,

Repeating Rifles
BEST FOR AUS-
TRALIAN GAME
Some rifle makers are always
talking about the strength of
their product. You want
strength, yes, but accuracy and
dependability are the prime
requisites of a rifle. The espec-
ially strong points about Win-
chester rifles are their reliability and accurate shooting
qualities, but in attaining these, strength and wear-
ability have not been lost sight of. There are thousands
of Winchester rifles giving satisfactory service today
that were in use twenty years ago. Winchester rifles
are made in calibers for all kinds of shooting. The
Model 1892, made in .25-20,
.32, .38 and .44 calibers, is
widely used for " Australian
game. These rifles are not
expensive, although they
are made to get game
and stand hard usage.

THROW AWAY YODR PIPE
AND
SMOKE MY CIGARS
■
-
I’VE knocked around a bit over Australasia in
my time —been in the back parts of pretty
well every State and New Zealand.
I've mixed with shearers and musterers and
rouseabouts at different sheds; been at cattle
camps in Queensland; up among the snow
country of Monaro- in the jarrah forests of
Westralia; in mining camps all over the Com-
monwealth; and everywhere Ive seen the boys
smoking their pipes—and seldom a sign of a
Cigar,
Then, I’ve pulled out my case and handed
some DIADEMAS around. And after all this,
people say to me—“ Oh, Australians won’t smoke
Cigars!”
wmT^P/,
?
iiHl
mm
I do just get riled when I
hear this, I tell you that
nearly every pipe-smoker,
once he gets the taste of my
DIADEMAS Cigars, swears
by them. They knock pipe-
smoking hollow!
There’s nothing but To-
bacco in them —and the best
Cigar Tobacco that is grown
—the wrapper, finest Java
leaf; and the inside filling,
all clean, straight,long Brazil.
They draw easily and burn
freely, for they are hand-
rolled by as good Cigar-
makers as there are any-
where in the world.
The boys out-back I’ve
given them to, as I said, just
swear by my Cigars, Men
in the Cigar trade here, too
—men with whom Cigar-
smoking is a life habit —come
to me and say—“Lookhere,
those Cigars of yours are
better than any we can get
in the city. Let us have a
box as a favor.”
Now, I want you to try
my Cigars —at my ex-
pense.
I want to convert you from
the pipe to Cigars—to my
Cigars. All I want isfor you
to buy my Cigars as against
your pipe and favorite to-
bacco—and I know you’ll be
a regular customer of mine.
I’ll post a box of 100 DIADEMAS right to
your address for 22/6. Smoke 5 or 6of them,
and if they’re not as good as the best 6d. Cigar
you can buy send the rest of the Cigars back,
and I’ll return every penny of your money. I’ll
make no deduction for the Cigars you’ve smoked
There's no funny business about this offer,
mind. I’ll keep to every word of it, and there’ll
be no “back answers” if you want your money
back. If you want to know the kind of man
you’re dealing with, write to the General
Manager, the Commercial Bank, Melbourne,
and he’ll tell you I’m a clean, straight chap in all
my dealings,
Send 22/6 to-day, and get the box of 100
DIADEMAS by return post. In ordering, say
whether you like the mild, medium, or strong
grade.
My Only Address is
LOUIS KAUFFMANN

Dyspepsia
Tortured by Wind Round the Heart—Skin Became
Sallow—Awful Complications Made Life a Burden.
Bile Beans are Invaluable in Mature Age
FOR all complications arising from
stomach and liver trouble, Bile
Beans are a sure and effective
remedy. Mr. H. Bannister, of Potts
Street, East Brisbane, Q., says:—
“Severe attacks of dyspepsia and acute bilious
bouts, followed by terrible pains in the head,
made my life a misery. My whole system would
be shaken and strained by fits of retching, liile
accumulated until it overflowed and caused me
much unpleasantness. My skin assumed an un-
natural yellow tint, and I always felt drowsy,
especially so after meals. Wind around the
heart, and pains in the chest and shoulders, all
added to my suffering, and, while these bouts
were on, my condition was very bad. After try-
ing a number of medicines, from which no good
resulted, I was persuaded to commence a course
of Bile Beans. I did so, and soon noticed that
the headaches and pains quickly disappeared.
After each dose I felt a corresponding benefit,
until, after' a thorough course of Bile Beans, I
was once more enjoying perfect health, and I
have never been troubled with sickness since.
Although I have led a very strenuous life, now,
at 62 years of age, I feel in splendid condition.
“My wife, when at all out of sorts, takes a dose or two of Bile Beans, and
they soon put her right. She has also found them splendid for headaches.
Every worker should always keep a box of Bile Beans near at hand. A dose or
two. on the slightest sign of any stomach trouble, will speedily put things right.

Matchless for the Complexion.
The Roly and the Burr.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
roly-poly is no common, inani-
mate thing. In its early stages,
when it is green and growing, it is a
peaceful, sociable sort of plant. Considered
in comparison with its frequent associate,
the Bathurst burr, which is a hostile, sel-
fish vegetable, it may be regarded as almost
friendly. It hurts nobody who stumbles
into it; the only objection is that it is a
useless land-grabber, whose address can’t be
depended on. All the same, were it content
to die and rot away on the spot, when its
season is over, no one would make any
great fuss about it.
The Bathurst burr is always hardy and
vigorous, for only its own kind can restrict
its room and stint its growth. It grows
anywhere, seeds in great profusion, and is
solely and wholly concerned in making it-
self a nuisance to all and sundry. The
seeds are thickly covered with little spines,
which cling to anything that touches them.
They attach themselves to the clothes of the
unwary, to the fur of the rabbit and mar-
supial, to the hair of the dog, and to the
tail of the horse or the bovine—all excellent
vehicles whereby to reach new country and
become a greater nuisance. On the sheep’s
coat and the traveller’s blanket they cluster
in matted heaps, provoking bad language
among a host of people. Some plant seeds,
as thistle-down, are fashioned to be borne
abroad by the wind ; others, as prickly pear
and inkweed, are fitted to be carried far and
wide by birds; but the burr has more ways
of getting around and discovering salubrious
climates than any other plant in the land.
Cats’-eggs and burr seeds are the pet
agents of the school-boy. Of the two he pre-
fers the latter. If he succeeds in dropping
i few down his companion’s neck, or rolling
some into his hair, he will go home in high
dee, and have pleasant dreams. Those
seeds ball up so in your cranial thatch that
the least painful way to effect a separation
is to get your hair cut.
If, by mischance, you stumble into the
aush, the damage you will do to it
will be nothing compared to the damage
Fou wall sustain. Whilst it will recover
piickly from the assault and battery, you
will be picking out prickles, nursing wounds,
ind making stringent remarks about the ob-
loxious thing for a fortnight. Still, it can
ie avoided with ordinary precautions, and
it doesn’t set up to be anything but a burr.
But the roly-poly pursues you across the
iandscape, bumping against you in unex-
pected places, in all manner of nooks and
corners. Other weed pests, when they die,
have the decency to remain dead; but the
roly gets up and walks. Breaking away
from its root, it gathers up the dry blades
if grass and herbage it rolls upon, till it be-
comes a big white ball. Then it sets out
to see the country, and to sow the remainder
of its seeds over hill and dale. Hundreds of
miles it may roll before dropping finally into
an excavated tank, or lodging against a
fence, where it helps to form an incline for
others to travel over.
It is a humorous curse. You hear a
Westerner allude to it as the “Devil’s Own,”
and before long you are brought to think
that the pronouncement is more than a mere
figure of speech. He comes to speak of it
as though it were some reasoning animal, or
as if he believed that a mischievous, impish
spirit, whose main object was to have a high
old time at his expense, had found lodg-
ment therein.
Three people, strongly imbued with this
idea, were Joe Steel, Bob Sharkey and
“Mulga Jack” Blunt. Joe was a boundary-
rider. His hut was near the edge of a
seven-mile plain. On the other side was a
pubbery, where he occasionally spent a Sat-
urday evening. He was riding home one
moonlight night, when a roly, starting sud-
denly from behind a bush, rolled across the
track under his horse’s nose. The half-
dozing animal woke up with a shock that
toppled Joe out of the saddle. For a little
while he sat on the ground; then, instead of
going after his horse, he set sail in pursuit
of the wandering herb. A strong puff of
wind carried it merrily along, springing and
bounding like a thing of life. With loud
whoops and a stream of caustic language,
Joe ran it down and jumped on it with en-
thusiasm. Telling about it afterwards, he
said: ‘‘You could see th’ dashed thing
laughin’ at me as it rolled away.”
“Mulga Jack” was employed as bullock-
driver and rouseabout on the same run. One
of his duties was to run in killing sheep out
of a big paddock. Riding across the same
paddock one day, I found him busily can-
tering about a level stretch over which many
big white rolies were scattered. Into each
one he dropped a lighted match. “Hulloa,
Jack!” I said. “Burning off?” “Ain’t it
time?” he demanded, fiercely. “When a
man comes miles out of his way to muster
th’ damn things, thinkin’ they’re sheep, am t
it time? They’re always foolin’ me; an’
they never let up that they’re nothing but
blasted weeds till you’re right on top of
’em.”
Jack had good cause to be annoyed. -Not
long before, while helping to drive some
sheep home rather late, he had made him-
self the laughing-stock of the station. When
nearing the yard, a roly that had been
kicked from its anchorage started away at
right angles to the mob. Mistaking it in
the dusk for a breaking woolly, Jack set
after it at full gallop, at the same time
shouting to his dog to “fetch him back.
Only when he had headed the object did he
realise what his mates were laughing at.
To Bob Sharkey, the drover, the rolies
were a source of annoyance when watching
sheep on camp at night. In the starlight,
with a stiff breeze blowing, they looked like
jumbucks making off. Consequently, Bob
put in an anxious time on watch, besides
doing a lot of useless riding about. Rolies
trundling on to the camp also disturbed the
mob. When the light was bad and the
rolies numerous, he was often doubtful
which was which. This state of anxiety
was brought about by a misadventure during
his first trip across the infested country.
The sheep had been camped near the junc-
tion of two creeks. Bob had last watch, and,
when morning broke, he found he had been
minding a patch of rolies, while the sheep
had gone back to the Inst fenc«.
The roly is an Australian, and, like most
indigenous plants of the obnoxious order, it
does its mischief in a good-humored way that
makes you laugh and swear by turns. The
stinging tree and the lawyer vine are un-
pleasant things to get mixed up with; but
these keep out of the way in dark scrubs,
and, when your business takes you there, and
they get hold of you, though you perform
a perisher for a minute, you see the “joke
immediately afterwards. Most bushmen are
tolerant in their bearing towards them,
abusing the “damn things” with a smile. But
the burr is detested. Like the prickly pear,
it is a vicious, encumbering horror, with
absolutely no redeeming features. Yet it
has friends. It provides a few hard-up
wanderers with an annual job. Burr-cut-
ting is an industry in some parts, having
its followers the same as cane-cutting, shear-
ing and fruit-picking. These men under-
stand the habits of the prickly nightmare.
They know on what runs, and at what time,
to look for the job. They speak kindly of
the burr. The old warriors leave a stud
plant here and there, so that the species will
not become extinct. It has been a friend
to them, therefore they would be sorry to see
it go. . , ,
One of the profession, who was known as
Jimmy-the-Kat, looked upon certain infested
areas as his special preserves. When put
in a paddock with a hoe, Jimmy was at
home and happy. He had been brought
up on a farm, he used to say; so the love
of agriculture was born in him. lhe nuis-
ance was no sooner showing above the her-
bage, than he was on the scene. One year,
on applying to Squatter McNabb, he was
told that the burrs were not big enough.
“And,” added Mac, “th’ crop is only a
varra sma’ one yet, Jimmy.” Jimmy didn t
like to go away. For one thing, his boots
were done, and he was afraid someone else
might get the job. So he made a camp not
far from the homestead.
On the following afternoon, while riding
through the paddocks, McNabb noticed
Jimmy dodging about with a bucket and
a pannikin. A little puzzled, he rode up.
Jimmy appeared not to notice him. He was
now on his knees, and irito a little trench
encircling a burr he was pouring pannikins
of water. . , .
“What are ye doin’, mon? Mac asked
at last. . , , „
“Makin’ the burrs big enough to cut.
Jimmy didn’t look up. He continued
watering. McNabb took a tighter grip ot
his reins.
“For what?” he roared.
“I want to be gettin’ along, Jimmy ex-
plained, “but I don’t like leavin’ burrs be-
k*"“Wuf— dom ye!” said Mac; “go and get a
lH> lt was a countryman of McNabb’s wb<
introduced Jimmy’s friend to Australia. He
had transplanted himself near Bathurst, and
pining for something he had been used to,
he sent home for some seeds ot Scotch wild
flowers. These were sown in specially-pre-
pared ground, and in due course,
other things, up came the rampant burr.
The introducer was charmed; it looked so
home-like. He brought his brother Scots to
see it; and they, being likewise charmed,
made early requests for seeds. Alack. tin
wretched weed took kindly to the new land,
and everybody got seeds, even people who
weren’t expecting any. I am not sure it the
Scotch thistle emanated from the same
packet; anyhow, it was another gem, the
emblem of home and mother and venerable
memories, that lang; syne brought comfort to
Sandy and Donald.
Though no plant possesses the resources ot
the burr in the matter of dissemination, there
are odd individuals who seem to think that it
doesn’t spread over the country half quickly
enough. One such was Brumby George.
Passing once through Thompson s place,
he came upon a patch of strong; bushes, well
seeded. “By gum,” he said with mixed feel-
ings, “that’s a fine crop—in a bad place.
Hungry Thompson don’t spend nuthin.lqn
burrs —an’ he’s well choked up already.
Now there’s Jobson, of Gidgeebroon, owns
10 times more property than Thompson-! an.
ain’t got a burr!” . , . .
Then he dropped his bundle, and donning
a pair of leather gloves, set patiently to work
to collect the atrocious seeds.
“He won’t pay me for th trouble, Hungry
Thompson won’t,” he remarked as he trans-
ferred them to a canvas bag; an yet bell
be choked clean out in another five years it
all th’ seeds is allowed to grow ’ere. 1 don t
owe him nuthin’, but I don’t like to see good
lanci bein’ ruined an’ not a hand stretched
out to save it.”
He picked and picked, till his bag was halt
full musing as he shook it down that manj
a good action goes unrecorded and unre-
Wa A d day or so later he presented himself at
Gidgeebroon.
“Good day, Mr. Jobson.
“Good day.”
“I hear you’re buyin’ seeds.
“What seeds?”
“Burr seeds—Bathurst burr.
“Buying them !” cried Jobson. yv hat the
h— would Ibe buying the d — infliction
f ° “To prevent ’em growin’ an’ spreadin’, I
s’pose,” said Brumby. “They’re terrible
things to travel if they’re not gathered up. ;
“There’s none to gather up on this run,
Jobson declared. “None whatever. .
“Well,” said Brumby, in a disappointed,
injured sort of way, “I was told by a couple
of chaps I met that it was your run where
they was growin’, an’ that Id get a weeks
rations for collectin’ th’ seeds. If Ive been
misinformed, I s’pose I’ll ave to throw em
away.” Brumby w r eighed the bag in his
hand, looking at it regretfully. I went to a
lot of trouble gatherin’ ’em, too, he con-
“ Let me see them.” Jobson reached out
eagerly for the bag. , , ...
“What’s th’ price?” asked Brumby, hold-
ing it behind him.
For a moment Jobson was speechless, rie
understood at once what sort of person
Brumby was; and he was terribly anxious
lest one of those awful seeds should get
astray. . . .. ,
Then he said quietly, while his eyes blazed,
and his face assumed an apoplectic hue,
“Fetch them into the store.”
On entering the grocery establishment his
temper left him. “Here’s the bins, he said.
“Help yourself while I burn these things.
He went out with the seeds, pulling the door
to. Three minutes later the bookkeeper and
the groom, who had been sent to empty a
bag of flour, found Brumby helping himsell
most liberally. 5 .
He was explaining to them when Jobson
returned, after which explanations and ex-
postulations were equally wasted. Jobson
had forgotten all about the deal.
Subsequently Brumby was arrested tor
burglary, and got six months.
EDWARD S. SORENSON.


DO YOU FEEL LIKE GIVING UP?
Does your back ache constantly ? Do you feel dull, miserable
and tired all the time ? Do you wonder why ordinary daily duties
wear you out ? Do you have “ blue ” days and restless nights ?
Kidney sickness silently plays out the strongest person. A
dull, draggy ache in the small of the back will tell on the healthiest
man or woman. And if the kidneys fail in their task of filtering
uric acid poisons from the blood, it leads to painful attacks of
neuralgia, rheumatism, sciatica, gravel, headaches—to worrying
attacks of dizziness, nervousness, heart palpitation, scanty,
painful and too frequent urination.
Don t neglect weak kidneys. There is danger of running
gradually into dropsy, Bright’s Disease or diabetes. At the first sign
of backache or disorders of the urine, use Doan’s Backache Kidney
Pills. A remedy that has cured your neighbors will cure you.
Proved by SYDNEY Testimony*
Mrs. M. Portors, Wellington Street, North Botany, Sydney, says:—“l had marked symptoms of
kidney trouble for two years. I had dull, tired feelings, headaches, and such severe backache that I
could scarcely get about. The secretions were seriously affected, being thick and cloudy. The pains
m my back were terrible; they sometimes went up between my shoulders, and nobody knows the
agony I suffered. But my worst suffering was with dropsy. At times I would not be able to
leave my bed, and once I was laid up for three months. I almost despaired of ever getting well
again, as I had used so many remedies without getting any relief. When a friend advised me to use
Doan’s Backache Kidney Pills I did not have much hope that they would cure me; but got a bottle
just to please her. After taking the one bottle my back felt a little easier, and I continued using
them. Gradually the headaches and backaches left me, and the swelling in my legs went down The
secretions became clear and regular, and I lost the tired-out, languid feeling. Doan’s Backache
Kidney Pills have restored me to health, and I never thought I would be well again My cure did
not happen yesterday, but five years ago, and, as I have been quite well all that time, can safely
say that I am cured for good.”

Jones’s Selection; a Kiandra
Man’s Yarn.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
YOU hear a lot of new-chum talk
Of “goin’ on the land” —
Of growin’ oats an’ wheat, an’ sich,
On rocks an’ flamin’ sand.
I ’ates exaggerated skite,
But, if yer likes, I can
Authenticate a case in which
The land went on the man.
Bill Jones ’e ’ad a mountain block
Up Kosciusko way;
He farmed it pretty nigh to death,
The neighbors used to say.
He scarified the surface with
His double-furrow ploughs,
An’ ate its suff’rin heart right out
With sheep an’ milkin’ cows;
He filled its blamed intestines up
With agricult’ral pipes,
An’ lime, and superphosphates—fit
To give the land the gripes—
Until at length the tortured soil,
YVorn out with Jones’s thrift,
Decided as the time was come
To up an’ make a shift.
One day the mountain shook itself,
An’ give a sort o’ groan ;
The neighbors was a lot more scared
Than they was game to own.
Their jaws were dropped upon their chests,
Their eyes was opened wide;
They saw the whole of Jones’s farm
Upend itself an’ slide.
It slithered down the mountain spur,
Majestic like an’ slow,
An’ landed in the river-bed
A thousand feet below.
Bill Jones was on the lower slopes
Of his long-sufferin’ farm
A-testin’ some new-fangled plough
Which acted like a charm.
He’d just lieen screwin’ np a nut
When somethin’ seemed to crack,
An’ fifty acres, more or less,
Come down on Jones’s back.
’Twas sudden-like, a shake, a crack,
A slitherin’ slide, an’ Bill
Was buried fifty feet below
The soil he used to till.
One moment Bill was standin’ up
A-ownin’ all that land,
The next ’e’s in Eternity—
A spanner in ’is ’and !
They never dug up no remains
Nor scraps of William Jones;
The superphosphates eat the lot —
Hide, buttons, boots and bones.
For this hero land wot Jones abused
And harassed in the past
’Ad turned an’ wiped ’im out, so things
Got levelled up at last.
From this untimely end o’ Bill
It would perhaps appear
'That goin’ free-selectin’ ain’t
All skittles, no, nor beer.
So all you cocky city coves
Wot’s savin’ up yer screw
'To get upon the land, look out
The land don’t get on you.
JM.S.W.
IRONBARK.
Commerce.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
WE were walking in Flinders-lane—
sacred to the men of rag's. My
friend the Austrian refused to
consider seriously these Merchant Princes or

Hess than epic. According to the Austrian
there is nothing w>orth thought but Art, and
when I remarked that Trade means not so
much Intelligence as Cunning, he said that I
Ibut begged the question. Further cross-
examined, he admitted one foolish and un-
successful excursion into Trade, and re-
marked that the result was justly expect-
able. He regarded it as the punishment that
belongs to the artist who dips into sordid
things.
“When was it?” I asked, and he replied,
"“In eighteen-ninety-free.”
